perpendicular to the first, and a rotation through an angle $\pi$
about a line perpendicular to both planes.

\mysect{Congruent transformations of euclidean space}
To pass to the limiting euclidean case
\[
\begin{aligned}
  x' &= A_0 + A_1 x + A_2 y + A_3 z,  \\
  y' &= B_0 + B_1 x + B_2 y + B_3 z,  \\
  z' &= C_0 + C_1 x + C_2 y + C_3 z,
\end{aligned}
\tag{9}
\]
where $\lVert A_1 B_2 C_3 \rVert$ is the matrix of a ternary orthogonal substitution.

There will be a three-parameter invariant sub-group; that
of all translations
\begin{align*}
  x' &= A_0 + x, \\
  y' &= B_0 + y, \\
  z' &= C_0 + z.
\end{align*}

In like manner we may find the six-parameter assemblage
of symmetry transformations.
\index{Congruent transformations|)}
\index{Transformations, congruent|)}

%[File: 102.png]
%CHAPTER IX
\mychap{IX}{POINT, LINE, AND PLANE TREATED ANALYTICALLY}
\mysect{Notable points of a triangle in the non-euclidean plane}

The object of the present chapter is to return, as promised
in Chapter VI, to the problems of elementary non-euclidean
geometry, from the higher point of view gained by extending
space to be a perfect analytic continuum. We shall find in
the Absolute a \emph{Deus ex Machina} to relieve us from many an
embarrassment. We shall leave aside the euclidean case,
and, for the most part, handle all of our non-euclidean cases
together, leaving to the reader the simple task of making
the distinction between the elliptic and the spherical cases.
Otherwise stated, our present task is to express the fundamental
metrical theorems of point, line, and plane, in terms
of the invariants of the congruent group.

Let us notice, at the outset, that the principle of duality
plays a fundamental rôle. The distance of two points is
$\dfrac{k}{2i} \times$~logarithm of the cross ratio that they form with the
points where their line meets the Absolute, the angle of two
planes is $\dfrac{1}{2i} \times$~logarithm of the cross ratio which they form
with two planes through their intersection, tangent to the
Absolute; the distance from a point to a plane is $\dfrac{\pi k}{2}$ minus its
distance to the pole of that plane with regard to the Absolute.
Two intersecting lines or planes which are conjugate with
regard to the Absolute are mutually perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}. Two
points which are conjugate with regard to the Absolute shall
be said to be mutually \emph{orthogonal}\index{Orthogonal points}. In the real domain of
hyperbolic space, if one of two such points be actual, the other
must be ideal; the converse is not necessarily true.

Let us begin in the non-euclidean plane, say $x_3 = 0$. Let
us take two points $A$, $B$ with coordinates $(x)$ and $(y)$ respectively,
and find the two points of their line which are at
%[File: 103.png]
congruent distances from them. These shall be called the
\emph{centres of gravity}\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points}
of the two points, and are, in fact, the two
points which divide harmonically the given points, and the
intersections of their line with the Absolute\index{Absolute}. We purposely
exclude the spherical case, where the centres of gravity will
be equivalent points.

The necessary and sufficient condition that the point
$\lambda(x) + \mu(y)$ should be at congruent distances from $(x)$ and
$(y)$ that
\[
  \lambda : \mu = \sqrt{(yy)} : \pm \sqrt{(xx)}.
\]
The coordinates of the centres of gravity will thus be
\[
  \Bigl( \frac{x}{\sqrt{(xx)}} \pm \frac{y}{\sqrt{(yy)}} \Bigr). \tag{1}
\]

Let the reader discover what complications may arise in the
ideal domain.

Let us next take three non-collinear points $A$, $B$, $C$ with
the coordinates $(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$. A line connecting $(x)$ with a
centre of gravity of $(y)$ and $(z)$ will be
\[
  \sqrt{(yy)}\,|Xxz| + \sqrt{(zz)}\,|Xxy| = 0.
\]
It is clear that such lines are concurrent\index{Concurrence} by threes, in four
points which may be called the \emph{centres of gravity} of the three
given points. On the other hand the centres of gravity of
our pairs of points are collinear\index{Collinearity} in threes. Lastly, notice that
a dual theorem might be reached by interchanging the objects,
point and line, distance and angle; by taking, in fact, a polar
reciprocation in the Absolute:---

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~1. The centres of
gravity of the pairs formed
from three given points are
collinear by threes on four
lines. The lines from the
given points to the centres
of gravity of their pairs are
concurrent by threes in four
points.}
{\emph{Theorem}~1$'$. The bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of
the angles formed by three
coplanar but not concurrent
lines are concurrent by threes
in four points. The points
where these bisectors meet
the given lines are collinear
by threes on four lines.}

The centres of gravity of the points $(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$ are easily
seen to be
\[
  \Bigl( \frac{x}{\sqrt{(xx)}}
     \pm \frac{y}{\sqrt{(yy)}}
     \pm \frac{z}{\sqrt{(zz)}} \Bigr).  \tag{2}
\]

Returning to the line $BC$ we see that the coordinates of its
%[File: 104.png]
pole with regard to the Absolute\index{Absolute} will have the coordinates $(s)$,
where for every value of $(r)$
\[
(rs) \equiv |ryz|.
\]
The equation of the line connecting this point with $A$, i.e.\ the
line through $A$ perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to $BC$, will be
\[
(Xy)(zx)-(Xz)(xy) = 0.
\]
If we permute the letters $x$, $y$, $z$ cyclically twice, we get two
other equations of the same type, and the sum of the three
is identically zero, so that

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~2. The lines
through each of three given
non-collinear points, perpendicular
to the line of the other
two, are concurrent\index{Concurrence}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~2$'$. The points on
each of three coplanar but not
concurrent lines, orthogonal
to the intersection of the other
two, are collinear\index{Collinearity}.}

Returning to a centre of gravity of the two points $BC$, we
see that a line through it perpendicular to the line $BC$ will
have the equation

\[
\begin{vmatrix}
  (xy) &  (xz)   \\
  \dfrac{(yy)}{\sqrt{(yy)}} + \dfrac{(yz)}{\sqrt{(zz)}}
& \dfrac{(yz)}{\sqrt{(yy)}} + \dfrac{(zz)}{\sqrt{(zz)}}
\end{vmatrix} = 0,
\]
\[
  \Bigl[ \frac{(yz)}{\sqrt{(yy)}\; \sqrt{(zz)}} - 1 \Bigr]
  \Bigl[ \frac{(xy)}{\sqrt{(yy)}} - \frac{(xz)}{\sqrt{(zz)}} \Bigr]
  = 0.
\]

The first factor will vanish (in the real domain) only when
$(y)$ and $(z)$ are identical, the equation will then be
\[
\frac{(xy)}{\sqrt{(yy)}} - \frac{(xz)}{\sqrt{(zz)}} = 0.
\]

We see immediately from the form of this equation, that
all points of this line are at congruent distances from $(y)$ and
$(z)$, thus confirming II.~33.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~3. If three non-collinear
points be given, the
perpendiculars to the lines of
their pairs at the centres of
gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of these pairs are
concurrent by threes in four
points, each at congruent distances
from all three of the
given points.}
{\emph{Theorem}~3$'$. If three coplanar
but not concurrent
lines be given, the points
orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points} to their intersections
on the bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of the
corresponding angles are collinear
by threes on four lines,
making congruent angles with
all three of the given lines.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let us now suppose that besides our three original points,
%[File: 105.png]
we have three others lying one on each of the lines of the
first set as follows
\begin{align*}
A' & = (ly + mz), \\
B' & = (pz + qx), \\
C' & = (rx + sy).
\end{align*}

\mysect{Analoga of the theorems of Menelaus and Ceva}
Let us, for the moment, suppose that we are restricted to
a consistent region of the plane. Then we shall easily see
from Axiom XVI that if $AA'$, $BB'$, $CC'$ be concurrent
\[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BA'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CA'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CB'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AB'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AC'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BC'}}{k} } < 0.
\]
On the other hand, if $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ be collinear\index{Collinearity},
\[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BA'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CA'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CB'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AB'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AC'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BC'}}{k} } > 0.
\]

Now, more specifically, we see that
\[
\sin^2\frac{\overline{BA'}}{k} =
  \frac{m^2\left[ (yy)(zz) - (yz)^2 \right]}
       {(yy)\left[ l^2(yy) + 2lm(yz) + m^2(zz) \right]},
\]
whence
\[
  \left[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BA'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CA'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{CB'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AB'}}{k} }
\cdot
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{AC'}}{k} }
       {\sin\dfrac{\overrightarrow{BC'}}{k} }
  \right]
= \left( \frac{mqs}{lpr} \right)^2.
\]
The equation of the line $AA'$ will be
\[
l\lvert Xxy \rvert + m\lvert Xzx \rvert = 0.
\]
And the condition for concurrence for the three lines
\[
(lpr + mqs) \cdot \lvert xyz \rvert^2 = 0,
\]
and this will give
\[
\frac{mqs}{lpr} = -1.
\]
On the other hand, we easily see that if $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ be collinear
\[
lpr-mqs = 0.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~4. If $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ be three points lying respectively
%[File: 106.png]
on the lines $BC$, $CA$, $AB$, all six points being in a consistent
region, then the expression
\begin{equation*}
\frac{\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{BA'}}{k} }
     {\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{CA'}}{k} }
\cdot
\frac{\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{CB'}}{k} }
     {\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{AB'}}{k} }
\cdot
\frac{\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{BC'}}{k} }
     {\sin \dfrac{\overrightarrow{AC'}}{k} },
\end{equation*}
will be equal to $-1$ when, and only when, $AA'$, $BB'$, $CC'$
are concurrent, while it will be equal to 1, when, and only
when, $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ are collinear.

These are, of course, merely the analogs of the theorems
of Menelaus\index{Menelaus} and Ceva\index{Ceva}. It is worth noticing also, that they
will afford a sufficient ground for a metrical theory of cross
ratios.

Let us next suppose that $A'$ is a point where a bisector
of an angle formed by the lines $BA$, $CA$, meets $BC$. We
find $l$ and $m$ easily in this case, by noticing that $A'$ must be
at congruent distances from $AB$ and $AC$, thus getting
\begin{equation*}
(y\sqrt{(zz)(xx)-(xz)^2}+z\sqrt{(xx)(yy)-(xy)^2}),
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\sin \frac{\overline{BA'}}{k}:\sin \frac{\overline{CA'}}{k}
=\sin \frac{\overline{BA}}{k}:\sin \frac{\overline{CA}}{k}.
\end{equation*}

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~5. If three noncollinear\index{Collinearity}
points be given, each
bisector of an angle formed by
the lines connecting two of
the points with the third will
meet the line of the two points
in such a point that the ratio
of the sines of the $k$th parts
of its distances from the two
points is equal to the corresponding
ratio for these two
with the third point.}
{\emph{Theorem}~5$'$. If three coplanar
but non-concurrent\index{Concurrence}
lines be given, each centre of
gravity of a pair of points
where two of the lines meet
a third determines with the
intersection of this pair of
lines such a line, that the ratio
of the sines of the angles which
it makes with these two lines,
is equal to the corresponding
ratio for the two lines with
the third.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~6. The locus of
a point which moves in a plane,
in such a way that the ratio
of the sines of the $k$th parts
of its distances from two points
is constant, is a curve of the
second order.}
{\emph{Theorem}~6$'$. The envelope of
a line which moves in such a
way in a plane, that the ratio
of the sines of its angles with
two fixed lines is constant, is
an envelope of the second class.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

%[File: 107.png]
It would be quite erroneous to suppose that either of these
curves would be, in general, a circle. Let the reader show
that if an angle inscribed in a semicircle be a right angle, the
euclidean hypothesis holds.

\mysect{Formulae of the parallel angle}
Our next investigation shall be connected with parallel\index{Parallelism}
lines. We suppose, for the moment, that we are in the
hyperbolic plane, and that $k=i$. We shall hunt for the
expression for the angle\index{Angle11@--- parallel}\index{Parallel angle}
which a parallel to a given line $l$
passing through a point $P$ makes with the perpendicular
to $l$ through $P$. This shall be called the \emph{parallel angle} of
the distance from the point to the line, and if the latter be $d$
the parallel angle shall be written%
\footnote{The concept \emph{parallel angle} and the notation $\Pi(d)$ are due to Lobatchewsky\index{Lobatchewsky}. }
%endfootnote
\begin{equation*}
\Pi(d).
\end{equation*}

Let us give to the point $P$ the coordinates $(y)$, while the
given line has the coordinates $(u)$. Let $(v)$ be the coordinates
of a parallel to $(u)$ through $(y)$. Let $D$ be the point where
the perpendicular to $(u)$ through $(y)$ meets $(u)$. We seek
$\cos \Pi(d)$.

Since $(u)$ and $(v)$ intersect on the Absolute\index{Absolute}
\begin{equation*}
(uu)(vv)-(uv)^2 = 0.
\end{equation*}
The equation of the line $PD$ will be
\begin{equation*}
|xyu|=0.
\end{equation*}
The cosine of the angle formed by $u$ and $PD$ will be
\begin{equation*}
\cos \Pi(d)=\frac{|yuv|}{\sqrt{\mystrut{6}vv}\;\sqrt{(uu)(yy)-(yu)^2}}
\end{equation*}
squaring, and remembering that
\begin{equation*}
(vy) = 0,
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\cos^2 \Pi(d)=\frac{\left| \begin{array}{ccc}
(yy) & (uy) & 0 \\
(uy) & (uu) & (uv) \\
0 & (uv) & (vv)
\end{array} \right| }
{(vv)[(uu)(yy)-(uy)^2]},
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\cos \Pi(d)=\frac{i(uy)}{\sqrt{(uu)(yy)-(uy)^2}},
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\cos \Pi(d) = \tanh d. \tag{3}
\end{equation*}
From these we easily see
\begin{equation*}
\sin \Pi(d) = \sech (d); \quad \tan \Pi(d) = \csch (d). \tag{4}
\end{equation*}
Furthermore, if $\measuredangle ACB$ be a right angle
\begin{equation*}
\cos \measuredangle ABC=\frac{\cos \Pi(\overline{BC})}{\cos \Pi(\overline{AB})}\quad
\sin \measuredangle ABC=\frac{\ctn \Pi(\overline{CA})}{\ctn \Pi(\overline{AB})}. \tag{5}
\end{equation*}

%[File: 108.png]
\begin{equation*}
\cos\measuredangle ABC =
  \frac{\sin\measuredangle CAB}{\sin\Pi (AC)}.          \tag{6}
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\sin \Pi (\overline{AB}) =
  \sin \Pi (\overline{BC})\sin\Pi(\overline{CA}) =
  \tan\measuredangle CAB\tan\measuredangle ABC.       \tag{7}
\end{equation*}\index{Angle11@--- parallel}\index{Parallel angle}

\mysect{Equations of parallels to a given line}
Let the reader prove the correctness of the following construction
for the parallels to $P$ through $l$:

Drop a perpendicular from $P$ on $l$ meeting it in $Q$. Take $S$
a convenient point on the perpendicular to $PQ$ at $P$, and let
the perpendicular to $PS$ at $S$ meet $l$ at $R$. Then with $P$ as
a centre, and a radius equal to $(QR)$, construct an arc meeting
$RS$ in $T$. $PT$ will be the parallel required.%
\footnote{The formulae given may be used as the basis for the whole trigonometric
structure. Cf.\ Manning\index{Manning}, \emph{Non-euclidean Geometry}, Boston, 1901. Manning's
reasoning is open to very grave question on the score of rigour. }
%endfootnote

Be it noticed that, as we should expect,
\[
\limit_{d \doteq 0}\frac{\cos \Pi (d)}{d} = 1.
\]

Let us now find the equations of the two parallels to the
line $(u)$ which pass through the point $(y)$. These two cannot,
naturally, be rationally separated one from the other, so that
we shall find the equations of both at once. Let the coordinates
of the line which connects the other intersections of the parallels
and the Absolute\index{Absolute} be $(w)$. The general form for an equation
of a curve of the second order through the intersections of
$(u)$ and $(w)$ with the Absolute will be
\[
  l(ux)(wx) - m(xx) - 0,
\]
and this will pass through $(y)$ if
\[
  l : m = (yy) : (uy)(wy).
\]

Since this curve is a pair of lines meeting in $(y)$ the polar
of $(y)$ with regard to it will be illusory, i.e.\ the coefficients of
$(x)$ will vanish in
\[
  (yy)(uy)(wx) + (yy)(wy)(ux) - 2(uy)(wy)(xy) = 0.
\]
This last equation may be written
\[
(uy) \begin{vmatrix}
       (wx) & (wy) \\ (yx) & (yy) \end{vmatrix} +
(wy) \begin{vmatrix}
       (ux) & (uy) \\ (yx) & (yy) \end{vmatrix} = 0.
\]

Now, by the harmonic theory of a quadrangle inscribed in
a curve of the second order, $w$ will pass through the intersection of $(u)$
with the polar of $y$ with regard to the Absolute,
so that we may write
\[
w_i = \lambda u_i + \mu y_i.
\]
Substituting
\[
[2\lambda(uy) + \mu(yy)]
  \begin{vmatrix} (ux) & (uy) \\ (yx) & (yy) \end{vmatrix} = 0.
\]
%[File: 109.png]
The coefficients of $x_0 x_1 x_2$ will vanish if
\[
  \lambda = -(yy),\quad \mu = 2(uy).
\]
Under these circumstances
\begin{align*}
  (wx) &= -(yy)(ux) + 2(uy)(xy),   \\
  (wy) &= (yy)(uy).
\end{align*}
Which leads to the required equation
\[
  (uy)^2(xx) + (ux)^2(yy) - 2(ux)(uy)(xy) = 0.   \tag{8}
\]

To get the euclidean formula, replace $x_0$ by $k_0 x_0$ and divide
by $k$. We get the square of the usual expression
\[
  [(uy)x_0 - (ux)y_0]^2 = 0.   \tag{9}
\]

The principles which we have followed in studying the
metrical invariants of the plane may be extended with ease
to three dimensions. We have merely to adjoin the fourth
homogeneous point or line coordinate.

\mysect{Notable points of a tetrahedron, and resulting desmic configurations}
Let us have four points, not in one plane, with the coordinates $(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$, $(t)$ respectively. We easily see that the
eight points
\[
  \Bigl( \frac{x}{\sqrt{(xx)}}
     \pm \frac{y}{\sqrt{(yy)}}
     \pm \frac{z}{\sqrt{(zz)}}
     \pm \frac{t}{\sqrt{(tt)}} \Bigr),  \tag{10}
\]
will be points of concurrence, four by four, of lines from each
of the given points to the centres of gravity of the other three.
These eight may, in fact, be called the \emph{centres of gravity} of the
four points. The centres of gravity will form with the given
points a \emph{desmic configuration}\index{Desmic configuration}.%
\footnote{The desmic configuration was first studied by Stephanos\index{Stephanos}, `Sur la configuration
desmique de trois t\'etraèdres,' \emph{Bulletin des Sciences math\'ematiques},
s\'erie 2, vol.\ iii, 1878.
}
%endfootnote
The meaning of this phrase
is as follows. Let us indicate the centres of gravity by the
signs prefixed to their radicals, giving always to the first
radical a positive sign. We may then divide our twelve
points into three lots as follows:---
\[
\begin{array}{cccc}
  (x)   &  (y)   &  (z)   &  (t)   \\
 (++++) & (++--) & (+-+-) & (+--+) \\
 (+++-) & (++-+) & (+-++) & (+---)
\end{array}
\tag{11}
\]

We see that a line connecting a point of one lot, with any
point of a second, will pass through a point of the third. The
twelve points will thus lie by threes on sixteen lines, four
%[File: 110.png]
passing through each. In like manner we shall find that if
we take the twelve planes obtained by omitting in turn one
point of each lot, two planes\index{Plane} of different lots are always coaxal
with one of the third. Let the reader who is unfamiliar with
the desmic configuration\index{Desmic configuration}, study the particular case (in euclidean
space) of the vertices of a cube, its centre, and the ideal points
of concurrence of its parallel edges.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~7. If four non-coplanar\index{Coplanarity}
points be given, the
lines from each to the four
centres of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of the other
three will pass by fours
through eight points which
form, with the original ones,
a desmic configuration.}
{\emph{Theorem}~7$'$. If four non-concurrent
planes be given,
the lines where each meets
the planes which severally are
coaxal with each of the three
remaining planes and a plane
bisecting\index{Bisector of an angle} a dihedral angle of
the two still left, lie by fours
in eight planes which, with
the original ones, form a
desmic configuration.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let the reader show that the centres of gravity of the six
pairs formed from the given points will determine a second
desmic configuration, and dually for the planes bisecting the
dihedral angles.

Let us seek for a point which is at congruent distances
from our four given points. It is easy to see that there cannot
be more than eight such points. Their coordinates are found
to be $(s)$ where, for all values of $r$,
\[
(rs) \equiv \sqrt{(xx)}\, \lvert ryzt \rvert
        \pm \sqrt{(yy)}\, \lvert rztx \rvert
        \pm \sqrt{(zz)}\, \lvert rtxy \rvert
        \pm \sqrt{(tt)}\, \lvert rxyz \rvert.
\tag{12}
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~8. If four non-coplanar
points be given, the
eight points which are severally
at congruent distances
from them form, with the
original four, a desmic configuration.}
{\emph{Theorem}~8$'$. If four non-concurrent
planes be given,
the eight planes which severally
meet them in congruent
dihedral angles, form, with the
original four, a desmic configuration.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

As there are eight points at congruent distances from the
four given points, so there will be eight planes at congruent
distances from them, we have but to take the polars of the
eight points with regard to the Absolute\index{Absolute}. In like manner,
if we consider not the points $(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$, $(t)$ but their four
%[File: 111.png]
planes\index{Plane}, there will be eight points at congruent distances from
them. The coordinates of these latter eight will be
\begin{multline*}
\left|x\sqrt{\left\| \begin{array} {cccc}
y_0 & y_1 & y_2 & y_3 \\
z_0 & z_1 & z_2 & z_3 \\
t_0 & t_1 & t_2 & t_3 \end{array}\right\|^2} \pm
y \sqrt{\left\| \begin{array} {cccc}
z_0 & z_1 & z_2 & z_3 \\
t_0 & t_1 & t_2 & t_3 \\
x_0 & x_1 & x_2 & x_3 \end{array}\right\|^2}  \right. \\ \pm
\left. z \sqrt{\left\| \begin{array} {cccc}
t_0 & t_1 & t_2 & t_3 \\
x_0 & x_1 & x_2 & x_3 \\
y_0 & y_1 & y_2 & y_3 \end{array}\right\|^2} \pm
t \sqrt{\left\| \begin{array} {cccc}
x_0 & x_1 & x_2 & x_3 \\
y_0 & y_1 & y_2 & y_3 \\
z_0 & z_1 & z_2 & z_3\end{array}\right\|^2} \right|.
\end{multline*}

\medskip\twincol
{\emph{Theorem}~9. If four non-coplanar
points be given, the
eight points which, severally,
are at congruent distances
from the planes of the first
four, form, with the first four
points, a desmic configuration\index{Desmic configuration}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~9$'$. If four non-concurrent
planes be given, the
eight planes which, severally,
are at congruent distances
from the points of concurrence
of the first four, form,
with the first four planes, a
desmic configuration.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Invariant formulae for distance and angle of skew lines in line coordinates}
The parallel angle of a point with regard to a plane can be
defined as its parallel angle\index{Parallel angle} with regard to any line of the
plane through the foot of the perpendicular. If the distance
from the point to the plane be $x$, we shall have for the parallel
angle
\begin{equation*}
\cos \Pi(x) = k \tan \frac{x}{k}. \tag{13}
\end{equation*}

\emph{Definition}. A line shall be said to be parallel to a plane,
if the point common to the two be on the Absolute. The
cone of parallels to a plane $(u)$ through a point $(y)$ will have
the equation
\begin{equation*}
(uy)^2(xx) + (ux)^2 (yy) - 2(ux) (uy) (xy) = 0. \tag{14}
\end{equation*}

We now pass to certain metrical invariants of non-euclidean
space expressed in line coordinates\index{Coordinates01@Co-ordinates of a line}. We take as coordinates
for the line joining $(x)$ and $(y)$ the usual Plueckerian form
\begin{equation*}
p_{ij}=x_iy_j-x_jy_i.
\end{equation*}
The coordinates of the polar of this line with regard to the
Absolute, the Absolute polar let us say, will be
\begin{equation*}
q_{ij}=p_{kl}.
\end{equation*}
The condition for the intersection of two lines $(p)$ and $(p')$
will be, naturally
\begin{equation*}
(p \mid p') \equiv \smallsum p_{ij} p'_{kl} = 0. \tag{15}
\end{equation*}
%[File: 112.png]
Each will meet the Absolute\index{Absolute} of polar of the other if
\begin{equation*}
\smallsum p_{ij} {p'}_{ij}=0. \tag{16}
\end{equation*}
Notice that $(p \mid p')$ is an invariant under the general group of
collineations, while $\smallsum p_{ij} {p'}_{ij}$ is invariant under the congruent
group only.

We shall mean by the distance of two lines the distance
of their intersections with a third line perpendicular to them
both. It is easy to see that if two lines be not paratactic,
there will be two lines meeting both at right angles, and these
are indistinguishable in the rational domain, that is, in the
general case. If, thus, $d$ be taken to indicate the distance
of two lines, $\sin^2 \dfrac{d}{k}$ will be a root of an irreducible quadratic
equation, whose coefficients are rational invariants under the
congruent group. Let us seek for this equation.

Let one of our lines be $p$ given by the points $(x)$, $(y)$, while
the other is $(p')$ given by $(x')$ and $(y')$. For the sake of
simplifying our calculations we shall make the obviously
legitimate assumptions
\begin{equation*}
(xy) = (xy') = (x'y) = (x'y') = 0.
\end{equation*}
The distances\index{Distance03@--- of skew lines} which we wish to find are
\begin{equation*}
\sin \frac{d_1}{k}=\frac{\sqrt{(xx)(x'x')-(xx')^2}}{\sqrt{(xx)}\;\sqrt{(x'x')}}\quad
\sin \frac{d_2}{k}=\frac{\sqrt{(yy)(y'y')-(yy')^2}}{\sqrt{(yy)}\;\sqrt{(y'y')}}.
\end{equation*}
We have
\begin{equation*}
(xx)(yy)-(xy)^2=\smallsum {p_{ij}}^2,
\end{equation*}
and this will vanish only when $(p)$ is tangent to the Absolute,
a possibility which we now explicitly exclude both for $(p)$
and $(p')$.
\begin{equation*}
(xx) (yy) = \smallsum {p_{ij}}^2, \quad (x'x')(y'y')=\smallsum {{p'}_{ij}}^2,
\end{equation*}
\begin{align*}
(p \mid p')^2 & = |xyx'y'|^2 \\
& = \left| \begin{array}{cccc}
(xx) & 0 & (xx') & 0 \\
0 & (yy) & 0 & (yy') \\
(xx') & 0 & (x'x') & 0 \\
0 & (yy') & 0 & (y'y') \end{array} \right| \\
& = [(xx)(x'x')-(xx')^2][(yy)(y'y')-(yy')^2], \\
\sin^2 \frac{d_1}{k} \sin^2 \frac{d_2}{k} & = \frac{[(xx)(x'x')-(xx')^2]}{(xx)(x'x')} \frac{[(yy)(y'y')-(yy')^2]}{(yy)(y'y')}.
\tag{17}
\\
\sin^2 \frac{d_1}{k}\sin^2 \frac{d_2}{k} & = \frac{(p \mid p')^2}{\smallsum {p_{ij}}^2 \smallsum {{p_{ij}}'}^2}.
\tag{18} \\
%[File: 113.png]
\sin^2 \frac{d_1}{k} \sin^2 \frac{d_2}{k}
& = 1 - \cos^2 \frac{d_1}{k} - \cos^2 \frac{d_2}{k}
  + \frac{(xx')^2 (yy')^2}{\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2}; \\
\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}'
& = \left|
   \begin{array}{cc}
    (xx') & (xy') \\
    (yx') & (yy')
   \end{array}
  \right|
= (xx')(yy'),
\end{align*}
%
\begin{equation*}
\cos^2 \frac{d_1}{k} + \cos^2 \frac{d_2}{k}
= 1 + \frac{(\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2 - (p \mid p')^2}
           {\smallsum p_{ij} \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2},
\end{equation*}
%
\begin{equation*}
\sin^2 \frac{d_1}{k} + \sin^2 \frac{d_2}{k}
= 1 - \frac{(\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2 - (p \mid p')^2}
           {\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2},
\end{equation*}
%
\begin{multline*}
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 \sin^4 \frac{d}{k}
   + \left[(\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2 - (p \mid p')^2
    - \smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 \right]
   \sin^2 \frac{d}{k} \\
   + (p \mid p')^2 = 0. \tag{19}
\end{multline*}\ungap
%
\begin{multline*}
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 \cos^4 \frac{d}{k}
   + \left[(p \mid p')^2 - (\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2
    - \smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 p_{ij}{}'^2 \right]
   \cos^2 \frac{d}{k} \\
   + (\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2 = 0. \tag{20}
\end{multline*}

The square roots of the products of the roots of these two
equations are well-known metrical invariants, and have been
studied under the names of \emph{moment}\index{Moment01@Moment, relative of two lines}
and \emph{commoment} of the
two lines.%
\footnote{See D'Ovidio\index{Ovidio@d'Ovidio},
`Studio sulla geometria proiettiva,' \emph{Annali di Matematica},
vi, 1873, and `Le funzioni metriche fondamentali negli spazii di quantesivogliono
dimensioni', \emph{Memorie dei Lincei}, i, 1877.}
%endfootnote
We shall return to the moment presently, attaching
a particular value to the signs of the radicals in the
denominator. If two lines intersect the moment must be zero,
and if each intersect the absolute polar of the other, the
commoment must vanish, thus bringing us back to equations
(15), (16).

To reach the limiting euclidean case we replace, as usual,
$x_0$ by $kx_0$, divide out $k^2$, and put $\dfrac{1}{k^2} = 0$. Then, since
\begin{equation*}
\lim_{k \doteq \infty} k \sin \frac{d}{k} = d.
\end{equation*}
\begin{multline*}
\text{We have } d^2 =\\
\frac{(p \mid p')^2}{(p_{01}{}^2 + p_{02}{}^2 + p_{03}{}^2)(p_{01}{}'^2 + p_{02}{}'^2 + p_{03}{}'^2)
    - (p_{01}p_{01}{}' + p_{02}p_{02}{}' + p_{03}p_{03}{}')^2},
\tag{21}
\end{multline*}
the usual formula\index{Distance03@--- of skew lines}.

With regard to the signs of the roots in (19) we see that in
the hyperbolic case, where the two lines are actual, one of
%[File: 114.png]
the points chosen to determine each line will be actual and the
other ideal, so that
\[
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 < 0,\quad
\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 < 0,
\]
\[
(p \mid p')^2 < 0,
\]
\[
\sin^2{\frac{d_1}{k}}
\sin^2{\frac{d_2}{k}} < 0.
\]
The square of the moment of the two lines is negative, so that
one distance will be real and the other pure imaginary. In
the elliptic case the two distances will be real.

\mysect{Criteria for parallelism and parataxy in line coordinates}
We shall mean by the angle of two non-intersecting lines
the angles of the plane, one through each, which contain the
same common perpendicular. This will be $k$ times the corresponding
distance of the absolute polars of the lines. We
thus get for the angles\index{Angle08@--- of skew lines} $\theta$ of the two lines ($p$), ($p'$)
\begin{multline*}
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2\sin^4{\theta} +
[(\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2 -
(p \mid p')^2 -
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2]
\sin^2{\theta} \\
+ (p \mid p')^2 = 0.
\end{multline*}
To get the euclidean formula we make the usual substitutions
and divisions, and put $\dfrac{1}{k} = 0 $, thus getting the well-known
formula
\[
\sin^2{\theta} =
\frac{
(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2 + x_3{}^2)
(x_1{}'^2 + x_2{}'^2 + x_3{}'^2) -
(x_1x_1{}' + x_2x_2{}' + x_3x_3{}')^2
}
{
(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2 + x_3{}^2)
(x_1{}'^2 + x_2{}'^2 + x_3{}'^2)
}.
\tag{22}
\]
The coordinates of the line $q$ cutting $p$ and $p'$ at right angles
will be given by
\[
(p \mid q) =
(p' \mid q) =
\smallsum p_{ij} q_{ij} =
\smallsum p_{ij}{}' q_{ij} =
(q \mid q) = 0.
\]
We have defined as a parallel\index{Parallelism}, two lines whose intersection
is on the Absolute\index{Absolute}; let us now give the name
\emph{pseudoparallel}\index{Pseudo-parallelism of lines}
to two coplanar lines whose plane touches the Absolute. The
necessary and sufficient condition that two lines should be
either parallel or pseudoparallel is that they should intersect,
and that there should be but a single line of their pencil
tangent to the Absolute. These conditions will be expressed
by the equations
\[
(p \mid p') =
[\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 -
(\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')^2] = 0
\tag{23}
\]

Let the reader notice that when we pass to the limit in the
usual way for the euclidean case, our equations (23) become
\[
(p \mid p') = \sin{\theta} = 0.
\tag{24}
\]

Let us now look at paratactic lines, i.e.\ lines which meet
the same two generators of one set of the Absolute. Of course
%[File: 115.png]
it is in the elliptic case only that two such lines can be real.
It is immediately evident that two paratactic lines have an
infinite number of common perpendiculars whereon they
always determine congruent distances, we have, in fact,
merely to look at the one-parameter group of translations
of space which carry these two lines into themselves. Conversely,
suppose that the distances of two lines be congruent.
Besides our previous equations connecting ($x$) ($y$) ($x'$) ($y'$), we
have
\[
\frac{(xx')^2}{(xx)(x'x')} =
\frac{(yy')^2}{(yy)(y'y')}.
\]

The lines $p$, $p'$ meet the Absolute respectively in the points
\[
\left
(x\sqrt{(yy)} \pm
iy\sqrt{(xx)}
\right)
\left
(x'\sqrt{(y'y')} \pm
iy'\sqrt{(x'x')}
\right).
\]
It is clear, however, that every point of the line
\[
\left(
x\sqrt{(yy)} +
iy\sqrt{(xx)}
\right)
\left
(x'\sqrt{(y'y')} +
iy'\sqrt{(x'x')}
\right),
\]
and of the line
\[
\left(
x\sqrt{(yy)} -
iy\sqrt{(xx)}
\right)
\left
(x'\sqrt{(y'y')} -
iy'\sqrt{(x'x')}
\right),
\]
\label{andoftheline}
belongs to the Absolute; the lines are paratactic. Lastly,
the absolute polars of paratactic lines are, themselves, paratactic\index{Parataxy}.
Hence

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~10. The necessary and sufficient condition that
two lines should be paratactic is that their distances or angles
should be congruent.

This condition may be expressed analytically by equating
to zero the discriminant of either of our equations (19), (20).
\begin{multline*}
\{[(p \mid p') + (\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')]^2 -
\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2 \}
\{[(p \mid p') + (\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}')]^2 \\
- \smallsum p_{ij}{}^2 \smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2\} = 0. \tag{25}
\end{multline*}

This puts in evidence that intersecting lines cannot be
paratactic unless they be parallel, or pseudoparallel.

\mysect{Relative moment of two directed lines}
In conclusion, let us return for an instant to the moment\index{Distance03@--- of skew lines} of
two real lines.
\[
\sin{\frac{d_1}{k}}
\sin{\frac{d_2}{k}} =
\frac{(p \mid p')}
{\sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2}\;
\sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2}}.
\]

We shall assume that the radicals in the denominator
are taken positively, so that the sign of the moment is
that of ($p \mid p'$). We now proceed to replace our concept of
a line by the sharper concept of a \emph{ray}\index{Ray} as follows. Let us,
%[File: 116.png]
in the hyperbolic case assume always $\dot x_0 > 0$, and in the
elliptic case $x_0 > 0$. The coordinates
\[
p_{ij'} = \left| \begin{matrix}
y_i{}' & y_j{}' \\
z_i{}' & z_j{}' \end{matrix} \right|,\quad
p_{ij} = \left| \begin{matrix}
y_i & y_j \\
z_i & z_j \end{matrix} \right|,
\]
shall be called the coordinates of the ray from $(y)$ to $(z)$, and
this shall be considered equivalent to any other ray\index{Ray} whose
coordinates differ therefrom by a positive factor. Interchanging
$(y)$ and $(z)$ will give a second ray, said to be \emph{opposite}
to this. The relative moment\index{Moment02@--- relative of two rays} of two rays is thus determined,
both in magnitude and sign. We shall later see various
applications of this concept.

%[File: 117.png]
%CHAPTER X
\mychap{X}{THE HIGHER LINE GEOMETRY}
\mysect{Linear complex in hyperbolic space}

In Chapter IX we took some first steps in non-euclidean
line-geometry. The object of the present chapter is to
continue the subject in the special direction where the
fundamental element is not, in general, a line, but a pair
of lines invariantly connected.%
\footnote{Practically
the whole of this chapter is sketched, without proofs, by
Study\index{Study} in his article, `Zur nicht-euklidischen etc.,' loc.\ cit. The elliptic case
is developed at length in the author's\index{Author} dissertation, `The dual projective
geometry of elliptic and spherical space,' Greifswald, 1904. For the hyperbolic
case, see the dissertation of Beck\index{Beck}, `Die Strahlenketten im hyperbolischen
Raume,' Hannover, 1905.}
%endfootnote

Let us start in the real domain of hyperbolic space and
consider a linear complex\index{Complex of lines} whose equation is
\[
(\dot{a} \mid \dot{p}) = 0.
\]
The dots indicate that the coordinates of a point are
$\dot{x}_0$, $\dot{x}_1$, $\dot{x}_2$, $\dot{x}_3$,  and choosing such a unit of measure that
$k^2 = -1$, we have for the Absolute\index{Absolute}
\[
- \dot{x}_0{}^2 + \dot{x}_1{}^2 + \dot{x}_2{}^2 + \dot{x}_3{}^2 = 0.
\]
The polar of the given complex will have the coordinates
\[
\dot{a}_{0i} = r\dot{b}_{jk},\quad
\dot{a}_{jk} =  - r\dot{b}_{0i},\quad
i, j, k = 1, 2, 3,
\]
and the congruence, whose equations are
\[
(\dot{a} \mid \dot{p}) =
\smallsum \dot{a}_{0i} \dot{p}_{0i} -
\smallsum \dot{a}_{jk} \dot{p}_{jk} = 0,
\]
will be composed of all lines of our complex and its absolute
polar, or common to all complexes of the pencil
\[
(l\dot{a}_{01} - m\dot{a}_{23})\ldots
(l\dot{a}_{23} + m\dot{a}_{01}).
\]

These complexes shall be said to form a \emph{coaxal
pencil}\index{Coaxal pencil of complexes}\index{Pencil01@Pencil of complexes}, and
the two mutually absolute polar lines, which are the directrices
of the congruence, shall be called \emph{axes}\index{Axis05@--- of a pencil of complexes}
of the pencil. We get
their pl\"uckerian coordinates by giving to $l:m$ such values
that the complex shall be special. Let us now write
\[\tag{1}
\begin{aligned}
& \dot{a}_{01} + i\dot{a}_{23} = \rho X_1, \\
& \dot{a}_{02} + i\dot{a}_{31} = \rho X_2, \\
& \dot{a}_{03} + i\dot{a}_{12} = \rho X_3.
\end{aligned}
\]
%[File: 118.png]
A complex coaxal with the given line will be obtained by
multiplying the numbers $(X)$ by $(l + mi)$.

\mysect{The cross, its coordinates}
A pair of real lines which are mutually absolute polar,
neither of which is tangent to the Absolute\index{Absolute}, shall be called
a \emph{proper cross}\index{Cross}. They will determine a pencil of coaxal
complexes. If either of the lines have the pl\"uckerian coordinates
$(a)$, then the three numbers $(X)$ given by equations (1)
may be taken to represent the cross. These coordinates $(X)$
are homogeneous in the complex (i.e.\ imaginary) domain, for
the result of multiplying them through by $(l + mi)$ is to
replace the complex $(\dot{a})$ by a coaxal complex, and therefore
to leave the axes of the pencil unaltered.

Conversely, suppose that we have a triad of coordinates $(X)$
which are homogeneous in the imaginary domain. The coordinates
of the lines of the corresponding cross will be found
from (1) by assigning to $\rho$ such a value that the coordinates
$(\dot{a})$ shall satisfy the fundamental pl\"uckerian identity. For
this it is necessary and sufficient that the imaginary part of
$\rho^2(XX)$ should vanish, i.e.
\[\tag{2}
\begin{aligned}
  \sigma\dot{a}_{0\,1} &= \Bigl( \frac{X_i}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(XX)}} +
  \frac{\overline{X_i}}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(\bar{X}\bar{X})}} \Bigr),
\\
  \sigma\dot{a}_{j\,k} &= -i\Bigl(\frac{X_i}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(XX)}} -
  \frac{\overline{X_i}}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(\bar{X}\bar{X})}} \Bigr).
\end{aligned}
\]
To get the other line of the cross, i.e.\ the Absolute polar
of the line $(\dot{a})$, we merely have to reverse the sign of one
of our radicals.

There is one, and only one case, where our equations (2)
become illusory, namely where
\[
(XX) = 0.
\]

This will arise when
\[
(\dot{a} \mid \dot{a})
= \smallsum\dot{a}_{0i}{}^2 - \smallsum\dot{a}_{ik}{}^2 = 0,
\]
i.e.\ when the directrices of the congruence are tangent to the
Absolute. All complexes of the pencil will here be special,
and will be determined severally by lines intersecting the
various tangents to the Absolute at this point. Any mutually
polar lines of the pencil of tangents, will, conversely, serve to
determine the coaxal system. We may then represent such
a pencil of tangent lines by a set of homogeneous values $(X)$
where $(XX) = 0$, and, conversely, every such set of homogeneous
values will determine a pencil of tangents to the
Absolute. We shall therefore define such a pencil of tangents
as an \emph{improper cross}\index{Improper01@Improper cross}.

%[File: 119.png]
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~1. There exists a perfect one to one correspondence
between the assemblage of all crosses in hyperbolic space, and
the assemblage of all points of the complex plane of elliptic
space. Improper crosses will correspond to points of the
elliptic Absolute\index{Absolute}.

We shall say that two crosses intersect if their lines intersect.
The N.~S. condition for this in the case of two proper
crosses will be
\begin{equation*}
  \frac{(XY)}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(XX)}\;\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(YY)}} =
  \frac{\pm(\bar{X}\bar{Y})}{\sqrt{\mystrut{9}(\bar{X}\bar{X})}\;
  \sqrt{\mystrut{9}(\bar{Y}\bar{Y})}}.
\end{equation*}
Geometrically a line may intersect either member of a cross\index{Cross}.
This ambiguity disappears in the case of perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} intersection.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~2. Two intersecting crosses will correspond to
points, the cosine of whose distance is real, or pure imaginary;
crosses intersecting orthogonally will correspond to orthogonal
points\index{Orthogonal points} of the elliptic plane.

\mysect{The use of the cross manifold to interpret the geometry of the complex plane}
The assemblage of crosses which intersect a given cross
orthogonally will be given by means of a linear equation.
A linear equation will be transformed linearly into another
linear equation, if the variables and coefficients be treated
contragrediently. Geometrically we shall imagine that our
assemblage of crosses, \emph{cross space}\index{Cross space} let us say, is doubly
overlaid,
the crosses of one layer\index{Layer of cross space} being represented by points and
those of the other by lines in the complex plane\index{Plane}, we have then

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~3. The necessary and sufficient condition that two
crosses of different layers should intersect orthogonally is that
the corresponding line and point of the complex plane should
be in united position.

If a cross be improper\index{Improper01@Improper cross}, the assemblage of all crosses cutting
it orthogonally will be made up of all lines through the point
of contact, and all lines in the plane of contact. This assemblage,
reducible in point space, is irreducible in cross space.

The collineation group of cross space, is the general group
depending on eight complex, or sixteen real parameters
\begin{equation*}
  \rho X_i{}' = \sum_j^{1..3} a_{ij}X_i,\quad |a_{ij}| \neq 0.\tag{3}
\end{equation*}

When will this indicate a transformation of point space?
It is certainly necessary that improper crosses should go into
improper crosses, hence the substitution must be of the orthogonal
%[File: 120.png]
type. Moreover, the Absolute\index{Absolute} of hyperbolic space will
be transformed into itself, so that our transformation of point
space must be a congruent one. Conversely, it is immediately
evident that a congruent transformation will transform cross
space linearly into itself. Also, an orthogonal substitution in
cross coordinates will carry an improper cross\index{Cross} into an improper
cross, and will carry intersecting crosses into other
intersecting crosses. The corresponding transformation in
point space is not completely determined, for a polar reciprocation
in the Absolute of point space appears as the identical
transformation of cross space. A transformation which
carries intersecting crosses into intersecting crosses may thus
be interpreted either as a collineation\index{Collineations}, or a correlation of
point space.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~4. Every collineation or correlation of hyperbolic
space which leaves the Absolute invariant will be equivalent
to an orthogonal substitution in cross space, and every such
orthogonal substitution may be interpreted either as a congruent
transformation of hyperbolic space, or a congruent
transformation coupled with a polar reciprocation in the
Absolute.

\mysect{Chain, and chain surface}
Let us now inquire as to what are the simplest figures of
cross space. The simplest one dimensional figure is the \emph{chain}\index{Chain02@--- of crosses}
composed of all crosses whose coordinates are linearly dependent,
by means of real coefficients, on those of two given
crosses,
\begin{equation*}
\rho X_i = aY_i + bZ_i, \quad i = 1,\: 2,\: 3.
\tag{4}
\end{equation*}

Interpreting these equations in the complex plane we see
that we have $\infty^1$ points of a line so related that the cross ratio
of any four is real. If this line be represented in the Gauss
plane, the chain will be represented by a circle. If the line
be imaginary, the real lines, one through each point of the
chain, will generate a linear pencil or a regulus.%
\footnote{The concept `chain of imaginary points' is due to Von Staudt. See his
`Beitr\"age', loc.\ cit., pp.~137--42. For an extension, see Segre\index{Segre}, `Su un nuovo
campo di ricerche geometriche,' \emph{Atti della R. Accademia delle Scienze di Torino},
vol.~xxv, 1890.}
%endfootnote

The crosses of the chain will cut orthogonally another cross
(of the other layer\index{Layer of cross space})
called the \emph{axis}\index{Axis04@--- of a chain} of the chain. The axis
being proper, the chain will contain two improper crosses,
namely, the pencils of tangents to the Absolute where it
meets the actual line of the chain.

There is a theorem of very great generality connected with
chains, which we shall now give. Suppose that we have a
%[File: 121.png]
congruence of lines of such a nature that the corresponding
cross coordinates $(U)$ are analytic functions of two real
parameters $u, v$. The cross of common perpendiculars to the
cross $(U)$ and the adjacent cross $(U+dU)$ will be given by
\begin{equation*}
X_i=\left|\begin{array}{cc}
U_j & U_k \smallskip\\
\dfrac{\partial U_j}{\partial u} & \dfrac{\partial U_k}{\partial u}
\end{array} \right| du +
\left|\begin{array}{cc}
U_j & U_k \smallskip\\
\dfrac{\partial U_j}{\partial v} & \dfrac{\partial U_k}{\partial v}
\end{array} \right| dv. \tag{5}
\end{equation*}
There are two sharply distinct sub-cases, (\emph{a})
\begin{equation*}
\left| U \frac{\partial U}{\partial u} \frac{\partial U}{\partial v} \right|
\equiv 0. \tag{6}
\end{equation*}

\mysect{Hamilton's theorem}
Here there is but one common perpendicular to $(U)$ and all
adjacent crosses. Such a congruence shall be called
\emph{synectic}\index{Congruence05@--- synectic}\index{Synectic congruence}.
Let us exclude this case for the moment and pass to the other,
where, (\emph{b})
\begin{equation*}
\left| U \frac{\partial U}{\partial u} \frac{\partial U}{\partial v} \right|
\not\equiv 0. \tag{7}
\end{equation*}
We shall mean by the \emph{general position} of a line in such a congruence,
one where this determinant does not vanish. We
have then the theorem:%
\footnote{The analogous theorem for euclidean space is due to Hamilton\index{Hamilton}, see
his paper on `Systems of Rays', \emph{Transactions of the Royal Irish Academy}, vol.~xv,
1829.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~5. The common perpendiculars to a line, in the
general position, of a non-synectic congruence, and each
adjacent line will generate a chain.

Let us find, in point coordinates, the equation of the surface
obtained by splitting off from a chain\index{Chain02@--- of crosses} its improper crosses.
We easily see that there will be two crosses of the chain
which intersect orthogonally; taking these and the axes to
determine the coordinate system, we may express our chain
in the simple form
\begin{equation*}
X_1=a(p+qi), \quad X_2=b(r+si), \quad X_3=0.
\end{equation*}

Eliminating $a/b$ we get
\begin{equation*}
(p \dot a_{31}+q \dot a_{02})(r \dot a_{10}-s \dot a_{23})=(p \dot a_{02}-q
\dot a_{31})(r \dot a_{23}+s \dot a_{01}).
\end{equation*}
This gives the equation of the chain surface in point coordinates
\begin{equation*}
(ps-qr)(-\dot x_0{}^2+\dot x_3{}^2) \dot x_1 \dot x_2 + (pr + qs) (\dot x_1{}^2 +
\dot x_2{}^2 ) \dot x_0 \dot x_3 = 0. \tag{8}
\end{equation*}
%[File: 122.png]
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{If} & (ps-qr) &= 0 \quad \mbox{or} \quad (pr+qs)=0, & \phantom{\text{If}}
\end{flalign*}
we have two real and two imaginary linear pencils; the conditions
for this in cross coordinates will be invariant under
the orthogonal, but not under the general group. The general
form of our surface is a ruled quartic, having a strong similarity
to the euclidean cylindroid.

\mysect{Chain congruence, synectic and non-synectic congruences}
The simplest two dimensional system of crosses is the \emph{chain
congruence}\index{Chain01@Chain congruence}\index{Congruence06@--- chain}.
This is made up of all crosses which have coordinates
linearly dependent with real coefficients on those of
three given crosses which do not cut a fourth orthogonally
\begin{equation*}
X_i=a Y_i+b Z_i+c T_0,
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
| XYZ | \neq 0,  \quad i = 1,\: 2,\: 3. \tag{9}
\end{equation*}

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~6. The crosses which correspond to the assemblage
of all points of the real domain of a plane will generate
a chain congruence.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~7. The common perpendiculars to pairs of crosses
of a chain congruence will generate a second chain congruence
in the other layer. Each congruence is the locus of the axes
of the $\infty^2$ chains of the other; the two are said to be reciprocal
to one another.

The reciprocal to the chain congruence (9) will have equations
\begin{equation*}
U_i=p \left| \begin{array}{cc}
Y_i & Y_k \\
Z_j & Z_k
\end{array} \right|+
q \left| \begin{array}{cc}
Z_j & Z_k \\
T_j & T_k
\end{array} \right|+
r \left| \begin{array}{cc}
T_j & T_k \\
Y_i & Y_k
\end{array} \right|. \tag{10}
\end{equation*}

Let the reader show that the chain congruence may be
reduced to the canonical form
\begin{equation*}
X_1=a(p+qi), \quad X_2=b(r+si), \quad X_3=c(t+ri),
\end{equation*}
where $a$, $b$, $c$ are real homogeneous variables.

There are various sub-cases under the congruent group. If
\begin{equation*}
(ps-qr) = 0,
\end{equation*}
the congruence will be transformed into itself by a one-parameter
group of rotations.

Again, let
\begin{equation*}
(ps-qr) = 0, \quad (pr-qt) = 0.
\end{equation*}
Here we see that
\begin{equation*}
\frac{(XX')}{\sqrt{(XX)}\;\sqrt{(X'X')}}
\end{equation*}
is real for any two crosses of the congruence, i.e.\ the congruence
consists in all crosses through the point $(1,\:0,\:0,\:0)$.

Leaving aside the special cases the following theorems may
be proved for the general case.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~8. The chain congruence, considered as an assemblage
%[File: 123.png]
of lines in point space, is of the third order and class.
It is generated by common perpendiculars to the pairs of lines
of a regulus. Those lines of the congruence which meet a line
of the reciprocal congruence, orthogonally generate a quartic
surface, those which meet such a line obliquely generate a
regulus whose conjugate belongs to the reciprocal congruence.
The two congruences have the same focal surface of order and
class eight.

Another simple two-parameter system of crosses is the
following
\begin{equation*}
\rho X_i=a Y_i+b Z_i+c T_i,
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
p Y_i+q Z_i+s T_i \neq 0, \quad |YZT|=0,\; (a\:b\:c\:p\:q\:r) \text{\ real}.
\end{equation*}
All these crosses cut orthogonally the cross
\begin{equation*}
U_i=\left|\begin{array}{cc}
Y_j & Y_k \\
Z_j & Z_k
\end{array} \right|.
\end{equation*}

Conversely, let us show that every cross orthogonally intersecting
$(U)$ may be expressed in this form. As such a form
as this is invariant for all linear transformations, we may
suppose
\begin{equation*}
Y_3 = Z_3 = T_3 = 0.
\end{equation*}

We have then the equations
\begin{align*}
a Y_1+b Z_1+c T_1&=(r+ir')X_1, \\
a Y_2+b Z_2+c T_2&=(r+ir')X_2,
\end{align*}
which amount to four linear homogeneous equations in five
unknowns $a$, $b$, $c$, $r$, $r'$ and these may always be solved. There
will be found to be one singular case where the same cross
has $\infty^1$\label{inftyp} determinations.

The assemblage of crosses cutting a cross orthogonally is
but a special case of what we have already defined as a
synectic congruence\index{Congruence05@--- synectic}\index{Synectic congruence}. If
\begin{equation*}
X = X(uv), \quad \left| X \frac{\partial X}{\partial u} \frac{\partial X}{\partial v} \right| \equiv 0,
\end{equation*}
there will be but one common perpendicular to a cross and its
adjacent crosses. This corresponds to the fact that there will
exist an equation
\begin{equation*}
f(X_1,X_2,X_3)=0,
\end{equation*}
so that our congruence is represented by a curve, the tangent
at any point representing the common perpendicular just
mentioned (in the other layer), and, conversely, every curve
will be represented by a synectic congruence. The points and
tangents will be represented by two synectic congruences so
%[File: 124.png]
related that each cross of one is a cross of striction of a cross
of the other, and all its adjacent crosses. We may reach
a still clearer idea of these congruences by anticipating some
of the results of differential geometry to be proved in later
chapters. For, if we look upon the congruence of lines
generated by our crosses, we see that the two focal points
on each are orthogonal and the two focal planes mutually
perpendicular. From this we shall conclude that our line-congruence
is one of normals\index{Congruence10@--- of normals, to surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero},
and the characteristics of the
developable surfaces of the congruence will be geodesics of
the focal surface, to which the lines of the other congruence
are binormals. We shall, moreover, show in a later chapter
that if $r_1$ and $r_2$ be the radii of curvature of normal sections of
a surface in planes of curvature, then the Gaussian\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian} expression
for the curvature of the surface at that point will be
\[
  \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}} \cdot
  \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} +
  \frac{1}{k^2}.
\]
In the present instance as the two focal points are orthogonal
\[
  \frac{r_2}{k} = \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{r_1}{k}, \quad
  \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}} \cdot
  \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} +
  \frac{1}{k^2} = 0.
\]
Our congruence is made up of normals to surfaces of Gaussian
curvature zero, i.e.\ to surfaces whose distance element may
be written
\[
ds^2 = du^2 + dv^2.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~9.%
\footnote{Cf.\ Study\index{Study}, `Zur nicht-euklidischen etc.,' cit., p.~328.}
%endfootnote
A synectic congruence will represent the points
of a curve of the complex plane. It will be made up of crosses
whose lines are normals to a series of surfaces of Gaussian
curvature zero\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero}. The characteristics of the developable surfaces
are geodesics of the focal surfaces. Their orthogonal trajectories
are a second set of geodesics whose tangents will
generate a like congruence.

In conclusion, let us emphasize the distinction between
these congruences and the non-synectic ones, where the
common perpendiculars to a cross and its adjacent ones
generate a chain.

Did we wish to represent the imaginary as well as the
real members of a synectic or non-synectic congruence, we
should be obliged to introduce into our representing plane,
points with hypercomplex coordinates. We shall not enter
into this extension, for, after all, the real point of interest of
%[File: 125.png]
the subject lies merely in this, namely, to give a real interpretation
for the geometry of the complex plane.

\mysect{Dual coordinates of a cross in elliptic case}
As we identify the geometry of the cross\index{Cross} in hyperbolic
space with that of a point of the complex plane, so we may
relate a cross of elliptic (or spherical) space to a pair of real
points of two plane. The modus operandi is as follows:---

We start, as before, with a pencil\index{Coaxal pencil of complexes} of coaxal linear complexes
defined by

\begin{align*}
a_{01}+a_{23}=\rho\,_lX_1, \quad &a_{01}-a_{23}=\sigma\,_rX_1,\\
a_{02}+a_{31}=\rho\,_lX_2, \quad &a_{02}-a_{31}=\sigma\,_rX_2,\tag{11}\\
a_{03}+a_{12}=\rho\,_lX_3, \quad &a_{03}-a_{12}=\sigma\,_rX_3,
\end{align*}

If we replace our complex by another coaxal therewith, we
shall merely multiply $({}_lX) ({}_rX)$ by two different constants.
Conversely, when we wish to move back from the independently
homogeneous sets of coordinates $({}_lX) ({}_rX)$ to the
degenerate complexes of the pencil, i.e.\ to the lines of the
cross defined thereby, we have to take for $\rho$ and $\sigma$ such values
that the fundamental pl\"uckerian identity is satisfied,
\[\tag{12}
\begin{aligned}
\tau a_{0i}&={}_lX_i\sqrt{(_rX_rX)}+ {}_rX_i\sqrt{({}_lX_lX)},\\
\tau a_{jk}&={}_lX_i\sqrt{(_rX_rX)}- {}_rX_i\sqrt{({}_lX_lX)}.
\end{aligned}
\]

The two separately homogeneous coordinate triads $( _lX)( _rX)$
may be taken to represent this proper cross, and, conversely,
as all quantities involved so far are supposed to be real, every
real pair of triads will correspond to a single cross.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~10. The assemblage of all real crosses of elliptic
or spherical space may be put into one to one correspondence
with the assemblage of all pairs of points one in each of two
real planes.

Our doubly homogeneous coordinates have a second interpretation
which is of the highest interest. Let us write the
coordinates of a point of the Absolute\index{Absolute} in terms of two independent
parameters, i.e.\ of the parameters determining the
one and the other set of linear generators
\begin{equation*}
x_0:ix_1:x_2:ix_3\\
=(\lambda_1\mu_1-\lambda_2\mu_2):(\lambda_1\mu_1+\lambda_2\mu_2):(\lambda_1\mu_2+\lambda_2\mu_1):(\lambda_1\mu_2-\lambda_2\mu_1).
\end{equation*}

The pl\"uckerian coordinates of a generator of the left or right
system\index{Left and right generators of Absolute}\index{Right and left generators of Absolute}
will thus be
\begin{align*}
&p_{01}=p_{23}=2\lambda_1\lambda_2, &q_{01}&=-q_{23}=2\mu_1\mu_2,\\
&p_{02}=p_{31}=i(\lambda_1{}^2+\lambda_2{}^2), &q_{02}&=-q_{31}=i(\mu_1{}^2+\mu_2{}^2),\\
&p_{03}=p_{12}=(\lambda_1{}^2-\lambda_2{}^2), &q_{03}&=-q_{12}=-(\mu_1{}^2+\mu_2{}^2).
\end{align*}
%[File: 126.png]
The parameter $(\lambda)$ of a left generator which meets a given
line $(a)$ will satisfy
\begin{equation*}
2\lambda_1\lambda_2(a_{01}+a_{23})+i(\lambda_1{}^2+\lambda_2{}^2)(a_{02}+a_{31})+(\lambda_1{}^2-\lambda_2{}^2)(a_{03}+a_{12})=0.
\end{equation*}
Similarly, for a right generator we have
\begin{equation*}
2\mu_1\mu_2(a_{01}-a_{23})+i(\mu_1{}^2+\mu_2{}^2)(a_{02}-a_{31})-(\mu_1{}^2-\mu_2{}^2)(a_{03}-a_{12})=0.
\end{equation*}\index{Left and right generators of Absolute}\index{Right and left generators of Absolute}

\mysect{Condition for parataxy}
We thus get as a necessary and sufficient condition that
two lines should be right (left) paratactic\index{Parataxy}, that the differences
(sums) of complementary pairs of pl\"uckerian coordinates in
the one shall be proportional to the corresponding differences
(sums) in the other. If the lines be $(p)$ and $(p')$, the first of
these conditions will be
\begin{equation*}
[(p \mid p')+\Sigma p_{ij}p{ij}']^2-\Sigma p_{ij}{}^2\Sigma p_{ij}{}'^2=0,
\end{equation*}
while the second is
\begin{equation*}
[(p \mid p')-\Sigma p_{ij}p{ij}']^2-\Sigma p_{ij}{}^2\Sigma p_{ij}{}'^2=0.
\end{equation*}

If these equations be multiplied together, we get (25) of
Chapter IX.

If a line pass through the point $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ its last three
pl\"uckerian coordinates will vanish, while the first three are
proportional to those of its intersections with $x_0 = 0$. It thus
appears that in (11) the coordinates $( _lX)$ and $( _rX)$ are nothing
more nor less than the coordinates of the points, where the
plane $x_0 = 0$ is met respectively by the left and the right
paratactic through the point $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ to the two lines of the
cross, for a line paratactic to the one is also paratactic to
the other. It will, however, be more convenient to consider
$( _lX)$ and $( _rX)$ as standing for points in two different planes,
called, respectively, \emph{the left and right representing planes}.
We shall speak of two crosses as being paratactic, when their
lines are so, and the necessary and sufficient condition therefore,
invariant under the group of cross space, is that they
should be represented by identical points in the one or the
other plane.%
\footnote{The whole question of left and right is considered most carefully in
Study's\index{Study} `Beitr\"age', cit., pp.~126, 156.}
%endfootnote

As in the hyperbolic case, so here, we shall look upon cross
space as doubly overlaid, and assign a cross to the upper layer\index{Layer of cross space}
if it be determined by two points in the representing planes,
while it shall be assigned to the lower layer if it be determined
by two lines. Under these circumstances we may say:---

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~11. In order that two crosses\index{Cross} of different layers
should intersect orthogonally, it is necessary and sufficient
%[File: 127.png]
that they should be represented by line elements in the two
planes.

\mysect{Clifford angles}
We may go still further in this same direction. We shall
mean by the right and left \emph{Clifford angles}\index{Angles03@--- Clifford}\index{Clifford}
of two crosses, the
angles of right and left paratactics to them through any chosen
point. Let the reader show that the magnitude of these angles
is independent of the choice of the last-named point. If, thus,
we choose the point $(1, 0, 0, 0)$, the cosines of the Clifford
angles will be
\begin{equation*}
\frac{( _lX_lY)}{\sqrt{( _lX_lX)}\; \sqrt{( _lY_lY)}}, \quad
\frac{( _rX_rY)}{\sqrt{( _rX_rX)}\; \sqrt{( _rY_rY)}}.
\end{equation*}

Now, from equations (19) and (20) of Chapter IX, we see that
\begin{align*}
&\sin\frac{d}{k} \sin\frac{d'}{k}=\sin\theta \sin\theta'=\frac{(p \mid p')}{\sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2}\; \sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2}}, \\
&\cos\frac{d}{k} \cos\frac{d'}{k}=\cos\theta \cos\theta'=\frac{\smallsum p_{ij}p_{ij}{}'}{\sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}^2}\; \sqrt{\smallsum p_{ij}{}'^2}};
\end{align*}
hence, we easily find
\begin{multline*}
\quad\text(1)\quad\cos\Bigl(\frac{d}{k}+\frac{d'}{k}\Bigr)=\frac{(_rX_rY)}{\sqrt{(_rX_rX)}\; \sqrt{(_rY_rY)}},
\\
\cos\Bigl(\frac{d}{k}-\frac{d'}{k}\Bigr)=\frac{(_lX_lY)}{\sqrt{(_lX_lX)}\; \sqrt{(_lY_lY)}}, \tag{13}
\end{multline*}
or else\label{rXrX}
\begin{multline*}
\quad\text(2)\quad\cos\Bigl(\frac{d}{k}-\frac{d'}{k}\Bigr)=\frac{(_rX_rY)}{\sqrt{(_rX_rX)}\; \sqrt{(_rY_rY)}},
\\
\cos\Bigl(\frac{d}{k}+\frac{d'}{k}\Bigr)=\frac{(_lX_lY)}{\sqrt{(_lX_lX)}\; \sqrt{(_lY_lY)}}. \phantom{(13)}
\end{multline*}

The ambiguity can be removed by establishing certain conventions
with regard to the signs of the radicals, into which
we shall not enter.%
\footnote{For an elaborate discussion, see Study\index{Study},
`Beitr\"age,' cit., especially p.~130.}
%endfootnote
We may, however, state the following
theorem:---

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~12. The Clifford angles of two lines have the same
measures as the sums and differences of the $k$th parts of their
distances, or the sums and differences of their angles. The
necessary and sufficient condition that two lines should intersect
is that their Clifford angles should be equal or supplementary.

%[File: 128.png]
When we adjoin the imaginary domain to the real one,
serious complications will arise which can only be removed
by careful definition. Without going into a complete discussion,
we merely give the facts.%
\footnote{Cf.\ the author's\index{Author} `Dual projective Geometry', loc.\ cit., \S~3.}
%endfootnote

If $( _lX_lX) = 0$, $( _rX_rX) \not= 0$, we shall say that we have
a \emph{left improper cross}\index{Improper01@Improper cross}, and denote thereby a left generator
of the Absolute\index{Absolute}, conjoined to a non-parabolic involution
among the right generators. There will be $\infty^3$ such improper
crosses, and $\infty^3$ right improper crosses, whose definition is
obvious. Left and right improper crosses together will constitute
what shall be called \emph{improper crosses of the first sort}.
Improper crosses of the second sort shall be defined, as in
hyperbolic space, as pencils of tangents to the Absolute,
corresponding to sets of values for which $( _lX_lX) = ( _rX_rX) = 0$.
The definitions of parataxy and orthogonal intersection may
be extended to all cases, their analytic expression being as
in the real domain.

The general group of linear transformations of cross space
will depend upon sixteen essential parameters. It will be
made up of the sixteen-parameter sub-group $G_{16}$ of all
transformations of the type
%
\begin{equation*}
\rho \, _lX_i{}' = \sum_j a_{ij} \,_lX_j, \quad
\sigma \, _rX_i{}' = \sum_j b_{ij} \, _rX_j, \quad
|a_{ij}| \times |b_{ij}| \not=0,
\tag{14}
\end{equation*}
%
and the sixteen-parameter assemblage $H_{16}$ of all
transformations of the type
%
\begin{equation*}
\rho \, _lX_i{}' = \sum_j a_{ij} \, _rX_j, \quad
\sigma \, _rX_i{}' = \sum_j b_{ij} \, _lX_j, \quad
|a_{ij}| \times |b_{ij}| \not=0.
\tag{15}
\end{equation*}
%
Notice that under $G_{16}$ left and right parataxy of crosses of
the same layer are invariant, while under $H_{16}$ the two sorts
of parataxy are interchanged.

The group $G_{16}$ will contain, as a sub-group, the group of all
motions, while $H_{16}$ includes the assemblage of all symmetry
transformations\index{Symmetry transformations}. Let the reader show that there can be no
collineations\index{Collineations} of point space under $G_{16}$, except congruent transformations,
and that the necessary and sufficient condition
that (14) should represent a motion of point space is that
the transformations of the two representing planes should
be of the orthogonal type.

The group $G_{16}$ is half-simple, being composed entirely of
two invariant sub-groups $_lG_8$, $_rG_8$, of which the former is
made up of the general linear transformation for $(_lX)$ with
%[File: 129.png]
the identical transformation for $(_rX)$, while in the latter, the
r\^oles of $(_lX)$ and $(_rX)$ are interchanged. The highest common
factors of the group of motions with $_lG_8$ and $_rG_8$ respectively,
will be the groups of left and right translations\index{Translations} (cf.
Chapter IX).

The simplest assemblages of crosses in elliptic space bear
a close analogy to those of hyperbolic space, although possessing
more variety in the real domain. Let
%
\[
_lX_i = a\, _lY_i + b\, _lZ_i, \quad
_rX_i = a\, _rY_i + b\, _rZ_i,
\]
\[
|_lX\, _lY\, _lS| \times
|_rX\, _rY\, _rT| \not\equiv 0.
\]

\mysect{Chain and strip}
The assemblage of crosses so defined shall be called a \emph{chain}\index{Chain02@--- of crosses}.
The properties of these chains are entirely analogous to those
in the hyperbolic case. For instance, take a congruence of
crosses whose coordinates are analytic functions of two essential
parameters $(u)$, $(v)$. Let us further assume that $( _lY)$ $( _rY)$
being crosses of the system
%
\begin{equation*}
\left|
_lY \frac{\partial}{\partial u}\, _lY\frac{\partial}{\partial v}\, _lY
\right|
\times
\left|
_rY \frac{\partial}{\partial u}\, _rY\frac{\partial}{\partial v}\, _rY
\right|
\not\equiv 0.
\end{equation*}
%
The meaning of this restriction is that neither $(_lY)$ nor $(_rY)$
can be expressed as functions of a single parameter, so that
the crosses of the congruence cannot be assembled into the
generators of $\infty^1$ surfaces, those of each surface being paratactic.
Let the reader then show that for every such
congruence, the common perpendiculars to a line in the
general position, and its immediate neighbours, will generate
a chain.

The chains of elliptic cross space will have the same subclassifications
under the congruent group, as in the hyperbolic
plane. Let the reader show that the general chain may be
represented by means of a homographic relation between the
points of two linear ranges in the representing planes, and
that the special chain, composed of two pencils, arises, when
the relation is a congruent one.

Suppose, next, that we have
%
\[
\rho\, {}_lX_i = a\, {}_lY_i + b\, {}_lZ_i, \quad
\sigma\, {}_rX_i = a\, {}_rY_i + b\, {}_rZ_i,
\]
\[
{}_lY_i = r\, {}_lZ_i, \quad
|{}_rY {}_rZ {}_rT| \not\equiv 0.
\]
%
This is a new one-parameter family of crosses called a \emph{strip}\index{Strip},
or, more exactly, a \emph{left strip}. The common perpendiculars
to pairs of crosses of the left strip will generate a right strip
(whereof the definition is obvious), and each strip shall be said
to be reciprocal to the other. A left strip of the upper layer
%[File: 130.png]
will be represented by a point of the left plane, and a linear
range of the right plane. The reciprocal strip in the lower
layer will be represented by the pencil through the point
in the left plane, and the line of the range in the right.

In point space, the lines of a strip\index{Strip} are generators of a
quadric, whose other generators belong to the reciprocal strip.
Owing to the parataxy of the generators of such a quadric,
it will intersect the Absolute\index{Absolute} in two generators of each set.
We shall call our quadric a \emph{Clifford surface}\index{Clifford}, when we wish
to refer to it as a figure of point space. We shall show in
Chapter XV, that these surfaces have Gaussian curvature zero,
since they are generated by paratactic\index{Parataxy} lines, and are minimal
surfaces\index{Minimal surfaces}, since their asymptotic lines form an orthogonal
system.%
\footnote{Cf.\ Klein\index{Klein}, `Zur nicht-euklidischen Geometrie,' \emph{Mathematische Annalen},
vol.~xxxvii, 1890.}
%endfootnote

\mysect{Chain congruence}
The simplest two dimensional system of crosses will be, as
before, the chain congruence\index{Chain01@Chain congruence}\index{Congruence06@--- chain}
\[
_lX_i=a\, _lY_i+b\, _lZ_i+c\, _lT_i, \quad _rX_i=a\, _lX_i+b\, _lY_i+c\,  _lZ_i
\]
\[
| {}_lY {}_lZ {}_lT| \times | {}_rY {}_rZ{} _rT| \neq 0.
\]
We may solve the first three equations for $a,b,c$, and substitute
in the last
\begin{equation*}
_rX_i=\sum_j a_{ij}\, _lX_j, \quad |a_{ij}| \neq 0.
\end{equation*}
This, again, may easily be reduced to the canonical form
\begin{equation*}
_rX_i=a_i\, _lX_i \tag{16}
\end{equation*}
The reciprocal congruence will be given by
\begin{equation*}
_lU_i = a_i\, _rU_i. \nonumber
\end{equation*}

There are various sub-classes under the congruent group.
If the squares of no two of our quantities $a_i$ in (16) be equal,
we have the general congruence, if we have one such equality,
the congruence will be transformed into itself by a one-parameter
group of rotations. If all three squares be equal,
we have a bundle of crosses through a point. The general
congruence will have all of the properties mentioned in (8).

A different sort of congruence will arise in the case where
\begin{equation*}
|{}_lY {}_lZ {}_lT|=0, \quad |{}_rY {}_rZ {}_rT| \neq 0. \tag{17}
\end{equation*}
This congruence will contain $\infty^1$ strips, whose reciprocals
generate the reciprocal congruence. The common perpendiculars
to all non-paratactic crosses of the congruence will
generate a bundle, those to paratactic crosses, the reciprocal
%[File: 131.png]
congruence. Such a congruence will be generated by the
common perpendiculars to the paratactic lines of two pencils
which have different centres and planes, but a common line
and paratactic axes. In point space the line congruence will
be of order and class two. The canonical form will be%
\footnote{Apparently
nothing has ever been published concerning this type of
congruence. The theorems here given are taken from an unpublished section
of the author's\index{Author} dissertation, cit.}
%endfootnote
\begin{align*}
_lX_1&=a_1\, _rX_1, \\
_lX_2&=a_2\, _rX_2, \\
_lX_3&=0.
\end{align*}

If, in addition to (17), we require the first minors of $|_lY\, _lZ\, _lT|$
all to vanish, we shall have a bundle of paratactic crosses.
If, on the other hand, we have
\begin{equation*}
|_lY{}_lZ{}_lT|=|_rY{}_rZ{}_rT|=0,
\end{equation*}
without the vanishing of the first minors of either determinant,
we have $\infty^2$ crosses cutting a given cross orthogonally. The
equations of the congruence may be reduced to the canonical
form
\begin{align*}
\rho\, _lX_1=a, & \quad\sigma\, _rX_1=b,\\
\rho\, _lX_2=b, & \quad\sigma\, _rX_2=c,\tag{18} \\
\rho\, _lX_3=0, & \quad\sigma\, _rX_3=0.
\end{align*}
The cross $(1, 0, 0)$ $(0, 1, 0)$ will be singular, having $\infty^1$ determinations.

In general, if we have
\begin{equation*}
F( _lX_1\, _lX_2\, _lX_3)=0,
\quad\phi( _rX_1\, _rX _2\, _rX_3)=0,
\end{equation*}
the line-congruence can be assembled into $\infty^1$ surfaces with
left, and $\infty^1$ surfaces with right paratactic generators. Such
surfaces will have Gaussian curvature\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian} zero. We shall show
also in Chapter XVI that the lines of such a congruence are
normals to a series of surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero.

%[File: 132.png]



%CHAPTER XI
\mychap{XI}{THE CIRCLE AND THE SPHERE}
\mysect{Simplest form for the equation of a circle}
\index{Circle01@Circle|(}

The simplest curvilinear figures in non-euclidean geometry
are circles, and it is now time to study their properties.%
\footnote{For a very simple treatment of this subject by means of pure Geometry,
see Riccordi\index{Riccordi}, `I cercoli nella geometria non-euclidea,' \emph{Giornale di Matematica},
xviii, 1880. Riccordi's results had previously been reached analytically by
Battaglini\index{Battaglini}, `Sul rapporto anarmonico sezionale e tangenziale delle coniche,'
ibid., xii, 1874.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Definition}.~The locus of all points of a plane at a constant
distance from a given point which is not on the Absolute
is called a \emph{circle}. The given point shall be called the \emph{centre}
of the circle, its absolute polar, which will also turn out to
be its polar with regard to the circle, shall be called the \emph{axis}\index{Axis01@Axis of a circle}
of the circle. A line through the centre of the circle shall be
called a \emph{diameter}. Let the reader show that all points of
a circle are at constant distances from the axis, a distance
whose measure becomes infinite in the limiting euclidean case.

\mysect{Dual nature of the curve}
To get the equation of the circle whose centre is $(a)$ and
whose radius is $r$, i.e.\ this shall be the measure of the distance
of all points from the centre, we have
\[
  \frac{(ax)}{\sqrt{(aa)}\;\sqrt{(xx)} } = \cos\frac{r}{k},
\]
\[
  \cos^2\frac{r}{k}(aa)(xx)-(ax)^2 = 0.     \tag{1}
\]
It is evident that when $\cos^2 \dfrac{r}{k} \neq 0$, this curve has double
contact with the Absolute, the secant of contact being the
axis, and, conversely, every such curve of the second order
will be a circle. The absolute polar of a circle will, hence,
be another circle, so that the circle is self-dual:---

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem} 1. \emph{Definition}. The
locus of all points of a plane
at a constant distance from
a given point thereof is a
circle whose centre is the given
point.}
{\emph{Theorem}~1$'$. The envelope
of all lines of a plane which
make a constant angle with
a given line is a circle having
the given line as axis.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Note that a circle of radius $\dfrac{\pi k}{2}$ is a line, and that circle of
radius $0$ is two lines.

%[File: 133.png]
Restricting ourselves, for the moment, to the real domain
of the hyperbolic plane, we see that if the centre be ideal,
the axis will be actual, and the curve will appear in the actual
domain as the locus of points at a constant distance from the
axis, an actual line. In this case the circle is sometimes
called an \emph{equidistant curve}\index{Equidistant01@Equidistant curves}. If the centre be actual we shall
have what may be more properly called a \emph{proper} circle.
Notice that to a dweller in a small region of the hyperbolic
space, a proper circle would appear much as does a euclidean
circle to a euclidean dweller, while an equidistant curve would
appear like two parallel lines. These distinctions will,
naturally, disappear in the elliptic case; in the spherical, the
circle will have two centres, which are equivalent points.

If the point $(a)$ tend to approach the Absolute\index{Absolute} (analytically
speaking) the equation (1) will tend to approach an indeterminate
form. The limiting form for the curve will be
a conic having four-point contact with the Absolute. Such a
curve shall be called a \emph{horocycle}\index{Horocycle}, the point of contact being
called the centre, and the common tangent the axis. If $(u)$ be
the coordinates of the axis, we have
\[
(uu) = 0,
\]
and the equation of the horocycle takes the form
\[
(u_1{}^2 + u_2{}^2) (xx) + C (ux)^2 = 0.
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~2. A tangent to
a circle is perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to the
diameter through the point of
contact.}
{\emph{Theorem}~2$'$. A point on a
circle is orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points} to the
point where the tangent thereat
meets the axis.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

These simple theorems may be proved in a variety of ways.
For instance every circle will be transformed into itself by
a reflection in any diameter, hence the tangent where the
diameter meets the curve must be perpendicular to the diameter.
Or, again, if $\overline{AB} \equiv \overline{AC}$, a line from $A$ to one centre of gravity
of $B$, $C$ will be perpendicular to $BC$; then let $B$ and $C$ close
up on this centre of gravity. Or, lastly, the equation of the
tangent to the circle (1) at a point $(y)$ will be
\[
(xy) (aa)-N(ax) (ay) = 0.
\]

The diameter through $(y)$ will have the equation
\[
  \lvert xya \rvert = 0.
\]

If we indicate these two lines by $(u)$ and $(v)$, then
\[
(uv) = (aa)\, \lvert yay \rvert - N(ay)\, \lvert aya \rvert.
\]

Let the reader show that these theorems hold also in the
case of the horocycle.

%[File: 134.png]
\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~3. The locus of
the centres of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of pairs
of points of a circle whose
lines are concurrent on the
axis, is the point of concurrence,
and the diameter perpendicular
to these lines.}
{\emph{Theorem}~3$'$. The envelope
of the bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of the angles
of tangents to a circle from
points of a diameter, is this
diameter, and its absolute
