pole.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~4. If two tangents
to a circle (horocycle) make
a constant angle, the locus of
their point of intersection is
a concentric circle (horocycle).}
{\emph{Theorem}~4$'$. If two points of
a circle (horocycle) are at a
constant distance, the envelope
of their line is a coaxal circle
(horocycle).}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The element of arc of a circle of radius $(r)$ will be, by
Chapter IV (5),
\[
ds = k\sin\frac{r}{k}d\theta.
\]

The circumference of the circle is thus
\[
k\sin\frac{r}{k} \int_0^{2\pi} d\theta = 2\pi k\sin\frac{r}{k}.
\]

\mysect{Curvature of a circle}
\index{Curvature01@Curvature of a curve}Let the tangents at $P$ and $P'$ meet at $Q$, the centre of the
circle being $A$. Let $\Delta \phi$ be the angle between the tangents,
and let $P''$ be the point on the tangent at $P$ whose distance
from $P$ equals $PP'$, or, in the infinitesimal, equals $ds$. The
$\Delta PAP'$ and $\Delta P'PP''$ are isosceles, hence
\begin{flalign*}
&&\Delta\phi &= 2\measuredangle P'PP'',
\\
&&\tan \frac{\overline{P'P''}}{2k} &=
  \sin\frac{\overline{PP'}}{k} \tan\frac{\Delta\phi}{4}
\\
&&\limit\frac{\Delta\phi}{ds} &=
  \limit\frac{4\tan\dfrac{\overline{P'P''}}{2k}}
  {\overline{PP'}\sin\dfrac{\overline{PP'}}{k}} =
  \limit\frac{\overline{2P'P''}}{\overline{PP'^2}}.
\\
&\text{But} \hfill &
\tan\frac{\overline{PQ}}{k} &=
  \sin\frac{r}{k}\tan\tfrac{1}{2}d\theta =
  \frac{ds}{2k}, & \hfill &\phantom{\text{But}}
\\
&&\tan\frac{\overline{PQ}}{k} &=
  \tan\frac{\overline{AQ}}{k} \cos\tfrac{1}{2}(\pi-\Delta\phi)
 \text{\ by IV (6),}
\\
&&\limit \tan \frac{\overline{PQ}}{k} &=
  \tfrac{1}{2}\tan\frac{r}{k}\Delta\phi.\\
%[File: 135.png]
&\text{Hence}
&  \limit\frac{\Delta\phi}{ds}
&= \limit\frac{\overline{2P'P''}}{\overline{PP'^2}}
= \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r}{k}}.  \tag{2}
\end{flalign*}

We shall subsequently define this expression as the curvature of the
circle at the point $(P)$. We see that, as we should
expect, it is constant.

We shall next take up simple systems of circles. We leave
to the reader the task of making the slight modifications in
what follows necessary to adapt it to the case of spherical
geometry. In the general case two circles, neither of which
is a line, will intersect in four points, real, or imaginary, in
pairs. If two circles lie completely without one another they
will have four real common tangents, the absolute polars of
such circles will intersect in four real points. The difficulty
of visualization disappears in the hyperbolic case where we
take one at least of the circles as an equidistant curve. If we
identify the euclidean hemisphere, where opposite points of
the equator are considered identical, with the elliptic plane,
we see how two circles there also can intersect in four real
points. In the spherical case, by Chapter VIII, the Absolute\index{Absolute} is
the locus of all points which are identical with their equivalents.
A point will have one absolute polar, a line two
equivalent absolute poles. The absolute polar of a circle is
two equivalent circles, which are also the absolute polars of
the equivalent circle. Two real circles cannot intersect in
more than two real points.

\mysect{Radical axes, and centres of similitude}
Two circles which intersect in four points will have three
pairs of common secants. The problem of finding the common
secants of two conics will, in general, lead to an irreducible
equation of the third degree. When, however, the two conics
have double contact with a third, the equation is reducible,
and one pair of secants appears which intersect on the chords
of contact, and are harmonically separated by them.%
\footnote{This theorem is, of course, well known. Cf.\ Salmon\index{Salmon}, \emph{Conic Sections},
sixth edition. London, 1879, p.~242.}
%endfootnote
In the case of two circles these secants shall be called the \emph{radical
axes}\index{Axis02@--- radical of two circles}.\label{theywill}

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~5. If two circles
intersect in four points, two
common secants called radical
axes are concurrent\index{Concurrence} with the
axes\index{Axis01@Axis of a circle} of the circles and
harmonically
%[File: 136.png]
separated by them,
They are perpendicular to one
another and to the line of centres. The centres of gravity
of the intersections of the
circles with a radical axis are
the intersections with the
other radical axis and with
the line of centres.}
{\emph{Theorem}~5$'$. If two circles
have four common tangents,
two intersections of these,
called centres of similitude\index{Centre05@--- of similitude}\index{Similitude, centres of}, lie
on the line\index{Collinearity} of centres, are
%[File: 136.png bis]
harmonically separated by the
centres and are mutually
orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}. The bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of
angles of the tangents at a
centre of similitude are the
line of centres and the line to
the other centre of similitude\index{Centre05@--- of similitude}\index{Similitude, centres of}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

If the equations of the two circles be
\[
  \cos^2 \frac{r_1}{k}(aa)(xx) - (ax)^2 = 0,\quad
  \cos^2 \frac{r_2}{k}(bb)(xx) - (bx)^2 = 0,
\]
the equations of the radical axes will be
\begin{multline*}
  \left( \cos\frac{r_2}{k}\sqrt{(bb)}(ax)
       + \cos\frac{r_1}{k}\sqrt{(aa)}(bx) \right)
\\
  \left( \cos\frac{r_2}{k}\sqrt{(bb)}(ax)
       - \cos\frac{r_1}{k}\sqrt{(aa)}(bx) \right) = 0. \tag{3}
\end{multline*}
The last factor equated to zero will give
\[
  \frac{\dfrac{(ax)}{\sqrt{(aa)}\;\sqrt{(xx)}} }
       {\cos \dfrac{r_1}{k}                  }
\\
= \frac{\dfrac{(bx)}{\sqrt{(bb)}\;\sqrt{(xx)}} }
       {\cos \dfrac{r_2}{k}                  },
\]
and the two sides of this equation will, by Ch.~IV~(4), be the
cosines of the $k$th parts of the distances from $(x)$ to the points
of contact of tangents, thence to the two circles.

\medskip

\mysect{Circles through two points, or tangent to two lines}
\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~6. If a set of circles
through two points have the
line of these points as a radical
axis\index{Axis02@--- radical of two circles}, the points of contact of
tangents to all of them from
a point of the line lie on a
circle whose centre\index{Centre01@Centre of a circle} is this
point.}
{\emph{Theorem}~6$'$. If a set of circles
tangent to two given lines
have the intersection of the
lines as a centre of similitude,
the envelope of tangents to
them at the points where they
meet a line through this
centre of similitude will be
a circle with this line as axis\index{Axis01@Axis of a circle}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Consider the assemblage of all circles through two given
points. If the line connecting the two points be a radical
axis for two of these circles it will be perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to their
line of centres at one centre of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of the two points, and
in every case a perpendicular from the centre of a circle on
%[File: 137.png]
a secant will meet it at a centre of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of the two points
of the circle on that line. We thus see---

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~7. The assemblage
of all circles through two
common points will fall into
two families according as the
perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} from the centre
on the line of these points
passes through the one or the
other of their centres of
gravity. Two circles of the
same family, and they only, will
have the line as a radical axis\index{Axis02@--- radical of two circles}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~7$'$. The assemblage
of all circles tangent to two
lines will fall into two families
according as the centres\index{Centre01@Centre of a circle} lie on
the one or the other bisector\index{Bisector of an angle}
of the angles of the lines.
Two circles of the same
family, and they only, will
have the intersection of the
lines as a centre of similitude\index{Centre05@--- of similitude}\index{Similitude, centres of}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let us now take a third point, and consider the circles that
pass through all three.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~8. Four circles will
pass through three given
points. Each line connecting
two of the given points will
be a radical axis for two pairs
of circles.}
{\emph{Theorem}~8$'$. Four circles will
touch three given lines. Each
intersection of two lines will
be a centre of similitude for
two pairs of circles.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~9. The radical axes
of three circles pass by threes
through four points.}
{\emph{Theorem}~9$'$. The centres of
similitude of three circles lie
by threes on four lines.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Of course when two circles touch one another, their common
tangent replaces one radical axis, and the point of contact one
centre of similitude. Two circles will have double contact
when, and only when, they are concentric. We get at once
from (6) and (9)

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~10. Four circles
may be constructed to cut
each of three circles at right
angles twice.}
{\emph{Theorem}~10$'$. Four circles
may be constructed so that
the points of contact of tangents
common to them and to
each of three given circles
form two pairs of orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}
points.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

It is here assumed that no two of the given circles are concentric.
There is no reason to expect that because two circles
intersect at right angles in two points they will in the other
two. Let the circles be
\[
  \cos^2 \frac{r_1}{k} (aa) (xx)-(ax)^2 = 0, \quad
  \cos^2 \frac{r_2}{k} (bb) (xx)-(bx)^2 = 0.
\]

%[File: 138.png]
Let $(y)$ be a point of intersection; the lines thence to the
centres are
\[
  \lvert xya \rvert = 0, \quad \lvert xyb \rvert = 0.
\]

The cosine of the angle formed by them will be
\[
\begin{split}
\cos\theta &=
  \frac{\begin{vmatrix} (yy) & (ay) \\ (by) & (ab) \end{vmatrix} }
       {\sqrt{(yy)(aa)-(ay)^2}\; \sqrt{(yy)(bb)-(by)^2}}
\\
&=\frac{(ab) - \cos\dfrac{r_1}{k} \cos\dfrac{r_2}{k}
                \sqrt{(aa)}\; \sqrt{(bb)}            }
       {\sin\dfrac{r_1}{k} \sin\dfrac{r_2}{k}
         \sqrt{(aa)}\; \sqrt{(bb)}                   }.
\end{split}
\tag{4}
\]

This gives two values for the angle which will be equal when,
and only when
\[
(ab) = 0.
\]

The condition of contact will be
\begin{equation*}
\tag{5}
  \cos\theta = \pm 1, \quad
  \cos \left( \frac{r_1}{k} \pm \frac{r_2}{k} \right) =
  \frac{(ab)}{\sqrt{(aa)}\; \sqrt{(bb)}};
\end{equation*}
and of orthogonal intersection
\begin{equation*}
\tag{6}
\cos\frac{r_1}{k} \cos\frac{r_2}{k} = \frac{(ab)}{\sqrt{(aa)}\;\sqrt{(bb)}},
\end{equation*}
these last two facts being, also, geometrically evident. We
see that two circles cannot have four rectangular intersections,
for if
\begin{equation*}
\tag{7}
(ab) = 0, \quad \cos \frac{r_2}{k} = 0,
\end{equation*}
the circle is a line.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~11. The necessary
and sufficient condition that
two circles should cut at the
same angle at all points is
that their centres\index{Centre01@Centre of a circle} should be
mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~11$'$. The necessary
and sufficient condition that
two circles should determine
by their points of contact,
congruent distances on all four
common tangents, is that their
axes should be mutually perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Notice that these two conditions are really identical.
\index{Circle01@Circle|)}

%[File: 139.png]
\mysect{Spheres}\index{Sphere|(}
We shall define as a sphere that surface which is the locus
of all points of space at congruent distances from a point not
on the Absolute.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~12. A sphere is
the locus of all points at a
constant distance from a given
point not on the Absolute\index{Absolute}.
It is, when not a plane, a
quadric with conical contact
with the Absolute.}
{\emph{Theorem}~12$'$. A sphere is
the envelope of planes meeting
at a constant angle a plane
which is not tangent to the
Absolute. It is, when not a
point, a quadric with conical
contact with the Absolute.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Note that a plane and point are special cases of the sphere.

The fixed point shall be called the centre, the plane of conical
contact the \emph{axial plane}\index{Axial plane of sphere} of the sphere. A line connecting any
point with the centre of a sphere is perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to the polar
plane of the point, a tangent plane is perpendicular to the line
from the point of contact to the centre, to the diameter through
the point of contact let us say.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~13. Two spheres
will intersect in two circles
whose planes are perpendicular to the line of centres
and to one another, and are
harmonically separated by the
axial planes.}
{\emph{Theorem}~13$'$. The common
tangent planes to two spheres
envelop two cones of revolution whose vertices are
mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points} and
harmonically separated by
the centres.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~14. Three spheres
not containing a common
circle will meet in three pairs
of circles whose planes are
collinear by threes in four
lines.}
{\emph{Theorem}~14$'$. Three spheres
not tangent to a cone of revolution have three such
pairs of common tangent cones
whose vertices are collinear in
threes on four lines.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~15. Four spheres
whose centres are not coplanar\index{Coplanarity} intersect in twelve
circles whose planes pass by
sixes through eight points
which, with the centres of the
spheres, form a desmic configuration.}
{\emph{Theorem}~15$'$. Four spheres
whose axial planes are not
concurrent are enveloped in
pairs by twelve cones of revolution whose vertices lie
by sixes in eight planes which,
with the axial planes, determine a desmic configuration\index{Desmic configuration}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~16. The necessary
and sufficient condition that
two spheres should cut at the
same angle along their two
%[File: 140.png]
circles is that their centres
should be mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~16$'$. The necessary
and sufficient condition that
two spheres should, by their
contact, determine congruent
%[File: 140.png bis]
distances on the generators of
the two circumscribed cones,
is that their axial planes
should be mutually perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Poincar\'e's sphere to sphere transformation from euclidean to non-euclid\-ean space}
We shall terminate this chapter by giving an unusually
elegant transformation from euclidean to non-euclidean space.%
\footnote{This transformation seems to have been first given in the second edition
of \emph{Wissenschaft und Hypothese}, by Poincar\'e\index{Poincar\'e}, translated by F. and L. Lindemann\index{Lindemann},
Leipzig, 1906, p.~258. This is fruitfully used in the dissertation of M\"unich\index{Munich@M\"unich},
`Nicht-euklidische Cykliden,' Munich, 1906. We have adapted the notation
to conform to our own usage.}
%endfootnote
Let us assume that we have a euclidean space where a point
has the homogeneous coordinates $x$, $y$, $z$, $t$ and a hyperbolic
space for which $k^2=-1$, a point being given by our usual $(\dot{x})$
coordinates. Let us then write
\begin{equation*}
  \rho x = \dot{x}_1,\quad \rho y=\dot{x}_2,\quad
  \rho z = \sqrt{\dot{x}_0{}^2 - \dot{x}_1{}^2
                - \dot{x}_2{}^2 - \dot{x}_3{}^2 },\quad
  \rho t = \dot{x}_0-\dot{x}_3.
\tag{8}
\end{equation*}

To each point of hyperbolic space will correspond two
points of euclidean space. Let us choose that for which the
real part of
$\sqrt{\dot{x}_0{}^2 - \dot{x}_1{}^2 - \dot{x}_2{}^2 - \dot{x}_3{}^2}$
is greater than zero. When
the real part vanishes, we may, by adjoining to our domain of
rationality a square root of minus one, distinguish between the
imaginary roots, and so choose one in particular. We may
thus say that to every point of hyperbolic space, not on the
Absolute, will correspond a point of euclidean space above
the plane $z = 0$, and to each points of the Absolute will
correspond points of this plane. The transformation is real,
so that real and actual points will correspond to real ones.
Conversely, we get from (8)
\begin{multline*}
  \sigma \dot{x}_0 = x^2 + y^2 + z^2 + t^2,\quad
  \sigma \dot{x}_1 = 2xt,\quad \sigma \dot{x}_2 = 2yt,
\\
  \sigma \dot{x}_3 = x^2 + y^2 + z^2 - t^2,
\tag{9}
\end{multline*}
and to each point of euclidean space, above, or on the $z$ plane,
will correspond a point of hyperbolic space, not on, or on the
Absolute.

Suppose that we have a euclidean sphere of centre $(a, b, c, d)$
and radius $r$. If we write for short
\[
(a^2 + b^2 + c^2 - d^2r^2) = p^2,
\]
the equation of this sphere may be written
\begin{align*}
  &(dx-at)^2 + (dy-bt)^2 + (dz-ct)^2 = d^2 r^2 t^2,
\\
  &d^2(x^2+y^2+z^2) - 2dt(ax+by+cz) + p^2 t^2 = 0.
\tag{10}
\end{align*}

%[File: 141.png]
Transforming we get, after splitting off a factor
$\dot{x}_3 - \dot{x}_0$ which
corresponds to the euclidean plane at infinity,
\[
d^2 (\dot{x}_0 + \dot{x}_3) - 2d (a\dot{x}_1 + b\dot{x}_2
  + c\sqrt{\dot{x}_0{}^2 - \dot{x}_1{}^2 - \dot{x}_2{}^2 - \dot{x}_3{}^2})
  + p^2(\dot{x}_0 - \dot{x}_3) = 0,
\]
\begin{multline*}
\tag{11}
[ (d^2+p^2)\dot{x}_0 - 2ad\dot{x}_1 - 2bd\dot{x}_2
+ (d^2-p^2)\dot{x}_3 ]^2   \\
= 4c^2d^2 (\dot{x}_0{}^2 - \dot{x}_1{}^2 - \dot{x}_2{}^2 - \dot{x}_3{}^2).
\end{multline*}

This is a sphere of hyperbolic space whose centre is
\[
(d^2+p^2,\; 2ad,\; 2bd,\; p^2-d^2),
\]
and whose radius $r_1$ is given by
\[
\cosh r_1 = \frac{c}{\sqrt{p^2 - a^2 - b^2}}.
\]

Conversely, if we have the hyperbolic sphere
\begin{multline*}
\tag{12}
( \dot{a}_0\dot{x}_0 - \dot{a}_1\dot{x}_1
- \dot{a}_2\dot{x}_2 - \dot{a}_3 \dot{x}_3 )^2   \\
= \cosh^2 r_1(\dot{a}_0{}^2 - \dot{a}_1{}^2 - \dot{a}_2{}^2 - \dot{a}_3{}^2)
             (\dot{x}_0{}^2 - \dot{x}_1{}^2 - \dot{x}_2{}^2 - \dot{x}_3{}^2),
\end{multline*}
we get from (9)
\begin{multline*}
\tag{13}
[(\dot{a}_0 - \dot{a}_3) (x^2 + y^2 + z^2)
- 2\dot{a}_1 xt - 2\dot{a}_2yt + (\dot{a}_0 + \dot{a}_3)t^2]   \\
= \pm 2\cosh r_1
  \sqrt{\dot{a}_0{}^2 - \dot{a}_1{}^2 - \dot{a}_2{}^2 - \dot{a}_3{}^2} zt.
\end{multline*}

We have here two spheres which differ merely in the $z$ coordinate
of their centre, i.e.\ two spheres which are reflections
of one another in the $z$ plane. If the hyperbolic sphere were
real and actual, one of the euclidean spheres would lie wholly
above the $z$ plane, and the other wholly below it. We may
say that (leaving aside special cases) a hyperbolic sphere will
correspond to so much of a euclidean sphere as is above or
in the $z$ plane, and to the reflection in the $z$ plane of so much
of the sphere as is below it.

A euclidean sphere for which $c = 0$, that is, one whose
centre is in the $z$ plane will correspond to a plane in hyperbolic
space, a hyperbolic sphere for which
\[
\dot{a}_0 - \dot{a}_3 = 0,
\]
that is, one whose centre is in the plane which corresponds
to the euclidean plane at infinity, will correspond to a plane
in euclidean space. A euclidean circle perpendicular to the
$z$ plane will correspond to a hyperbolic line, a hyperbolic circle
which is perpendicular to the plane $\dot{a}_0-\dot{a}_3 = 0$, will correspond
to a euclidean line.

We may go a step further in this direction. Suppose that
we have two euclidean spheres given by an equation of the
%[File: 142.png]
type (13), and the condition that they shall be mutually orthogonal is that
\begin{multline*}
  -\dot{a}_0\dot{a}_0{}' + \dot{a}_1\dot{a}_1{}' + \dot{a}_2\dot{a}_2{}'
\\
  \pm \cosh r_1 \cosh r_1{}'
  \sqrt{\dot{a}_0{}^2 - \dot{a}_1{}^2 - \dot{a}_2{}^2 - \dot{a}_3{}^2}\;
  \sqrt{\dot{a}_0{}^{'2} - \dot{a}_1{}^{'2} - \dot{a}_2{}^{'2} - \dot{a}_3{}^{'2}}
\\
+ \dot{a}_3\dot{a}'_3 = 0,
\end{multline*}
\[
  \cosh r_1 \cosh r_1{}'
= \pm \frac{-\dot{a}_0\dot{a}_0{}' + \dot{a}_1\dot{a}_1{}'
           + \dot{a}_2\dot{a}_2{}' + \dot{a}_3\dot{a}'_3 }
           {\sqrt{-\dot{a}_0{}^2 + \dot{a}_1{}^2
                  + \dot{a}_2{}^2 + \dot{a}_3{}^2  }\;
             \sqrt{-\dot{a}_0{}^{'2} + \dot{a}_1{}^{'2}
                  + \dot{a}_2{}^{'2} + \dot{a}_3{}^{'2}}       }.
\]

But this gives immediately that the corresponding hyperbolic
spheres are also mutually orthogonal, and conversely. We
thus have a correspondence of orthogonal spheres to orthogonal
spheres. We see next that the lines of curvature of any
surface will go into any lines of curvature of the corresponding
surface, and hence the Darboux-Dupin\index{Darboux}\index{Dupin} theorem must hold in
hyperbolic space, namely, in any triply orthogonal system
of surfaces, the intersections are lines of curvature.

Were we willing to sacrifice the real domain, we might in
a similar manner establish a correspondence between spheres
of euclidean and of elliptic space.\index{Sphere|)}

%[File: 143.png]


%CHAPTER XII
\mychap{XII}{CONIC SECTIONS}
\mysect{Classification of conics}
\index{Conic|(}

The study of the metrical properties of conics in the non-euclidean
plane, is, in the last analysis, nothing more nor less
than a study of the invariants and covariants of two conics.
We shall not, however, go into general questions of invariant
theory here, but rather try to pick out those metrical properties
of non-euclidean conics which bear the closest analogy
to the corresponding euclidean properties.%
\footnote{The treatment of conics in the present chapter is in close accord with
three articles by D'Ovidio\index{Ovidio@d'Ovidio}, `Le propriet\`a focali delle coniche,' `Sulle coniche
confocali,' and `Teoremi sulle coniche', all in the \emph{Atti della R. Accademia delle
Scienze di Torino}, vol.~xxvi, 1891. These articles suffer from the curious
blemish, not uncommon in Italian mathematical publications, that the
theorems are not given in distinctive type. See also Story\index{Story}, `On the
non-euclidean Properties of Conics,' \emph{American Journal of Mathematics}, vol.~v, 1882;
Killing\index{Killing}, `Die nicht-euklidische Geometrie in analytischer Behandlung,'
Leipzig, 1885, and Liebmann\index{Liebmann}, `Nicht-euklidische Geometrie,' in the \emph{Sammlung
Schubert}, xlix, Leipzig, 1904.}
%endfootnote

First of all, let us classify our conics under the real congruent group;
that is, in relation to their intersections with
the Absolute\index{Absolute}. This may be done analytically by means of
Weierstrass's\index{Weierstrass} elementary divisors, but the geometric question
is so easy that we give the results merely. We shall begin
with the real conics in the actual domain of hyperbolic space.

(1) Convex hyperbolas\index{Hyperbola}. Four real absolute points, no real
absolute tangents.

(2) Concave hyperbolas. Four real absolute points, four
real absolute tangents.

(3) Semi-hyperbolas\index{Semi-hyperbola}. Two real and two imaginary absolute
points and tangents.

(4) Ellipses\index{Ellipse}. Four imaginary absolute points and tangents.

(5) Concave hyperbolic parabolas\index{Parabola}. Two coincident, and
two real and distinct absolute points and tangents.

(6) Convex hyperbolic parabolas. Two coincident, and two
real and distinct absolute points. Two coincident, and two
conjugate imaginary absolute tangents.

(7) Elliptic parabolas. Two coincident, and two conjugate
imaginary absolute points and tangents.

(8) Osculating parabolas. Three real coincident, and one
real distinct absolute point, and the same for absolute tangents.

%[File: 144.png]
(9) Equidistant curves\index{Equidistant01@Equidistant curves}.

(10) Proper circles\index{Circle01@Circle}.

(11) Horocycles\index{Horocycle}.

In the real elliptic, or spherical, plane, we shall have
merely---

(1) Ellipses\index{Ellipse};

(2) Circles.

\mysect{Equations of central conic and Absolute}\index{Central conic|(}
In what follows we shall limit ourselves to \emph{central conics},
i.e.\ to those which cut the Absolute\index{Absolute} in four distinct points.
A real central conic in the actual domain of the hyperbolic
plane will have a common self-conjugate triangle with the
Absolute which is real, except in the case of the semi-hyperbola.
In the elliptic case it will surely be real. Taking this
as the coordinate triangle we may write the equation of the
Absolute in typical form, while that of the conic is

\[
\tag{1}
\sum_i^{0..2}c_ix_i{}^2=0.
\]

We assume that no two of our $c$'s are equal, and that none
of them are equal to zero.

\mysect{Centres, axes, foci, focal lines, directrices, and director points}
Our plane being $x_3 = 0$, we shall use the letters $h$, $k$, $l$ as
a circular permutation of the numbers $0$, $1$, $2$, and define the
vertices of the common self-conjugate triangles as \emph{centres}\index{Centre02@--- of a conic} of
the conic, while its sides are called the \emph{axes}\index{Axis03@--- of a conic}. Be it noticed
that in speaking of \emph{triangle} in this sense we are using the
terminology of projective geometry where a triangle is a figure
of three coplanar, but not concurrent lines, and not the exact
definition of Chapter I, which is meaningless except in a restricted
domain. There will, however, arise no confusion
from this.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~1. Each centre of
a central conic is a centre
of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} for every pair of
points of the conic collinear
therewith.}
{\emph{Theorem}~1$'$. Each axis of
a central conic is a bisector\index{Bisector of an angle}
of an angle of each pair of
tangents to the conic concurrent
thereon.}\par\medskip
%end twincol


The three pairs of lines which connect the pairs of intersections
of a central conic with the Absolute shall be called
its pairs of \emph{focal lines}. The three pairs of intersections
of its absolute tangents shall be called its pairs of \emph{foci}.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~2. Conjugate points
of a focal line of a conic are
mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~2$'$. Conjugate lines
through a focus of a conic are
mutually perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}.}
%end twincol


%[File: 145.png]

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~3. Two focal lines\index{Focal03@--- lines}
of a central conic pass through
each vertex, and are perpendicular to the opposite axis.}
{\emph{Theorem}~3$'$. Two foci\index{Foci} of a
central quadric lie on each
axis, and are orthogonal to
the opposite centre.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The coordinates of the focal lines $f_h$, $f_h{}'$, through the centre
$u_h = 0$, will be
\[
  u_h : u_k : u_l
= 0 : \sqrt{c_h-c_k} : \pm \sqrt{c_l-c_h}.   \tag{2}
\]

The coordinates of the foci $F_h$, $F_h{}'$ on the opposite axis
will be
\[
  x_h : x_k : x_l
= 0 : \sqrt{c_l(c_h-c_k)} : \pm \sqrt{c_k(c_l-c_h)}. \tag{3}
\]

\mysect{Relations connecting distances of a point from foci, directrices, \&c., and their duals}
The polars of the foci with regard to the conic shall be called
\emph{directrices}\index{Director points and directrices},
the poles of the focal lines its \emph{director points}.
A directrix $d_h$ perpendicular to the axis $x_h$ will have the
equation
\[
  \sqrt{c_k(c_h-c_k)}x_k + \sqrt{c_l(c_l-c_h)}x_l = 0. \tag{4}
\]

Let $(x)$ be a point of the conic. Eliminating $x_h$ by means
of (1) we get
\[
  (xx) = \frac{(c_h-c_k)}{c_h}x_k{}^2 - \frac{(c_l-c_h)}{c_h}x_l{}^2.
\]

We then have
\begin{align*}
  \cos\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k}
&= \frac{\sqrt{c_l(c_h-c_k)}x_k + \sqrt{c_k(c_l-c_h)}x_l        }
        {\sqrt{(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - (c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2}\;\sqrt{(c_l-c_k)} }. \tag{5}
\\
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k}
&= \frac{\sqrt{c_k(c_h-c_k)}x_k + \sqrt{c_l(c_l-c_h)}x_l        }
        {\sqrt{(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - (c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2}\;\sqrt{(c_k-c_l)} }. \tag{6}
\end{align*}

If $d_h$ be the corresponding directrix
\[
  \sin\frac{\overline{Pd_h}}{k}
= \frac{\sqrt{c_k(c_h-c_k)}x_k + \sqrt{c_l(c_l-c_h)}x_l            }
       {\sqrt{(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - (c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2}\;
         \sqrt{\dfrac{c_k}{c_h}(c_h-c_k) + \dfrac{c_l}{c_h}(c_l-c_h)} },
\tag{7}
\]
the signs of the radicals in the numerators of the two expressions being the same
\begin{align*}
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{PF_h}}{k}}
       {\sin\dfrac{\overline{Pd_h}}{k}}
&= \frac{\sqrt{c_k(c_h-c_k) + c_l(c_l-c_h)} }
        {\sqrt{c_h(c_k-c_l)}                }
\\
&= \sqrt{\frac{c_h - (c_k+c_l)}{c_k}}.  \tag{8}
\end{align*}

%[File: 146.png]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~4. The ratio\index{Ratios, constant connected with conics} of the
sines of the $k$th parts of the
distances from a point of a
central conic to a focus\index{Foci} and
to the corresponding directrix\index{Director points and directrices}
is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~4$'$. The ratio of
the sines of the angles which
a tangent to a central conic
makes with a focal line\index{Focal03@--- lines} and
the absolute polar of the
corresponding director point
is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol


\[
  \cos\frac{\overline{F_hF_h{}'}}{k}
= \frac{c_l(c_h-c_k) - c_k(c_l-c_h)}
       {c_l(c_h-c_k) + c_k(c_l-c_h)},\quad
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\overline{F_hF_h{}'}}{k}
= \frac{c_k(c_l-c_h)}{c_l(c_h-c_k)},
\]
\begin{multline*}
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\overline{F_hF_h{}'}}{k}
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\overline{F_kF_k{}'}}{k}
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\overline{F_lF_l{}'}}{k}
\\
= \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2} \measuredangle f_h f_h{}'
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2} \measuredangle f_k f_k{}'
  \tan^2\tfrac{1}{2} \measuredangle f_l f_l{}' = 1.
\tag{9}
\end{multline*}\ungap
\begin{multline*}
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k}
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k}
= \frac{c_k(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - c_l(c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2}
       {\bigl[(c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2 - (c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2\bigr](c_k-c_l)}
\\
= \frac{\smallsum c_h{}^2 x_h{}^2}{c_h(c_k-c_l)(xx)}, \phantom{(9)}
\end{multline*}\ungap
\begin{multline*}
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k}\ :
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_k}}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{PF_k{}'}}{k}\ :
  \sin\frac{\overline{PF_l}}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{PF_l{}'}}{k}
\\
= \frac{1}{c_h(c_k-c_l)}\ :
  \frac{1}{c_k(c_l-c_h)}\ :
  \frac{1}{c_l(c_h-c_k)}.
\tag{10}
\end{multline*}
\[
  \csc\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k} \csc\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k}
+ \csc\frac{\overline{PF_k}}{k} \csc\frac{\overline{PF_k{}'}}{k}
+ \csc\frac{\overline{PF_l}}{k} \csc\frac{\overline{PF_l{}'}}{k} = 0.
\tag{11}
\]
\[
 \cos\frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k} \cos\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k}
= \frac{c_l(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - c_k(c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2 }
       {\bigl[(c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2 - (c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2\bigr](c_l-c_k) },
\]
\label{xhxl}
\[
\cos\Bigl[ \frac{\overline{PF_h}}{k}
         \pm\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k} \Bigr]
= \frac{c_k+c_l}{c_k-c_l}.
\tag{12}
\]
\[
 \tan\tfrac{1}{2}\Bigl[ \frac{\overline{PF_h }}{k}
                     \pm\frac{\overline{PF_h{}'}}{k} \Bigr]
 \tan\tfrac{1}{2}\Bigl[ \frac{\overline{PF_k }}{k}
                     \pm\frac{\overline{PF_k{}'}}{k} \Bigr]
 \tan\tfrac{1}{2}\Bigl[ \frac{\overline{PF_l }}{k}
                     \pm\frac{\overline{PF_l{}'}}{k} \Bigr]
= \pm 1.
\tag{13}
\]

With regard to the ambiguity of signs: the upper sign in
(12) will go with the upper sign throughout in (13), and so
for the lower sign. It is also geometrically evident that
in the case of an ellipse we must take the upper, and in
the case of a hyperbola the lower sign (when in the real
domain).

%[File: 147.png]
\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~5. The sum of the
distances\index{Sum04@Sum of distances connected with a conic} from real points of
an ellipse and the difference
of the distances from real
points of a hyperbola or semi-hyperbola
to two real foci on
the same axis is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~5$'$. The sum of the
angles which the real tangents
to an ellipse\index{Ellipse} or convex hyperbola\index{Hyperbola},
or the difference of the
angles which the real tangents
to a concave hyperbola or a
semi-hyperbola make with
two real focal lines through
a centre is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Reverting to our point $(x)$ we see

\[
\sin\frac{\overline{Pf_h}}{k} =
  \frac{\sqrt{c_h-c_k}x_k + \sqrt{c_l-c_h}x_l}
  {\sqrt{(c_l-c_h)x_l{}^2 - (c_h-c_k)x_k{}^2}\;
  \sqrt{\dfrac{-(c_k-c_l)}{c_h}}},
\]
\[
\sin\frac{\overline{Pf_h}}{k}
  \sin\frac{\overline{Pf_h{}'}}{k} = \pm \frac{c_h}{c_k-c_l}.
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~6. The product\index{Products connected with a conic} of
the sines of the $k$th parts
of the distances from a point
of a central conic to two focal
lines through the same centre
is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~6$'$. The product of
the sines of the $k$th parts
of the distances to a tangent
from two foci of a central
conic on the same axis is
constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let us now recall Desargues'\index{Desargues} theorem, whereby a transversal
meets the conics of a pencil in pairs of points of an involution.
This will apply to a central conic, the Absolute\index{Absolute}, and the pairs
of focal lines\index{Focal03@--- lines}. A dual theorem will of course hold for a central
conic, the Absolute, and the pairs of foci\index{Foci}.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~7. The intersections
of a line with a central
conic, and with its pairs of
corresponding focal lines, all
have the same centres of
gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~7$'$. The tangents
from a point to a central
conic, and the pairs of lines
thence to its pairs of corresponding
foci, form angles
with the same two bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~8. The polar of a
point with regard to a central
conic passes through one
centre of gravity of the intersections
of each focal line with
the tangents from the point to
the conic.}
{\emph{Theorem}~8$'$. The pole of a
line with regard to a central
conic lies on one bisector of
the angle determined at each
focus by the lines thence to
the intersections of the given
line with the conic.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

%[File: 148.png]
A variable point of a conic will determine projective pencils
at any two fixed points thereof, and these will meet any line
in projective ranges, hence

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~9. If a variable point of a central conic be connected with
two fixed points thereof, the distance which these lines cut on any focal line\index{Focal03@--- lines}
is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~9$'$. If a variable tangent to a central conic be brought to intersect two fixed
tangents thereof, the angle of the lines from a chosen focus\index{Foci}
to the two intersections is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Recalling the properties of the eleven-point conic\index{Conic, eleven-point or line} of two
given conics and a line:

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~10. If a line and a central conic be given, the two
mutually conjugate and orthogonal points of the line, the points of the
focal lines orthogonal to their intersections with the line, and the
three centres lie on a conic.}
{\emph{Theorem}~10$'$. If a point and a central conic be given,
the two lines through the point which are mutually conjugate and
perpendicular, the perpendiculars on the line from the foci,
and the three axes all touch a conic.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

It is a well-known theorem that the locus of points, whence
tangents to two conics form a harmonic set, is a conic passing
through the points of contact with the common tangents.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~11. The locus of points whence tangents to a central conic are
mutually perpendicular is a conic meeting the given conic where it meets its
directrices\index{Director points and directrices}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~11$'$. The envelope of lines which meet a central conic in pairs of mutually
orthogonal points is a conic touching the tangents to the given circle from its director points.}

It is clear that neither of these conics will, in general, be
a circle, as in the euclidean case. If the mutually perpendicular
tangents from the point $(y)$ be

\[
(ux) = 0,\quad (vx) = 0.\\
\]
\[
\sum_i^{0..2} \frac{u_i{}^2}{c_i} = \sum_i^{0..2} \frac{v_i{}^2}{c_i} = \sum_i^{0..2} u_i v_i=0,\\
\]
\[
\sum_i^{0..2} \frac{u_i{}^2}{c_i} (vv) + \sum_i^{0..2} \frac{v_i{}^2}{c_i} (uu)-2\sum_i^{0..2} \frac{u_i v_i}{c_i} (uv)=0,
\]
%[File: 149.png]
\[
  \sum_h^{0..2} c_h(c_k+c_l)y_i{}^2 = 0.   \tag{14}
\]
Let the reader show that the equation of the other conic
will be
\[
  \sum_h^{0..2} (c_k+c_l)u_i{}^2 = 0.
\]
We may extend the usual euclidean proof to the first of the
following theorems---

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~12. The locus of
the reflection of a real focus
of an ellipse in a variable
tangent, is a circle whose
centre is the corresponding
focus.}
{\emph{Theorem}~12$'$. The envelope
of the reflection in a variable
point of an ellipse, of a real
focal line, is a circle whose
axis is the corresponding focal
line.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Conjugate and mutually perpendicular lines through a centre}
Let $(y)$ be the coordinates of a point $P$ of our conic. The
equation of a line through the centre\index{Centre02@--- of a conic} $O_h$ conjugate to the line
$O_hP$ will be
\[
  c_k y_k x_k + c_l y_l x_l = 0.
\]
This will meet the conic in two points $P'$ having the coordinates
\[
  x_h  : x_k  : x_l
= \pm\sqrt{c_k c_l}y_h  : c_l y_l  : - c_k y_k,
\]
\[
  \tan^2 \frac{\overline{OP }}{k}
+ \tan^2 \frac{\overline{OP'}}{k}
= \frac{-c_h(c_k+c_l)}{c_k c_l}   \tag{15}
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~13. The sum\index{Sum04@Sum of distances connected with a conic} of
the squares of the tangents
of the $k$th parts of the distances from a centre of a
central conic to any pair of
intersections with two conjugate lines through this
centre is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~13$'$. The sum of
the squares of the tangents
of the angles which an axis of
a central conic makes with a
pair of tangents to the curve
from two conjugate points of
this axis is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

We shall call two such diameters\index{Diameters01@Diameters of conic} as $O_hP$, $O_hP'$ \emph{conjugate
diameters}\index{Conjugate01@Conjugate diameters of a conic}.
\begin{align*}
  \sin \measuredangle PO_hP'
&= \frac{(c_k y_k{}^2 + c_l y_l{}^2)}
        {\sqrt{y_k{}^2 + y_l{}^2}\;\sqrt{c_k{}^2y_k{}^2 + c_l{}^2y_l{}^2}}
\\
&= \frac{-c_h y_h{}^2 }
        {\sqrt{y_k{}^2 + y_l{}^2}\;\sqrt{c_k{}^2y_k{}^2 + c_l{}^2y_l{}^2}} ;
\end{align*}
\[
  \tan \frac{\overline{O_hP }}{k}
  \tan \frac{\overline{O_hP'}}{k}
  \sin \measuredangle PO_hP'
= \pm \frac{c_h}{\sqrt{c_k c_l}}.
\]

%[File: 150.png]
\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~14. The product
of the tangents of the $k$th
parts of the distances from a
centre\index{Centre02@--- of a conic} of a central conic to
two intersections with a pair
of conjugate diameters\index{Diameters01@Diameters of conic} through
that centre, multiplied by the
sine of the angle of these
diameters is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~14$'$. The product\index{Products connected with a conic}
of the tangents of the angles
which an axis of a central
conic makes with two tangents
to it from a pair of conjugate
points of this axis, multiplied
by the sine of the $k$th part of
the distance of these points is
constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The equation of a line through the centre $O_h$ perpendicular
to $O_hP$ will be
\[
  y_k x_k + y_l x_l = 0.
\]
This will meet the conic in points $P''$ having coordinates
\begin{align*}
  x_h : x_k : x_l
&=\pm\sqrt{\frac{-(c_l y_k{}^2 + c_k y_l{}^2)}{c_h}} : y_l : -y_k,
\\
  \cos\frac{\overline{OP''}}{k}
&= \frac{\sqrt{-(c_l y_k{}^2 + c_k y_l{}^2)       } }
        {\sqrt{(c_h-c_l)y_k{}^2 + (c_h-c_k)y_l{}^2} },
\\
  \ctn^2 \frac{\overline{OP''}}{k}
&= \frac{-(c_l y_k{}^2 + c_k y_l{}^2)}{c_h(y_k{}^2 + y_l{}^2)},
\\
  \ctn^2 &\frac{\overline{OP  }}{k}
+ \ctn^2 \frac{\overline{OP''}}{k}
= -\frac{c_k+c_l}{c_h}.     \tag{16}
\end{align*}%[** Overlines assumed]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~15. The sum\index{Sum04@Sum of distances connected with a conic} of
the squares of the cotangents
of the $k$th parts of the distances
from a centre of a
central conic to two intersections
of the curve with
mutually perpendicular diameters
through this centre is
constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~15$'$. The sum of
the squares of the cotangents
of the angles which an axis of
a central conic makes with
two tangents from a pair of
orthogonal points of this axis
is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The equation of the tangent $t'$ at the point $P'$ is
\[
  c_h y_h x_h + \sqrt{c_k c_l}(x_k y_l - x_l y_k) = 0.
\]

From this we get
\[
  \sin^2 \frac{\overline{O_h t'}}{k}
= \frac{c_h{}^2 y_h{}^2}{(c_l-c_h)c_k y_k{}^2 + (c_k-c_h)c_l y_l{}^2},
\]
\[
  \tan \frac{\overline{O_h P }}{k}
  \tan \frac{\overline{O_h t'}}{k}
= \frac{c_h}{\sqrt{c_k c_l}}.      \tag{17}
\]

%[File: 151.png]
\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~16. The product\index{Products connected with a conic}
of the tangents of the $k$th
parts of the distances from
a centre of a central conic\index{Centre02@--- of a conic}
to a point of the curve and
to the tangent where the curve
meets a diameter\index{Diameters01@Diameters of conic} conjugate to
that from the centre to the
point of the curve, is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~16$'$. The product
of the tangents of the angles
which an axis of a central
conic makes with a tangent
and with the absolute polar
of a point of contact with
a tangent from a point of this
axis conjugate to the intersection
with the given tangent,
is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The equations of two conjugate diameters through $O_h$ have
already been written
\[
y_l x_k - y_k x_l = 0,\quad c_k y_k x_k + c_l y_l x_l = 0.
\]

The product of the tangents of the angles which they make
with the $x_k$ axis is
\[
\frac{y_k c_l y_l}{y_l c_k y_k} = \frac{c_l}{c_k}.
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~17. The product
of the tangents of the angles
which two conjugate diameters
through a centre make
with either axis through this
centre is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~17$'$. The product
of the tangents of the $k$th
parts of the distances of two
conjugate points of an axis
from either centre on this
axis is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let $P_h$, $P'_h$ be the intersections of the $x_h$ axis with the conic
\[
\cos\frac{\overline{P_h P'_h}}{k} =
  \frac{c_h + c_l}{c_k - c_l},
\]
%[**c_k + c_l?]
\[
\tag{18}
  \tan^2 \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{\overline{P_h P'_h}}{k} \cdot
  \tan^2 \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{\overline{P_k P'_k}}{k} \cdot
  \tan^2 \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{\overline{P_l P'_l}}{k} = -1.
\]

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~18. The product
of the squares of the tangents
of the $2k$th parts of the
distances determined by a
central conic on the axes is
equal to $-1$.}
{\emph{Theorem}~18$'$. The product
of the squares of the tangents
of the half-angles of the
pairs of tangents to a central
conic from its centres is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

If a circle have double contact with a conic, we have, with
the Absolute, the figure of two conics having double contact
with a third, already studied in the last chapter.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~19. If a circle have
double contact with a conic,
its axis\index{Axis01@Axis of a circle} and the lines connecting
%[File: 152.png]
the points of contact are
harmonically separated by a
pair of focal lines\index{Focal03@--- lines}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~19$'$. If a circle have
double contact with a conic,
its centre and the intersections
%[File: 152.png bis]
of the common tangents are
harmonically separated by a
pair of foci\index{Foci}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Of course we mean by foci and focal lines of any conic what
we mean in the special case of the central conic.

\mysect{Auxiliary circles}
A circle\index{Circle01@Circle} which has double contact with a central conic
where the latter meets an axis is called an \emph{auxiliary circle}.
There will clearly be six such circles, their centres being the
centres of the conic. Consider the circle having its centre
at $O_k$ while it has double contact with our central conic at
the intersections with $x_h = 0$.
\[
  p\sum_i^{0..2} c_i x_i{}^2 + q x_h{}^2 \equiv
  r(xx) + s x_k{}^2 = 0,
\]
\[
  \sum_i^{0..2} c_i x_i{}^2 + (c_l-c_h) x_h{}^2 =
  c_h x_h{}^2 + c_k x_k{}^2 + c_l x_l{}^2 = 0.
\]

This will meet the line $(u)$ through $O_h$ in points $Q$, $Q'$, having
coordinates
\[
  x_h  : x_k  : x_l =
  \sqrt{-\Bigl( \frac{c_l u_k{}^2}{c_l}
              + \frac{c_k u_l{}^2}{c_l} \Bigr)}  : u_l  : -u_k.
\]

The same line will meet the conic in points $P$, $P'$, having
coordinates
\[
  x_h  : x_k  : x_l =
  \sqrt{-\frac{c_l u_k{}^2 + c_k u_l{}^2}{c_h}}  : u_l  : -u_k,
\]
\[
  \tan^2\frac{\overline{O_h Q}}{k} =
  \frac{-c_l(u_k{}^2 + u_l{}^2)}{c_l u_k{}^2 + c_k u_l{}^2},
\quad
  \tan^2\frac{\overline{O_h P}}{k} =
  \frac{-c_h(u_k{}^2 + u_l{}^2)}{c_l u_k{}^2 + c_k u_l{}^2},
\]
\[
\tag{19}
  \tan\frac{\overline{O_h Q}}{k}  : \tan\frac{\overline{O_h P}}{k}
= \sqrt{c_l}  : \sqrt{c_h}.
\]

Let us remark, finally, that the tangent of the $k$th part of the
distance from a point to a line, is the cotangent of the $k$th part
of its distance to the pole of the line, and that if the tangents
of two distances bear a constant ratio\index{Ratios, constant connected with conics}, so do their cotangents:

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~20. If the tangents
of the $k$th parts of the distances
from the points of a
circle to any diameter\index{Diameters01@Diameters of conic} be
%[File: 153.png]
altered in a constant ratio, the
locus of the resulting points
will be a conic having the
given circle as an auxiliary\index{Circle02@Circle auxiliary to conic}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~20$'$. If the tangents
of the angles which the tangents
to a circle make with a
diameter be altered in a constant
%[File: 153.png bis]
ratio, the envelope of the
resulting lines will be a conic
having the given circle as an
auxiliary circle.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Normals}
The normal at any point of a conic is the line connecting
it with the absolute pole of its tangent. This line is also
perpendicular to the absolute polar of the given point, so that
the conic and its absolute polar conic are geodesically parallel
curves. The equation of the normal to our conic (1) will be
\begin{equation*}
\tag{20}
\sum_i^{0..2}\frac{c_k - c_l}{y_h} x_h = 0.
\end{equation*}

The tangents to a central conic from a centre shall be called
\emph{asymptotes}\index{Asymptotes}. The equation of the pair of asymptotes through
the centre $(O_h)$ will evidently be
\begin{equation*}
  c_k x_k{}^2 + c_l x_l{}^2 = 0.
\tag{21}
\end{equation*}

The tangent at the point $P$ with coordinates $(y)$ will meet
them in two points $R$, $R'$, whose coordinates are
\begin{equation*}
x_h  : x_k  : x_l =
  \sqrt{-c_k c_l} (\sqrt{-c_l} y_l \pm \sqrt{c_k} y_k)  :
  \mp c_h y_h \sqrt{-c_l}  :
  -c_h y_h \sqrt{c_k},
\end{equation*}\label{whatsce}
\begin{equation*}
\tag{22}
\tan\frac{\overline{O_h R}}{k} \tan\frac{\overline{O_h R'}}{k} =
  \frac{(c_l-c_k) c_h{}^2 y_h{}^2}{c_k c_l (c_ky_k{}^2+c_ly_l{}^2)} =
  \frac{c_h (c_k-c_l)}{c_k c_l}.
\end{equation*}

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~21. The product of
the tangents of the $k$th parts
of the distances from a centre
of a central conic to the intersection
with the asymptotes
through that centre of a tangent
is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~21$'$. The product
of the tangents of the angles
which an axis of a central
conic makes with the lines
from a point of the curve to
the intersections of the curve
with this axis is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Confocal and homothetic conics}
A set of conics which meet the Absolute\index{Absolute} in the same four
points shall be said to be \emph{homothetic}\index{Homothetic01@Homothetic conics}. If they have the same
four absolute tangents they shall be called \emph{confocal}. We get
at once from Desargues' involution theorem:---

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~22. One conic
homothetic to a given conic
will pass through every point
of space, and two will touch
every line, not through a point
%[File: 154.png]
common to all the conics, in
the centres of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of all
pairs of intersections of the
homothetic conics\index{Homothetic01@Homothetic conics} with this
line.}
{\emph{Theorem}~22$'$. One conic confocal
with a given conic will
touch every line, and two
will pass through every point
not on the common tangents
%[File: 154.png bis]
to all. The tangents to these
two will bisect\index{Bisector of an angle} the angles of
the pairs of tangents from that
point to all of the confocal
conics\index{Confocal conics}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Concentric circles are a special case both of homothetic and
of confocal conics. The general form for the equations of conics
homothetic and confocal respectively to our conic (1) will be
\[
  \sum_i^{0..2} (c_i + m)x_0{}^2 = 0.       \quad(23);
\qquad
  \sum_i^{0..2} \frac{c_i}{l+c_i}x_i = 0. \quad(24)
\]
\index{Conic|)}\index{Central conic|)}

\mysect{Elliptic coordinates}
It is sometimes useful to modify the second of these
equations, in order to introduce the elliptic coordinates\index{Elliptic01@Elliptic co-ordinates} of
a point, i.e.\ the two parameters giving the conics of the
confocal system which pass through it. Let us write $\dfrac{1}{c_i}$ in
place of $c_i$.
\[
  \frac{x_i}{\sqrt{(xx)}} = X_i.
\]

Our confocal conics have, then, the general equation
\[
  \sum_i^{0..2} \frac{X_i}{c_i-\lambda} = 0. \tag{25}
\]

If $\lambda_1$ and $\lambda_2$ be the parameter values of the conic through $(X)$
we have
\[
  X_h
= \sqrt{\frac{(c_k-c_l) (c_h-\lambda_1) (c_h-\lambda_2) }
             {\sum\limits_h^{0..2} c_h{}^2(c_h-c_l)           }}. \tag{26}
\]
\[
ds^2 = \sum_i^{0..2} dX_i{}^2
= \frac{1}{4}
  \left[ \frac{(\lambda_1 - \lambda_2) d\lambda_1{}^2}
              {\prod\limits_i^{0..2} (c_i-\lambda_1) }
       + \frac{(\lambda_2 - \lambda_1) d\lambda_2{}^2}
              {\prod\limits_i^{0..2} (c_i-\lambda_2) }
 \right]. \tag{27}
\]

With the aid of these coordinates, we may easily prove for
the non-euclidean case Graves'\index{Graves} theorem, namely, if a loop
of thread be cast about an extremely thin elliptic disk, and
pulled taut at a point, that point will trace a confocal ellipse\index{Ellipse}.
We shall not give the details here, however, for in the next
chapter we shall work at length the more interesting corresponding
problem in three dimensions, and the calculations
are too fatiguing to make it advisable to carry them through
twice.

%[File: 155.png]



%CHAPTER XIII
\mychap{XIII}{QUADRIC SURFACES}
\mysect{Classification of quadrics}

The discussion of non-euclidean quadric surfaces may be
carried on in the same spirit as that of conic sections in the
preceding chapter. There is not, however, the same wealth
of easy and interesting theorems, owing to the greater complication
in the formation of the simultaneous covariants
of two quadrics.

Let us begin by classifying non-euclidean quadrics under
the group of real congruent transformations.%
\footnote{The classification here given is that which appears in the author's\index{Author}
article `Quadric Surfaces in Hyperbolic Space', \emph{Transactions of the American
Mathematical Society}, vol.~iv, 1903. This classification was simplified and
put into better shape by Bromwich\index{Bromwich}, `The Classification of Quadratic Loci,'
ibid., vol.~vi, 1905. The latter, however, makes use of Weierstrassian
Elementary Divisors, and it seemed wiser to avoid the introduction of these
into the present work. Both Professor Bromwich and the author wrote in
ignorance of the fact that they had been preceded by rather a crude article
by Barbarin\index{Barbarin}, `\'Etude de g\'eom\'etrie non-euclidienne,' \emph{M\'emoires couronn\'es par
l'Acad\'emie de Belgique}, vol.~vi, 1900.}
%endfootnote
We begin
in the actual domain of hyperbolic space, giving only those
surfaces which have a real part in that domain and a non-vanishing
discriminant. The names adopted are intended to
give a certain idea of the shape of the surface. We shall
mean by \emph{curve}, the curve of intersection of the surface and
Absolute\index{Absolute}, while \emph{developable} is the developable of common
tangent planes.

\medskip A. Central Quadrics.

(1) Ellipsoid\index{Ellipsoid}. Imaginary quartic curve and developable.

(2) Concave, non-ruled hyperboloid. Real quartic curve
and developable.

(3) Convex non-ruled hyperboloid.  Real quartic curve,
imaginary developable.

(4) Two-sheeted ruled hyperboloid. Real quartic curve
and developable.

(5) One-sheeted ruled hyperboloid.  Real quartic curve,
imaginary developable.

(6) Non-ruled semi-hyperboloid\index{Semi-hyperboloid}. Real quartic curve and
developable.

(7) Ruled semi-hyperboloid. Real quartic curve and developable.

The last two surfaces differ from the preceding ones in that
%[File: 156.png]
here two vertices of the common self-conjugate tetrahedron
(in the sense of projective geometry) of the surface and
Absolute\index{Absolute} are conjugate imaginaries, while in the first five
cases all four are real.

\medskip B.

(8) Elliptic paraboloid\index{Paraboloid}. Imaginary quartic curve with real
acnode, imaginary developable.

(9) Tubular non-ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Real quartic
with acnode, real developable.

(10) Cup-shaped non-ruled hyperbolic paraboloid.  Real
quartic with acnode, imaginary developable.

(11) Open ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Real acnodal
quartic, real developable.

(12) Gathered ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Real crunodal
quartic, imaginary developable.

(13) Cuspidal non-ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Real cuspidal quartic curve, real developable.

(14) Cuspidal ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Real cuspidal
quartic curve, real developable.

(15) Horocyclic non-ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. The curve
is two mutually tangent conics, developable real.

(16) Horocyclic elliptic paraboloid. Curve is two mutually
tangent imaginary conics, developable imaginary.

(17) Horocyclic ruled hyperbolic paraboloid. Curve is two
real mutually tangent conics, developable imaginary.

(18) Non-ruled osculating semi-hyperbolic paraboloid. The
curve is a real conic and two conjugate imaginary generators
meeting on it. The developable is a real cone, and two
imaginary lines.

\medskip C. Surfaces of Revolution.\index{Revolution, surfaces of}

(19) Prolate spheroid\index{Spheroid}. Curve is two imaginary conics in
real ultra-infinite planes, imaginary developable.

(20) Oblate spheroid. Curve is two imaginary conics in
conjugate imaginary planes meeting in an ultra-infinite line,
imaginary developable.

(21) Concave non-ruled hyperboloid\index{Hyperboloid} of revolution. Curve
is two real conics whose planes meet in an ideal line, real
developable.

(22) Convex non-ruled hyperboloid of revolution. Absolute
curve two real conics whose planes meet in an ideal line,
imaginary developable.

(23) Ruled hyperboloid of revolution.  Curve two real
conics whose planes meet in an ideal line, imaginary developable.

%[File: 157.png]
(24) Semi-hyperboloid of revolution. The curve is a real
conic, and an imaginary one in a real plane, the developable
is a real cone and an imaginary one.

(25) Elliptic paraboloid of revolution. The absolute curve
is an imaginary conic in an ultra-infinite plane, and two
imaginary generators not intersecting on the conic. The developable
is an imaginary cone, and the same two generators.

(26) Tubular semi-hyperbolic paraboloid of revolution.
The curve is a real conic and two imaginary generators not
intersecting on it; the developable is the same two lines and
a real cone.

(27) Cup-shaped semi-hyperbolic paraboloid of revolution\index{Revolution, surfaces of}.
Real conic and two imaginary lines not meeting on it. Developable
same two lines and imaginary cone.

(28) Clifford\index{Clifford} surface. Curve and developable two generators
of each set.

\medskip D. Canal Surfaces\index{Canal surface}.%
\footnote{Called \emph{Surfaces of Translation} in the author's\index{Author} article `Quadric Surfaces',
loc.\ cit.}
%endfootnote

(29) Elliptic canal surface. Curve is two imaginary conics
whose planes meet in an actual line, developable imaginary.

(30) Non-ruled hyperbolic canal surface. Two real conics
whose planes meet in an actual line, developable two real
cones.

(31) Ruled hyperbolic canal surface. Curve two real conics
whose planes meet in an actual line, imaginary developable.

\medskip E. Spheres.\index{Sphere}

(32) Proper sphere.  Curve is two coincident imaginary
conics, developable imaginary.

(33) Equidistant surface\index{Equidistant02@--- surfaces}. Curve two real coincident conics,
developable two real coincident cones.

(34) Horocyclic surface\index{Horocyclic surface}. Curve and developable
two conjugate imaginary intersecting generators, each counted twice.

\medskip In elliptic or spherical space the number of real varieties
will, of course, be much smaller. We have

(1) Non-ruled ellipsoid\index{Ellipsoid}.

(2) Ruled ellipsoid.

(3) Prolate spheroid\index{Spheroid}.

(4) Oblate spheroid.

(5) Ruled ellipsoid of revolution.

(6) Clifford surface.

(7) Sphere.

%[File: 158.png]

It is worth mentioning that the Clifford\index{Clifford} surface of elliptic
space has real linear generators, while that in hyperbolic space
has not.

\mysect{Central quadrics}
\index{Central quadric|(}
Let us next turn our attention to that class of quadrics
which we have termed central, and which are distinguished
by the existence of a non-degenerate tetrahedron (in the
projective sense) self-conjugate with regard both to the surface
and the Absolute\index{Absolute}. The vertices of this tetrahedron shall be
called the \emph{centres}\index{Centre04@--- of quadric} of the surface,
and its planes the \emph{axial planes}.
When this tetrahedron is chosen as the basis of the coordinate
system, the Absolute may be written in the typical form
while the equation of the surface involves none but squared
terms.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~1. A centre of a
central quadric is equidistant
from the intersections with
the surface of every line
through this centre.}
{\emph{Theorem}~1$'$. An axial plane
of a central quadric bisects\index{Bisector of an angle}
a dihedral angle of every two
tangent planes to the surface
which meet in a line of this
axial plane.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

We obtain a good deal of information about our central
quadrics by enumerating the Cayleyan\index{Cayley} characteristics of their
curves of intersection with the Absolute, and the corresponding
developables. The curve is a twisted quartic of deficiency
one. Its osculating developable is of order eight and class
twelve. It has sixteen stationary tangent planes, thirty-eight
lines in every plane lie in two osculating planes, two secants,
i.e.\ two lines meeting the curve twice, pass every point not on
the curve, sixteen points in every plane are the intersection of
two tangents, eight double tangent planes pass through every
point. The developable will, of course, possess the dual
characteristics.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~2. Through an
arbitrary point in space will
pass twelve planes cutting a
central quadric in osculating
parabolas, eight planes of
parabolic\index{Paraboloid} section will pass
through an arbitrary line. An
arbitrary point will be the
centre of one section. Sixteen
planes cut the surface in horocycles, sixteen points in an
%[File: 159.png]
arbitrary plane are the centres
of circular sections, eight
planes of circular section pass
through an arbitrary point.}
{\emph{Theorem}~2$'$. In an arbitrary
plane there will be twelve
points, vertices of cones circumscribed to a central
quadric which have stationary
contact with the cone of tangents
to the Absolute, eight
points on an arbitrary line
are vertices of circumscribed
cones which touch the Absolute.
An arbitrary plane will
%[File: 159.png bis]
be a plane of symmetry for
one circumscribed cone. Sixteen
points are vertices of
circumscribed cones which
have four-plane contact with
the Absolute. Sixteen planes
through an arbitrary point are
perpendicular to the axes of
revolution of circumscribed
cones of revolution.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Planes of circular section and parabolic section}
The planes of circular section are those which touch the
cones whose vertices are the centres of the quadric, and which
pass through the Absolute curve. It may be shown that not
more than six real planes of circular section will pass through
an actual point, and that only two of these will cut the surface
in proper circles.%
\footnote{See the author's\index{Author} `Quadric Surfaces', loc.\ cit., p.~164.}
%endfootnote

Let us write as the equation of a typical quadric
\[
  \sum_i^{0..3} c_i x_i{}^2 = 0.  \tag{1}
\]

No two of the $c$'s shall be equal, and none shall equal
zero.

The cones whose vertices are the centres and which pass
through the Absolute curves shall be called the \emph{focal cones}\index{Focal01@Focal cones}.
In like manner there will be four \emph{focal conics}\index{Focal02@--- conics} in the axial
planes. The equation of the focal cone whose vertex is $O_h$
will be
\[
  \sum_i^{0..3} (c_i - c_h)x_i{}^2 = 0.  \tag{2}
\]

The focal conic in the corresponding axial plane will be
\[
  x_h = 0,\quad \sum_i^{0..3} \frac{c_h-c_i}{c_h c_i} w_i{}^2 = 0.  \tag{3}
\]

Let the reader show that each of these conics passes through
two foci of each other one.

We next seek the locus of points whence three mutually
tangent planes may be drawn to the surface. Let these be the
planes $(v)$, $(w)$, $(\omega)$, and let the equation of the surface and
%[File: 160.png]
the Absolute in plane coordinates be, in the Clebsch-Aronhold\index{Clebsch}\index{Aronhold}
notation
\[
{u_\gamma}^2 = 0, \quad {u_\alpha}^2 \equiv u_\alpha{}'^2 = 0,
\]
\[
{v_\gamma}^2 = {w_\gamma}^2 = {\omega_\gamma}^2 = 0,
\]
\[
v_\alpha w_\alpha = w_\alpha \omega_\alpha
= \omega_\alpha v_\alpha = 0,
\]
\[
\begin{vmatrix}
  v_\gamma  & w_\gamma  & \omega_\gamma \\
  v_\alpha  & w_\alpha  & \omega_\alpha \\
  v_\alpha{}' & w_\alpha{}' & \omega_\alpha{}'
\end{vmatrix}^2
= \lvert \gamma\alpha\alpha' x \rvert^2 = 0,
\]
where $(x)$ is the point of concurrence of the planes $(v)$, $(w)$, $(\omega)$.
Returning to actual coefficients, the coefficients of $x_i x_j$ will
vanish, for they involve $\gamma_i \gamma_j$ or $\alpha_k \alpha_l$ which are zero. We
shall find eventually
\begin{equation*}
\tag{4}
\sum_h^{0..3} c_h (c_k c_l + c_l c_m + c_m c_k) x_h{}^2 = 0.
\end{equation*}

This quadric is also the locus of points whence triads of
tangents to the Absolute are conjugate with regard to the
given quadric, hence interchanging $\gamma$ and $\alpha$, we get the locus
of points whence triads of mutually perpendicular tangents
may be drawn to the quadric (1)
\begin{equation*}
\tag{5}
\sum_h^{0..3} c_h (c_k + c_l + c_m) x_h{}^2 = 0.
\end{equation*}

If the quadric be ruled, the former of these loci will intersect
it along a curve where generators of different sets intersect
at right angles.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~3. A line will meet
a central quadric and its focal
cones\index{Focal01@Focal cones} in five pairs of points
with the same centres of
gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~3$'$. The tangent
planes to a central quadric
and its focal conics\index{Focal02@--- conics} through
a line form five sets of dihedral
angles with the same bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

The proof of these two theorems is immediate.

\mysect{Conjugate and mutually perpendicular lines through a centre}
If we mean by a diameter of a quadric\index{Diameters02@--- of quadric}, a line through
a centre, we see that we may pass from any set of three
concurrent conjugate diameters to any other such set through
that same centre by changing two diameters at a time, and
keeping the third one fixed. We may thus continually apply
Theorem 14, of Chapter XII\@. In the same way we may pass
from any set of three mutually perpendicular diameters to
%[File: 161.png]
any other such set, and apply Theorem 15 of the same
chapter.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~4. The sum of the
squares of the tangents of the
$k$th parts of the distances
from a centre of a central
quadric to three intersections
of the surface with three conjugate diameters\index{Diameters02@--- of quadric} through that
centre is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~4$'$. The sum of the
squares\index{Sum05@Sum of distances connected with a quadric} of the tangents of the
angles which an axial plane
of a central quadric makes
with three tangent planes
through three conjugate lines
in that axial plane is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~5. The sum of the
squares of the cotangents of
the $k$th parts of the distances
from a centre of a central
quadric to three intersections
with the surface of three
mutually perpendicular lines
through that centre is constant.}
{\emph{Theorem}~5$'$. The sum of the
squares of the cotangents of
the angles which an axial
plane of a central quadric
makes with three tangent
planes through three mutually
perpendicular lines in that
axial plane is constant.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

To find the values of the constants referred to in Theorems 4
and 5, we have but to choose a particular set of diameters,
say the intersections of the axial planes through $O_h$. We
thus get
\[
  \tan^2 \frac{\overline{O_hP}}{k}
+ \tan^2 \frac{\overline{O_hP'}}{k}
+ \tan^2 \frac{\overline{O_hP''}}{k}
= -c_h\left( \frac{1}{c_k} + \frac{1}{c_l} + \frac{1}{c_m} \right). \tag{6}
\]
\[
  \ctn^2 \frac{\overline{O_hQ}}{k}
+ \ctn^2 \frac{\overline{O_hQ'}}{k}
+ \ctn^2 \frac{\overline{O_hQ''}}{k}
= -\frac{(c_k + c_l + c_m)}{c_h}.
\tag{7}
\]
\index{Central quadric|)}

A set of quadrics having the same absolute focal curve, and,
hence, the same focal cones, shall be called \emph{homothetic}\index{Homothetic02@--- quadrics}. A set
inscribed in the same absolute developable, and possessing,
in consequence the same focal conics shall be called \emph{confocal}\index{Confocal quadrics}.

\medskip

\mysect{Confocal and homothetic quadrics}
\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~6. An arbitrary
line will meet a set of confocal quadrics in pairs of
points with the same centres
of gravity.}
{\emph{Theorem}~6$'$. The tangent
planes to a set of confocal
quadrics through an arbitrary
line, form dihedral angles with
the same bisectors.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~7. Three homothetic quadrics will touch an
arbitrary plane in three
mutually orthogonal points.}
{\emph{Theorem}~7$'$. Three confocal
quadrics will pass through an
arbitrary point, and intersect
orthogonally.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

%[File: 162.png]
\mysect{Elliptic coordinates, various forms of the distance element}
Let us now set up our system of elliptic coordinates as we
did in the plane
\begin{equation*}
\tag{8}
X_i=\frac{x_i}{\sqrt{(xx)}},\quad (XX) = 1.
\end{equation*}

These coordinates $(X)$ are inapplicable to points of the
Absolute\index{Absolute}; we imagine that all such points are excluded from
consideration. The general equation for the system of quadrics
confocal with that given by (1) will be,%
\footnote{The residue of the present chapter is closely analogous to the treatment
of the corresponding euclidean problem given by Klein\index{Klein} in his `Einleitung in
die h\"ohere Geometrie', lithographed notes, G\"ottingen, 1893, pp.~38--73, and
Staude\index{Staude}, `Fadenconstruktion des Ellipsoids,' \emph{Mathematische Annalen}, vol.~xx,
1882. Staude returns to the subject in his \emph{Die Fokaleigenschaften der Fl\"achen
zweiter Ordnung}, Leipzig, 1896. This book is intended as a supplement to the
usual textbooks on analytic geometry, and is somewhat prolix in its attempts
at simplicity.}
%endfootnote
if we replace $c_i$ by $\dfrac{1}{c_i}$,
\begin{equation*}
\tag{9}
\sum_i^{0..3}\frac{X_i{}^2}{c_i-\lambda}=0.
\end{equation*}
If the roots be $\lambda_1$, $\lambda_2$, $\lambda_3$, we have
\begin{equation*}
\tag{10}
X_h = \sqrt{\frac{(c_h-\lambda_1)(c_h-\lambda_2)(c_h-\lambda_3)}
  {(c_h-c_k)(c_h-c_l)(c_h-c_m)}}.
\end{equation*}
For the differential of distance we have
\begin{equation*}
\tag{11}
\frac{ds^2}{k^2} =
  \frac{(xx)(dx\,dx)-(x\,dx)^2}{(xx)^2} = (dX\,dX).
\end{equation*}
We wish to express this in terms of our elliptic coordinates\index{Elliptic01@Elliptic co-ordinates}.
It will be found that the coefficients of $d\lambda_p\; d\lambda_q$ will vanish,
and, indeed, this is \emph{a priori} evident if we have in mind that
our coordinate system is a triply orthogonal one, and the
general formulae for orthogonal curves, as will be shown in
Chapter XV, are the same for euclidean as for non-euclidean
We thus get
\[
\frac{ds^2}{k^2} =
  \frac{1}{4}\sum_h^{0..3}\sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(c_h-\lambda_q)(c_h-\lambda_r)d\lambda_p{}^2}
  {(c_h-c_k)(c_h-c_l)(c_h-c_m)(c_h-\lambda_p)}.
\]
If we give to $c_h$ each of its four values, divide the terms into
partial fractions and recombine, we get
\begin{equation*}
\tag{12}
\frac{ds^2}{k^2} =
  \frac{1}{4}\sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_p-\lambda_r)d\lambda_p{}^2}
  {\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)}.
\end{equation*}
The analogy to the corresponding formula in euclidean space
is striking.

%[File: 163.png]
The cones whose vertices are all at an arbitrary point, and
which are circumscribed to a set of confocal quadrics, will
themselves be confocal, i.e.\ they will have four common
tangent planes which touch the Absolute\index{Absolute}. Any two of these
cones will intersect orthogonally. This shows that the congruence\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}
of lines tangent to two confocal quadrics will be
a normal\index{Normals02@--- to surface} one, the edges of regression of their developable
surfaces being geodesics of the quadrics. These facts, well
known in the euclidean case, will be proved for the noneuclidean
one in Chapter XVI\@. Notice that we get the
system of geodesics of a quadric by means of its $\infty^3$ common
tangents with confocal quadrics. The difficulties which arise
for special positions, as umbilical points\index{Umbilical points}, need not concern
us here.

The equation of the cone whose vertex is $(Y)$ and which
circumscribes the quadric (1) will be

\begin{equation*}
\sum_i^{0..3} \frac{Y_i{}^2}{c_i-\lambda} \sum_i^{0..3} \frac{X_i{}^2}{c_i-\lambda} - \Bigl[\sum_i^{0..3} \frac{Y_iX_i}{c_i-\lambda}\Bigr]^2 =0.
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
\sum_i^{0..3} \sum_j^{0..3} \frac{(X_iY_j-X_jY_i)^2}{(c_i-\lambda)(c_j-\lambda)}=0.
\end{equation*}

Putting $X = Y+dY$ we get the differential form

\begin{equation*}
\sum_i^{0..3} \sum_j^{0..3} \frac{(Y_idY_j-Y_jdY_i)^2}{(c_i-\lambda)(c_j-\lambda)}=0.
\end{equation*}

Let us change this also to the elliptic form. We notice
that the coefficients of the expressions $d\lambda_p$ $d\lambda_q$ will be 0,
for the axial planes of the cones will be given by tangents to

\begin{equation*}
\lambda_p=0,\quad \lambda_q=0,\quad \lambda_r=0.
\end{equation*}

The $\infty^1$ confocal cones form a one-parameter family all
touching the same tangent planes to the cone $ds^2=0$. The
%[File: 164.png]
equation of one cone of the family may be thrown into
the form
\[
\sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_p-\lambda_r)}
  {\Bigl[\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)\Bigr]L_p}
  d\lambda_p{}^2=0,
\]
where $L_p$ is a function of $\lambda$. Hence the general form will be
\[
\sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_p-\lambda_r)}
  {\Bigl[\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)\Bigr](L_p-\mu)}
  d\lambda_p{}^2=0.
\]
It remains to find the value of $L_p-\mu$. It is clearly a polynomial
in powers of $\lambda$, which vanishes only when
$\lambda = \lambda_p$,
for then only shall we have $d\lambda_p{}^2 = 0$. We thus get
\[
L_p-\mu=A_p(\lambda_p-\lambda)^m,
\]
where $A_p$ is a constant. Again, as two of these confocal
quadrics contain every line through the vertex, we must
have $m = 1$. Lastly, our expression is symmetrical in $p$, $q$, $r$,
hence
\[
A_p=A_q=A_r.
\]
We finally get for our cone
\begin{equation*}
\tag{13}
\sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_p-\lambda_r)d\lambda_p{}^2}
       {\Bigl[\prod\limits_i^{0..3}
        (c_i-\lambda_p)\Bigr](\lambda_p-\lambda)}=0.
\end{equation*}
For progress along an arc of a geodesic\index{Geodesic02@--- lines} of
$\lambda_r = $ const., we
have
\[
d\lambda_p\sqrt{\frac{\lambda_p-\lambda_r}
  {(\lambda_p-\lambda)\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)}}
  \pm d\lambda_q\sqrt{\frac{\lambda_q-\lambda_r}
  {(\lambda_q-\lambda)\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_q)}} = 0.
\]
so that the problem of finding the geodesics of a quadric
depends merely upon elliptic integrals. If we take
$\lambda_r = \lambda$,
%[File: 165.png]
we have double tangents to the surface, i.e.\ rectilinear
generators,
\[
    \frac{d\lambda_p}{\sqrt{\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)}}
\pm \frac{d\lambda_q}{\sqrt{\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_q)}} = 0.
\]
The general differential of arc on a surface
$\lambda_r = \text{\ const.}$ is
\[
\frac{ds^2}{k^2}
= \frac{1}{4}
  \left[ \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_p-\lambda_r)}
              {\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p) } d\lambda_p{}^2
       + \frac{(\lambda_q-\lambda_p)(\lambda_q-\lambda_r)}
              {\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_q) } d\lambda_q{}^2
 \right],
\]
we have, then, for a distance along a generator
\[
  s = \frac{k}{2}
      \int\frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q) d\lambda_p}
               {\sqrt{\prod\limits_i^{0..3}(c_i-\lambda_p)}}.
\]
This expression is independent of $\lambda_r$, whence

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~8. If from a set of
confocal central quadrics\index{Confocal quadrics} a one-parameter
set of linear generators be so chosen that all
intersect the same $\infty^1$ lines of
curvature of $\infty^1$ confocal quadrics of the system, then any
two of these lines of curvature
will cut congruent distances
on all of these linear
generators.}
{\emph{Theorem}~8$'$. If from a set of
homothetic central quadrics
a one-parameter set of linear
generators be so chosen that
all touch $\infty^1$ developables
circumscribed to pairs of
quadrics of the homothetic
system, then the tangent
planes to any two of these
developables will determine
congruent dihedral angles
whose edges are the given
linear generators.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Theorem 8 may also be easily proved by showing that the
generators of a set of confocal quadrics form an isotropic
congruence, whereof much more later.%
\footnote{The general theorem concerning isotropic
congruences\index{Congruence11@--- isotropic}\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence} upon which this
depends will be proved in Chapter XVI, where also will be found a bibliography of the subject.}
%endfootnote

%[File: 166.png]
We now seek for the expression for the element of distance
upon a common tangent to two confocal quadrics $\lambda$, $\lambda'$.
\[
  \sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_q-\lambda_p) (\lambda_r-\lambda_p)d\lambda_p{}^2}
       {\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_p) \Bigr]
        (\lambda-\lambda_p)}
= 0,
\]
\[
  \sum_p^{1..3}
  \frac{(\lambda_q-\lambda_p) (\lambda_r-\lambda_p)d\lambda_p{}^2}
       {\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_p) \Bigr]
        [\lambda'-\lambda_p]}
= 0.
\]
\[
    \frac{(\lambda_p - \lambda_q) d\lambda_q                      }
         {\sqrt{\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_q) \Bigr]
                 (\lambda-\lambda_q) (\lambda'-\lambda_q)        } }
\\
=\pm\frac{(\lambda_p - \lambda_r) d\lambda_r                      }
         {\sqrt{\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_r) \Bigr]
                 (\lambda-\lambda_r) (\lambda'-\lambda_r)        } }.
\tag{14}
\]
\[
    \frac{(\lambda_q - \lambda_p) d\lambda_p                      }
         {\sqrt{\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_p) \Bigr]
                 (\lambda-\lambda_p) (\lambda'-\lambda_p)        } }
\\
=\pm\frac{(\lambda_q - \lambda_r) d\lambda_r                      }
         {\sqrt{\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_r) \Bigr]
                 (\lambda-\lambda_r) (\lambda'-\lambda_r)        } }.
\]
\[
    \frac{ds}{k}
=\pm\frac{d\lambda_p  }
         {2\sqrt{\Bigl[ \prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_p) \Bigr]} }
 \times\frac{\sqrt{(\lambda_q - \lambda_p) (\lambda_r - \lambda_p)}}
            {\sqrt{(\lambda   - \lambda_p) (\lambda'  - \lambda_p)}}
 \times \\
 \frac{\sqrt{\begin{vmatrix}
                \lambda_p{}^2 & \lambda_q{}^2 & \lambda_r{}^2 \\
                \lambda_p   & \lambda_q   & \lambda_r   \\
                1           & 1           & 1
              \end{vmatrix}}                 }
            {\sqrt{\lambda_q - \lambda_r } }
\]
%[File: 167.png]
\[
(\lambda_q-\lambda_r)\frac{ds}{k}=\frac{d\lambda_p}{2\sqrt{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_p)}}
\times \frac{(\lambda_p-\lambda_q)(\lambda_q-\lambda_r)(\lambda_r-\lambda_p)}
{\sqrt{(\lambda-\lambda_p)(\lambda'-\lambda_p)}}.
\]

Multiplying through by $(\lambda-\lambda_p)$, $(\lambda'-\lambda_p)$, and summing for $p=1,\; 2,\; 3$

\begin{align*}
\frac{ds}{k}&=\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{(\lambda-\lambda_1)(\lambda'-\lambda_1)}
{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_1)}}d\lambda_1\\
&\hspace{0.5in}+\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{(\lambda-\lambda_2)(\lambda'-\lambda_2)}{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_2)}}d\lambda_2\\
&\hspace{1in}+\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{(\lambda-\lambda_3)(\lambda'-\lambda_3)}{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_3)}}d\lambda_3. \tag{15}
\end{align*}


For a geodesic on $\lambda=\lambda_1$ whose tangent touches $\lambda'$ we have

\[
\frac{ds}{k}=\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{(\lambda-\lambda_2)(\lambda'-\lambda_2)}
{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_2)}}d\lambda_2
+\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{(\lambda-\lambda_3)(\lambda'-\lambda_3)}{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_3)}}d\lambda_3.
\tag{16}
\]
