For a line of curvature common to $\lambda=\lambda_1$, $\lambda'=\lambda_2$

\begin{equation*}
\frac{ds}{k}=\frac{1}{2}
\sqrt{\frac{(\lambda_1-\lambda_3)(\lambda_2-\lambda_3)}{\prod\limits_i^{0..3} (c_i-\lambda_3)}}d\lambda_3. \tag{17}
\end{equation*}

\mysect{String construction for the ellipsoid}
It is now necessary to look more closely into the signs
of the radicals in (15). We know that, at least in a restricted
domain, three confocal quadrics will pass through each point.
In elliptic space one of these will be ruled, and the other two
not ruled; assuming, of course, that we are dealing with the
case of central quadrics. In hyperbolic space, two possible
cases can arise in the actual domain. If the developable be
%[File: 168.png]
real, two ruled, and one non-ruled hyperboloid will pass
through each point. If it be imaginary we shall have an
ellipsoid, a ruled, and a not-ruled hyperboloid.%
\footnote{See the Author's\index{Author} `Quadric Surfaces', p.~165.}
%endfootnote
Let us
confine ourselves to this case, taking $\lambda_3$ as the parameter of
the non-ruled hyperboloid, $\lambda_2$ as that of the ruled one, while $\lambda_1$
gives the ellipsoid\index{Ellipsoid}. The elliptic case will follow immediately
if we suppress the word \emph{hyperboloid}\index{Hyperboloid} substituting \emph{ellipsoid}.
In (15) let us assume that $\lambda$ refers to an ellipsoid, and $\lambda'$
to a ruled hyperboloid. In two of the three actual axial
planes we shall have real focal conics\index{Focal02@--- conics}. There will be a real
focal ellipse\index{Ellipse} which, looked upon as an envelope, constitutes
the transition between the ellipsoid and the ruled hyperboloid.
It will be surrounded by all ellipsoids, and surround all ruled
hyperboloids. If we take a point in this axial plane, without
the focal ellipse, the ellipsoid and non-ruled hyperboloid will
subsist, the ruled hyperboloid, looked upon as a point locus,
will shrink into the plane counted doubly. The other real
focal conic will be a hyperbola, and will serve as a transition
between the two sorts of hyperboloids, looked upon as
envelopes. It will surround the non-ruled hyperboloids, but
be surrounded by the ruled ones. The plane counted doubly,
will replace a non-ruled hyperboloid for each point without
the hyperbola\index{Hyperbola}. If a point be taken in the remaining axial
plane, this plane, counted doubly, will replace a non-ruled
hyperboloid for each of its points. Similar considerations
will hold in the elliptic case.

Once more, let us look at the signs of the terms in (15).
$d\lambda_i$ will change sign as a point passes through an axial plane
that counts doubly in the $\lambda_i$ family, or when passing along
a tangent to one of these surfaces, the point of contact is
traversed. On the other hand we see from (14) that when
$d\lambda_i$ changes sign, the radical associated with it in (15) changes
sign also, and vice versa. The radical associated with $d\lambda_3$
will change sign as we pass through a point of the axial plane
with an imaginary focal conic (which we shall call $\pi_3$), and
for a point of the axial plane $\pi_2$ of the focal hyperbola, which
is without this hyperbola. The radical with $d\lambda_2$ will change
sign for points of $\pi_1$, the plane of the focal ellipse without this
curve, or points of $\pi_2$ within the focal hyperbola. The radical
with $d\lambda_1$ will change sign for points of $\pi_1$ within the focal
ellipse.

We next suppose that a loop of inextensible thread is slung
about an ellipsoid $\lambda$, and a confocal, ruled, one-sheeted
hyperboloid $\lambda'$, and pulled taut at a point $P$. The loop is supposed
%[File: 169.png]
to surround the ellipsoid\index{Ellipsoid}, so that it winds partly on each of
the portions of the hyperboloid, which, in a restricted domain,
are separated by the ellipsoid. The form for the element of
length throughout the whole string will be that given by (15).
For when we pass from the ellipsoid to the hyperboloid\index{Hyperboloid} we
pass along a geodesic whose tangent touches both surfaces,
and this will be true throughout the continuation of that
geodesic, for a geodesic is traced by a line rolling on a quadric,
and touching a confocal one. The same form of distance
element will hold for the rectilinear parts of the loop. We
see, moreover, that two, and only two surfaces, of a confocal
system will touch any line; hence $\lambda$ and $\lambda'$ are the only two
which will touch the rectilinear parts of the loop. Lastly,
let us limit ourselves to those regions of the plane where the
various portions of the loop may be named in order: straight,
hyperboloidal, ellipsoidal, hyperboloidal, ellipsoidal, straight.
The constant length of the thread may be written
\[
C=\int_{\lambda_1}^{\lambda_1}F_1d\lambda_1 +
  \int_{\lambda_2}^{\lambda_2}F_2d\lambda_2 +
  \int_{\lambda_3}^{\lambda_3}F_3d\lambda_3.
\]

We see that $F_3$ can never vanish, for $\lambda$ and $\lambda'$ are the parameters
of an ellipsoid and ruled hyperboloid respectively,
while $\lambda_3$ refers to a non-ruled hyperboloid. It will become
infinite four times, twice when the loop passes $\pi_2$ the plane
of the focal\index{Focal02@--- conics} hyperbola\index{Hyperbola}, and twice when it passes $\pi_3$. We may,
however, integrate right up to these limits, and, as we have
seen, $d\lambda_3$ changes sign with the radical. We thus have
\begin{align*}
  \int_{\lambda_3}^{\lambda_3}F_3d\lambda_3 &=
  \int_{\lambda_3}^{c_3}F_3d\lambda_3 -
  \int_{c_3}^{c_2}F_3d\lambda_3 +
  \int_{c_2}^{c_3}F_3d\lambda_3 -
  \int_{c_3}^{c_2}F_3d\lambda_3 +
  \int_{c_2}^{\lambda_3}F_3d\lambda_3 \\
  &=4 \int_{c_2}^{c_3}F_3d\lambda_3 =\text{const}.
\end{align*}

We may approach the second integral in the same spirit.
$F_2$ will become infinite twice when the loop passes the plane
of the focal ellipse\index{Ellipse} $\pi_1$. It will vanish throughout those two
portions of the loop that lie on the ruled hyperboloid $\lambda_2 = \lambda'$,
and these two are separated by an intersection with $\pi_1$ We
have then
\begin{align*}
\int_{\lambda_2}^{\lambda_2}F_2d\lambda_2 &=
  \int_{\lambda_2}^{\lambda'}F_2d\lambda_2 -
  \int_{\lambda'}^{c_1}F_2d\lambda_2 +
  \int_{c_1}^{\lambda'}F_2d\lambda_2 -
  \int_{\lambda'}^{c_1}F_2d\lambda_2 +
  \int_{c_1}^{\lambda_2}F_2d\lambda_2 \\
 & =4 \int_{c_1}^{\lambda'}F_2d\lambda_2 =\text{const}.
\end{align*}

%[File: 170.png]
We must, in conclusion, consider the first integral. It will
never become infinite, but will vanish along those two portions
of the loop which lie on the ellipsoid\index{Ellipsoid} $\lambda = \lambda_1$. We have
therefore:
\[
  \int_{\lambda_1}^{\lambda_1} F_1 d\lambda_1
= \int_{\lambda_1}^{\lambda  } F_1 d\lambda_1
- \int_{\lambda  }^{\lambda_1} F_1 d\lambda_1
=2\int_{\lambda_1}^{\lambda  } F_1 d\lambda_1 = \phi(\lambda_1).
\]
We have therefore, since the first two integrals and the
sum are constant,
\[
  \phi(\lambda_1) = \text{\ const.,}
\]
and the locus of the moving point is an ellipsoid. Lastly, let
the ellipsoid and hyperboloid\index{Hyperboloid} shrink down to the focal\index{Focal02@--- conics} ellipse
and focal hyperbola\index{Hyperbola} respectively, we have in the limiting case:

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~9. If an ellipse and hyperbola in mutually
perpendicular planes pass each through two foci of the other, and
if a loop of inextensible thread\index{Thread construction} be slung around the ellipse
and pulled taut at a point $P$ in such a way that it meets
the two curves alternately, then the locus of $P$ will be an
ellipsoid confocal with the given ellipse and hyperbola.

%[File: 171.png]
%CHAPTER XIV
\mychap{XIV}{AREAS AND VOLUMES}
\mysect{Amplitude of a triangle}

The subjects \emph{area}\index{Area01@Area} and \emph{volume} offer some of the most
striking points of disparity between euclidean and non-euclidean
geometry.%
\footnote{For a bibliographical account of the subject-matter of the present chapter
see the dissertation of Dannmeyer\index{Dannmeyer}, \emph{Die Oberfl\"achen- und Volumenberechnung f\"ur
Lobatschefskijsche R\"aume}, G\"ottingen, 1904.}
%endfootnote
A first notable difference arises from
the fact that, in the non-euclidean cases, two different functions
of a triangle\index{Triangle} appear to play the r\^ole of the euclidean
area. The first is present in the analoga of those formulae
which give the area in terms of the sides and angles; the
second appears when the area is defined as the limit of a
sum, i.e.\ as a definite integral. We shall reserve the name
\emph{area} for the second of these, giving to the first the name
\emph{amplitude}\index{Amplitude of triangle}.%
\footnote{The concept \emph{amplitude} of a triangle, and the various trigonometric identities
connected with it, are taken directly from an admirable paper by
D'Ovidio\index{Ovidio@d'Ovidio}, `Su varie questioni di metrica proiettiva,' \emph{Atti della R. Accademia
delle Scienze di Torino}, vol.~xxviii, 1893. Unfortunately the author gives, p.~20,
an incorrect formula for the volume of a tetrahedron.}
%endfootnote

Let us, as in elementary geometry, use the letters $A$, $B$, $C$
to indicate, either the vertices of a triangle, or the measures
of its angles. We assume that these points are real, and,
in the hyperbolic case, situated in the actual domain. We
shall define triangle as in Chapter II\@. We might carry
through the same sort of work for any three points, but,
as we saw in the closing pages of Chapter VII, we should
thereby be compelled, in the hyperbolic case at least, to
introduce certain very delicate considerations as to algebraic;
sign, not only in our analytic expressions, but even in the
trigonometric formulae first introduced in Chapter IV.

We begin by rewriting IV\@. 9
\[
 -\sin \frac{b}{k} \sin\frac{c}{k} \cos A =
  \cos \frac{b}{k} \cos\frac{c}{k}-\cos\frac{a}{k}.
\]

This formula, established for one region, is seen at once to
hold for all the others.
%[File: 172.png]
\begin{align*}
\sin \frac{b}{k} \sin\frac{c}{k} &\sin A \\
& = \Bigl[ \sin^2\frac{b}{k} \sin^2\frac{c}{k} -
           \cos^2\frac{b}{k} \cos^2\frac{c}{k} +
          2\cos\frac{a}{k} \cos\frac{b}{k} \cos\frac{c}{k} -
           \cos^2\frac{a}{k} \Bigr]^{\frac{1}{2}} \\
& = \Bigl[ 1 - \cos^2\frac{a}{k} - \cos^2\frac{b}{k} -
           \cos^2\frac{c}{k} +
          2\cos\frac{a}{k} \cos\frac{b}{k} \cos\frac{c}{k}
    \Bigr]^{\frac{1}{2}}.
\end{align*}

The right-hand side is symmetrical in the three letters $a$, $b$, $c$,
so that we may write
\begin{multline*}
  \sin \frac{b}{k} \sin \frac{c}{k} \sin A =
  \sin \frac{c}{k} \sin \frac{a}{k} \sin B =
  \sin \frac{a}{k} \sin \frac{b}{k} \sin C
\\
\tag{1}
= \begin{vmatrix}
    1               & \cos\dfrac{c}{k} & \cos\dfrac{b}{k} \\
    \mystrut{18}\cos\dfrac{c}{k} & 1               & \cos\dfrac{a}{k} \\
    \mystrut{18}\cos\dfrac{b}{k} & \cos\dfrac{a}{k} & 1
  \end{vmatrix}^{\frac{1}{2}}.
\end{multline*}

In the real domain, if the measures of sides and angles be
taken positively, the left side is essentially negative in the
hyperbolic case, and positive in the elliptic, so that the
radical on the right must be chosen accordingly. It will
vanish only when the three points are collinear (under the
restrictions made at the outset of this chapter), and shall be
called the \emph{Sine Amplitude}\index{Amplitude of triangle} of the triangle, written
$\sin (ABC)$.

Let the reader show that if the coordinates of $A$, $B$, $C$ be
$(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$ respectively
\begin{equation*}
\tag{2}
\sin (ABC) =
  \frac{\begin{vmatrix}
  (xx) & (xy) & (xz) \\
  (yx) & (yy) & (yz) \\
  (zx) & (zy) & (zz) \end{vmatrix}^{\frac{1}{2}}}
  {\sqrt{(xx)}\;\sqrt{(yy)}\;\sqrt{(zz)}} =
  \frac{\lvert x y z \rvert}
  {\sqrt{(xx)}\;\sqrt{(yy)}\;\sqrt{(zz)}}.
\end{equation*}

\mysect{Relation to other parts}
We may rewrite (1) in the form
\begin{equation*}
\tag{3}
  \frac{\sin A}{\mystrut{13.5}\sin\dfrac{a}{k}} =
  \frac{\sin B}{\sin\dfrac{b}{k}} =
  \frac{\sin C}{\mystrut{13.5}\sin\dfrac{c}{k}} \equiv
  \frac{\sin(ABC)}{\sin\dfrac{a}{k}\sin\dfrac{b}{k}\sin\dfrac{c}{k}}.
\end{equation*}

If $A'$, $B'$, $C'$ be the points where the sides of the triangle
meet the perpendiculars from the vertices, we have
\begin{equation*}
\tag{4}
  \sin\frac{a}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{AA'}}{k} =
  \sin\frac{b}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{BB'}}{k} =
  \sin\frac{c}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{CC'}}{k} =
  \sin(ABC).
\end{equation*}
%[File: 173.png]
We see at once the close analogy of the sine amplitude\index{Amplitude of triangle} of a
non-euclidean triangle\index{Triangle} to double area of a euclidean triangle.
Let the reader show that
\[
  \Limdot\frac{1}{k^2} = 0,\quad k^2
  \sin (ABC) = 2\text{\ Area }\triangle ABC.
\]

A function correlative to the sine amplitude may be
obtained from the correlative formula
\begin{align*}
  \sin B \sin C \cos \frac{a}{k} = \cos B &\cos C + \cos A.
\\
  \sin B \sin C \sin \frac{a}{k}
= \sin C \sin A \sin \frac{b}{k}
&= \sin A \sin B \sin \frac{c}{k}
\\
\hfill &= \begin{vmatrix}   1   & \cos C & \cos B  \\
                         \cos C &    1   & \cos A  \\
                         \cos B & \cos A &    1
          \end{vmatrix}^{\frac{1}{2}}
\\
       &= \sin (abc).                 \tag{5}
\end{align*}
This $> 0$ in the elliptic case, pure imaginary in the hyperbolic
\[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{a}{k} }{\sin A}
= \frac{\sin\dfrac{b}{k} }{\sin B}
= \frac{\sin\dfrac{c}{k} }{\sin C}
= \frac{\sin(abc) }{\sin A\sin B\sin C}.   \tag{6}
\]
\[
\sin A \sin\frac{\overline{AA'}}{k}
= \sin B \sin\frac{\overline{BB'}}{k}
= \sin C \sin\frac{\overline{CC'}}{k} = \sin(abc).   \tag{7}
\]
\[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{a}{k}}{\sin A}
= \frac{\sin\dfrac{b}{k}}{\sin B}
= \frac{\sin\dfrac{c}{k}}{\sin C}
= \frac{\sin(ABC)}{\sin(abc)}.   \tag{8}
\]
\[
  \sin(abc)
= \frac{\sin^2(ABC)}
       {\sin\dfrac{a}{k} \sin\dfrac{b}{k} \sin\dfrac{c}{k}}, \quad
  \sin(ABC)
= \frac{\sin^2(abc)}
       {\sin A \sin B \sin C}.   \tag{9}
\]


\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent If}  &&a + b + c = 2s, &\phantom{\text{\indent If}}
\end{flalign*}
\begin{align*}
  \cos A &= \frac{\cos\dfrac{a}{k} - \cos\dfrac{b}{k} \cos\dfrac{c}{k} }
                {\sin\dfrac{b}{k} \sin\dfrac{c}{k}                   },
\\
  \sin\tfrac{1}{2}A
&= \left[ \frac{\sin\dfrac{s-b}{k} \sin\dfrac{s-c}{k} }
              {\sin\dfrac{b  }{k} \sin\dfrac{c  }{k} }
  \right]^{\frac{1}{2}},
\\
%[File: 174.png]
\cos \tfrac{1}{2} A &=
\left[ \frac{\sin \dfrac{s}{k} \sin \dfrac{s-a}{k}}
{\sin \dfrac{b}{k} \sin \dfrac{c}{k}} \right]^\frac{1}{2}, \\
\ctn \tfrac{1}{2} A &=
\left[ \frac{\sin \dfrac{s}{k} \sin \dfrac{s-a}{k}}
{\sin \dfrac{s-b}{k} \sin \dfrac{s-c}{k}} \right]^\frac{1}{2},
\end{align*}
\[
\sin (ABC)=2 \sqrt{\sin \frac{s}{k} \sin \frac{s-a}{k} \sin \frac{s-b}{k} \sin \frac{s-c}{k}}. \tag{10}
\]

In like manner, let us put\index{Amplitude of triangle}
\begin{align*}
&A+B+C = 2\sigma. \\
\sin \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{a}{k} &= \left[
\frac{-\cos \sigma \cos (\sigma-A)}{\sin B \sin C}
\right]^\frac{1}{2}, \\
\cos \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{a}{k} &= \left[
\frac{\cos (\sigma-B) \cos (\sigma-C)}{\sin B \sin C}
\right]^\frac{1}{2}, \\
\ctn \tfrac{1}{2} \frac{a}{k} &= \left[
\frac{\cos (\sigma-B) \cos (\sigma-c)}{-\cos \sigma \cos (\sigma-a)}
\right]^\frac{1}{2},
\end{align*}
\[
\sin (abc)=2 \sqrt{-\cos \sigma \cos (\sigma-A) \cos (\sigma-B) \cos (\sigma-C)}.\tag{11}
\]
\begin{equation*}
\sin \tfrac{1}{2}A \sin \tfrac{1}{2}B \sin \tfrac{1}{2}C=
\frac{\Bigl(\sin \dfrac{s-a}{k} \sin \dfrac{s-b}{k} \sin \dfrac{s-c}{k}\Bigr)}
{\sin \dfrac{a}{k} \sin \dfrac{b}{k} \sin \dfrac{c}{k}},
\end{equation*}
\begin{align*}
\sin \frac{s}{k} &= \frac{\sin (abc)}{4 \sin \tfrac{1}{2}A \sin \tfrac{1}{2}B \sin \tfrac{1}{2}C}. \tag{12} \\
-\cos \sigma &= \frac{\sin (ABC)}{4 \cos \tfrac{1}{2} \dfrac{a}{k} \cos \tfrac{1}{2}
\dfrac{b}{k} \cos \tfrac{1}{2} \dfrac{c}{k}}. \tag{13}
\end{align*}

It should be noticed that the denominator on the right of
equation (13) is essentially positive. The numerator is
negative in the hyperbolic case, as we have already seen,
%[File: 175.png]
but here also $\sigma < \dfrac{\pi}{2}$ and $\cos \sigma > 0$.
In the elliptic case the
numerator is positive but $\sigma > \dfrac{\pi}{2}$, $\cos \sigma < 0$.

In Chapter III we defined as the \emph{discrepancy}\index{Discrepancy of a triangle}
of a triangle\index{Triangle},
the absolute value of the difference between the sum of the
measures of the angles and $\pi$. Let us now define as the
\emph{excess}\index{Excess of a triangle} of our triangle the expression
\[
  e = A + B + C - \pi.
\]

This will have the same sign as $\dfrac{1}{k^2}$ the measure of curvature
of space. We have
\[
  \sin\frac{e}{2} = -\cos\sigma
= \frac{\sin(ABC)}{4\cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{a}{k}
                    \cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{b}{k}
                    \cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{c}{k} }.   \tag{14}
\]

\mysect{Limiting form when the triangle is infinitesimal}
Passing to the limiting case where the triangle becomes
infinitesimal\index{Infinitesimal domain}, we have
\begin{align*}
\Limdot \frac{\sin(ABC)}{\sin\dfrac{e}{2}}
&= 4\limdot \left( \cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{a}{k}
                      \cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{b}{k}
                      \cos\tfrac{1}{2}\dfrac{c}{k} \right)
\\
&= 4
\\
  \limdot\, e &= \tfrac{1}{2}\limdot\, (ABC)
\\
&= \frac{1}{2k^2}\limdot bc \sin A
\\
&= \frac{1}{2k^2}\limdot\, a\, \overline{AA'}.
\end{align*}

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~1. In an infinitesimal triangle the limit of the
ratio of the excess to the product of the euclidean area and
the measure of curvature of space is unity.

Let us next examine the infinitesimal quadrilateral\index{Quadrilateral01@Quadrilateral}, whose
vertices are $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$. $AB$ and $CD$ shall intersect in $H$ (actual
or ideal) while $AC$ and $BD$ intersect in $K$; the latter two
points remaining at a finite distance from $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$.
\[
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{AB}}{k}}
       {\sin\dfrac{\overline{BK}}{k}}
= \frac{\sin K}{\sin A},
\quad
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{CD}}{k}}
       {\sin\dfrac{\overline{DK}}{k}}
= \frac{\sin K}{\sin C},
\]
\[
  \limdot \frac{\sin A}{\sin C} = 1,\quad
  \limdot \frac{\overline{AB}}{\overline{CD}} = 1.
\tag{Ch.~III.~2.}
\]
%[File: 176.png]
\begin{eqnarray*}
    \limdot \frac{\sin(CAB)}{\sin(DAB)}
&=& \limdot \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{AB}}{k}\sin\dfrac{\overline{AC}}{k}\sin A}
             {\sin\dfrac{\overline{DB}}{k}\sin\dfrac{\overline{DC}}{k}\sin D} \\
&=& \limdot \frac{\overline{AB}\,.\,\overline{AC}\,.\,\sin A}
             {\overline{DB}\,.\,\overline{DC}\,.\,\sin D} \\
&=& 1.
\end{eqnarray*}

\mysect{Deficiency and area}
We shall define as the \emph{area}\index{Area01@Area}\index{Area05@--- of a triangle}
of an infinitesimal\index{Infinitesimal domain} triangle\index{Triangle}
the common value of $k^2$ times its excess\index{Excess of a triangle}, its half-amplitude,
and the euclidean expression for its area.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~2. If the opposite sides of an infinitesimal
quadrilateral do not intersect in points infinitesimally near
the vertices, the limit of the ratio of the areas of the triangles
into which it is divided by a diagonal is unity.

The sum of these two infinitesimal areas shall be called
the \emph{area} of the infinitesimal quadrilateral; it will be equal
(always neglecting infinitesimals of higher order) to the
product of two adjacent sides multiplied into the sine of the
included angle.

Suppose now that we have a region of the plane, connex
right up to the boundary, which is limited by one or more
closed curves, and let this be covered by a network of infinitesimal
quadrilaterals of the sort just described. Let the
area of each of these be multiplied by the value for a point
therein of a continuous function of the coordinates of the
point. The limit of this sum as the individual areas tend
uniformly toward zero shall be called the \emph{surface integral}\index{Surface integral} of
the given function for the given area. The proof of the
existence of such a limit, and its independence of network
employed will be identical with that used in the corresponding
euclidean case, and need not detain us here.%
\footnote{Conf.\ e.g. Picard\index{Picard}, \emph{Trait\'e d'Analyse}, first ed., Paris, 1891, vol.~i, pp.~83--102.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Definition}.~When the surface integral of the function $1$
exists over a region of the plane, that integral shall be defined
as the \emph{area} of the region.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~3. The area of a region of a plane is the sum of
the areas of any two regions into which it may be divided
provided that these two have no common area.

This follows immediately from the definition given above.
As an application of these principles let us determine the
%[File: 177.png]
area of a triangle\index{Area05@--- of a triangle}\index{Triangle}.
It is the limit of the sum of the areas of
a network of infinitesimal triangles, or by (1) the limit
of the sum of $k^2$ times their excesses\index{Excess of a triangle}. Now it is perfectly
clear that if a triangle be divided in two by a segment whose
extremities are a vertex and a point of the opposite side, the
excess of the original triangle is the sum of the excesses of
the parts, and we may establish our network by a repetition
of this process of division\label{processor}, hence%
\footnote{It is surprising to see how unsatisfactory are the proofs usually given for
this, the best-known theorem of non-euclidean geometry. In Frischauf\index{Frischauf},
\emph{Elemente der absoluten Geometrie}, Leipzig, 1876, will be found a geometrical proof
applicable to the hyperbolic case but not, so far as I can see, to the elliptic,
and the same remark will apply to the book of Liebmann\index{Lindemann}, cit. Manning\index{Manning},
loc.\ cit., makes an attempt at a general proof, but the use of intuition is
scarcely disguised. In Clebsch-Lendemann\index{Clebsch}, \emph{Vorlesungen \"uber Geometrie}, Leipzig,
1891, vol.~ii, p.~49, is a proof by integration, but the analysis is unnecessarily
complicated owing to the fact that, apparently, the author overlooked the
consideration that it is sufficient to prove the theorem for a right triangle.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~4. The area of a triangle is the quotient of the
excess divided by the measure of curvature of space\index{Measure02@--- of curvature of
space}\index{K@$\dfrac{1}{k^2}$ measure of curvature of space}\index{Curvature06@--- of space}.

\mysect{Area found by integration}
Let us give a second demonstration of this fundamental
theorem with the aid of integration. It will be sufficient to
do so in the case of a right triangle, and we shall take a right
triangle with one angle at $C$ the intersection of $x_1 = 0$, $x_2 = 0$,
the right angle being at $B$ a point of the axis $x_2 = 0$. We
may introduce polar coordinates\index{Coordinates02@Co-ordinates of a point}
%
\begin{equation*}
\frac{x_1}{x_0} = k\tan \frac{r}{k}\cos\phi, \quad
\frac{x_2}{x_0} = k\tan \frac{r}{k}\sin\phi,
\end{equation*}
%
the elements of arc along $\phi = $ const.\  and $r = $ const.\  will be
$dr$ and $k\sin\dfrac{r}{k}d\phi$ respectively. The element of area will be
%
\[
df = k\sin\frac{r}{k}dr\,d\phi. \tag{15}
\]
\[
k \int^R_0 \sin\frac{r}{k}dr = k^2\left(1-\cos\frac{R}{k}\right),
\]
\[
\tan\frac{R}{k} = \tan\frac{\overline{BC}}{k}\sec\phi. \tag{Ch. IV\@. (6).}
\]
\[
\cos\frac{R}{k} = \frac{\cos\phi}{\sqrt{\cos^2\phi + \tan^2\dfrac{\overline{BC}}{k}}}.
\]

Remembering that the limits for $\phi$ are $0$ and $C$
%[File: 178.png]
\begin{equation*}
\text{Area} = k^2\int^C_0 d\phi
   - k^2\int^C_0 \frac{\cos\phi\,d\phi}{\sqrt{\cos^2\phi + \tan^2\dfrac{\overline{BC}}{k}}}.
\end{equation*}\index{Area05@--- of a triangle}\index{Triangle}
%
The first integral is $k^2C$. If, further, we put $\sin\phi = x$,
%
\begin{equation*}
  \int\frac{dx}{\sqrt{\sec^2\dfrac{\overline{BC}}{k} - x^2}}
= \sin^{-1}\Bigl[x\cos\frac{\overline{BC}}{k}\Bigr] + \text{const.}
\end{equation*}
%
Hence our second integral will be
%
\begin{equation*}
-k^2\left\{\sin^{-1}\left[\sin\phi\cos\frac{\overline{BC}}{k}\right]\right\}^C_0.
\end{equation*}
%
This vanishes at the lower limit. On the other hand by
Chapter IV\@. (7)
%
\begin{equation*}
\cos A = \sin C \cos \frac{\overline{BC}}{k},
\end{equation*}
%
our second integral becomes
%
\[
-k^2\left[\frac{\pi}{2} - A\right] = k^2\left[A + B - \pi\right],
\]
\[
\text{Area} = k^2(A + B + C - \pi). \tag{16}
\]


Two regions with the same area may, naturally, have very
different shapes. There are, however, three simple cases
where the equivalence of area is immediately evident. First,
where the two figures are congruent; second, when they are
composed of the same number of non-overlapping sub-regions
(i.e.\ sub-regions no two of which have in common a region
which has an area) congruent in pairs; third, where by the
adjunction of pairs of mutually congruent non-overlapping
sub-regions to them, they may be transformed into congruent
regions. In this latter case they may be said to be \emph{equivalent
by completion.}%
\footnote{The term \emph{equivalent by completion} is borrowed from
Halsted\index{Halsted}, loc.\ cit., p.~109.
The distinction between \emph{equivalent} and \emph{equivalent by completion} is, I believe, due
to Hilbert\index{Hilbert}, loc.\ cit., p.~40. For an admirable discussion of the question of
area see Amaldi\index{Amaldi}, in the fifth article in Enriques\index{Enriques},
\emph{Questioni riguardanti la geometria elementare}, Bologna, 1900.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Definition}.~Given $n$ successive coplanar segments $(A_1A_2)$,
$(A_kA_{k+1})$, $(A_{n-1}$ $A_1)$ so situated that no line other than one
through a point $A_i$ can contain points of more than two of the
segments; the assemblage of all points of all segments whose
%[File: 179.png]
extremities are points of the given segments shall be called a
\emph{convex polygon} or, more simply, a polygon\index{Polygon}. The definition
of sides, vertices, and angles is immediate. If one vertex, say
$A_1$ be connected with all the others, the polygon will be
divided into $n-2$ triangles, no two of which have in common
any area. The area\index{Area01@Area} of the polygon\index{Area04@--- of a polygon} will thus be the sum of
the areas of these triangles. We may convince ourselves of
the compatibility of these statements as follows. A triangle
is certainly a polygon, and if a polygon of $n-1$ sides exist,
we may easily enlarge it to have $n$ sides by taking an additional
vertex near one side. On the other hand, if a polygon
of $n-1$ sides may be divided up in the manner suggested,
it is immediately evident that one of $n$ sides may be so
divided also.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~5. The area of a convex polygon is the quotient of
the excess\index{Excess of a triangle} of the sum of its angles over $(n-2)\pi$ divided by
the measure of curvature of Space.

\mysect{Area of circle}
Let the reader show that the area of a proper circle\index{Circle01@Circle}\index{Area02@--- of a circle} is
\begin{equation*}
\tag{17}
2\pi k^2\left( 1 - \cos\frac{r}{k} \right).
\end{equation*}
\mysect{Area of whole elliptic or spherical plane}
The total areas of the elliptic and the spherical planes\index{Area03@--- of a plane} will be
respectively
\[
2\pi k^2, \quad 4\pi k^2.
\]
In the hyperbolic plane regions may be found having any
desired area.

\mysect{Amplitude of a tetrahedron}
Our next undertaking shall be to see how far the methods
which we have established for studying areas are applicable
in three dimensions. We shall begin, as before, with amplitudes,
following, however, an analytical rather than a trigonometric
method.

Let the vertices of a tetrahedron, as defined in Chapter II,
be $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$ with the coordinates $(x)$, $(y)$, $(z)$, $(t)$ respectively.
The opposite faces shall be $\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$, $\delta$ with coordinates $(u)$, $(v)$,
$(w)$, $(\omega)$, so that, e.g.
\[
r(\omega X) \equiv (X\,xyz).
\]
We shall define as sine amplitude of the tetrahedron\index{Amplitude of tetrahedron}
\begin{align*}
\sin (ABCD)
&= \left\lvert \cos\frac{\overline{AA}}{k}
               \cos\frac{\overline{BB}}{k}
               \cos\frac{\overline{CC}}{k}
               \cos\frac{\overline{DD}}{k} \right\rvert^{\frac{1}{2}} \\
&= \frac{\lvert (xx)(yy)(zz)(tt) \rvert^{\frac{1}{2}}    }
        {\sqrt{(xx)} \sqrt{(yy)} \sqrt{(zz)} \sqrt{(tt)} } \\
%[File: 180.png]
&= \frac{\lvert xyzt \rvert}
       {\sqrt{(xx)} \sqrt{(yy)} \sqrt{(zz)} \sqrt{(tt)}}. \tag{18}
\end{align*}

\mysect{Relation to other parts}
We shall give to the radicals involved such signs that
$k$ sine amplitude shall have the sign of $k^2$. Recalling the
concept of the moment of two lines introduced in Chapter IX,
we get
\[
\tag{19}
\sin\frac{\overline{AB}}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{CD}}{k}
  (\Moment AB,CD)=\sin(ABCD).
\]
\[
\sin(ABC)
= \frac{\lvert (xx)(yy)(zz) \rvert^{\frac{1}{2}}}
       {\sqrt{(xx)}\sqrt{(yy)}\sqrt{(zz)}}.
\]

Let $A'$, $B'$, $C'$, $D'$ be the points where perpendiculars from
the vertices of a tetrahedron meet the opposite faces. Then
\begin{align*}
\sin\frac{\overline{DD'}}{k} &=
  \frac{\lvert xyzt \rvert}
  {\sqrt{(tt)}\,\lvert (xx)(yy)(zz) \rvert^{\frac{1}{2}}},
\\
  \sin(BCD)\sin\frac{\overline{AA'}}{k} =&
  \sin(CDA)\sin\frac{\overline{BB'}}{k} =
  \sin(DBA)\sin\frac{\overline{CC'}}{k}
\\
\tag{20}
=& \sin(ABC)\sin\frac{\overline{DD'}}{k} = \sin(ABCD).
\end{align*}

If we mean by $\measuredangle \alpha\beta$ the dihedral angle of these two faces
\begin{align*}
\cos\measuredangle \alpha\beta &= \frac{(uv)}{\sqrt{(uu)}\,\sqrt{(vv)}}
\\
&= \frac{\begin{vmatrix}
  (xy) & (xz) & (xt) \\
  (zy) & (zz) & (zt) \\
  (ty) & (tz) & (tt) \end{vmatrix} }
  {\sqrt{\dfrac{\partial\lvert (xx)(yy)(zz)(tt) \rvert}{\partial(xx)}}\;
   \sqrt{\dfrac{\partial\lvert (xx)(yy)(zz)(tt) \rvert}{\partial(yy)}}
  },
\end{align*}
\[
\sin\measuredangle\alpha\beta =
  \frac{\sin(ABCD)\sin\dfrac{\overline{AB}}{k}}
       {\sin(BCD)\sin(ACD)},
\]
\[
\tag{21}
  \sin(BCD) \sin(ACD)
  \frac{\sin\measuredangle\alpha\beta}
       {\sin\dfrac{\overline{AB}}{k}} = \sin(ABCD).
\]

The geometry of lines through a point is an example of the
%[File: 181.png]
geometry of the elliptic plane, where $k^2 = 1$. We may thus
speak of the sine amplitude of a trihedral angle
\begin{align*}
\sin(AB, AC, AD) &=
  \frac{\begin{vmatrix}
          \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tx) \\ (xt) & (xx) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (xt) & (xy) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tz) \\ (xt) & (xz) \end{vmatrix}
        \smallskip \\
          \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (xt) & (xy) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (yt) & (yy) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tz) \\ (yt) & (yz) \end{vmatrix}
        \smallskip \\
          \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tx) \\ (zt) & (zx) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (zt) & (zy) \end{vmatrix}
        & \begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tz) \\ (zt) & (zz) \end{vmatrix}
        \end{vmatrix}^{\frac{1}{2}}                                 }
    {\sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tx) \\ (xt) & (xx) \end{vmatrix}}\;
     \sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (yt) & (yy) \end{vmatrix}}\;
     \sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tz) \\ (zt) & (zz) \end{vmatrix}}} \\
&= \frac{(tt)\, \lvert xyzt \rvert}
    {\sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tx) \\ (xt) & (xx) \end{vmatrix}}\;
     \sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (ty) \\ (yt) & (yy) \end{vmatrix}}\;
     \sqrt{\begin{vmatrix} (tt) & (tz) \\ (zt) & (zz) \end{vmatrix}}}.
\end{align*}
\[
\tag{22}
  \sin\frac{\overline{DA}}{k}
  \sin\frac{\overline{DB}}{k}
  \sin\frac{\overline{DC}}{k}
  \sin(AB,AC,AD) = \sin(ABCD).
\]

The reader will not fail to notice in formulae (19), (20), and
(22) the striking analogy between the sine amplitude\index{Amplitude of tetrahedron} and six
times the euclidean volume. There will be a function correlative
to $\sin (ABCD)$ which we shall call $\sin (\alpha\beta\gamma\delta)$.
\[
\tag{23}
  \sin\measuredangle\alpha\beta
  \sin\measuredangle(\gamma\delta) (\Moment AB, CD) =
  \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta).
\]
\[
\tag{24}
  \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma)\sin\frac{\overline{DD'}}{k}
= \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta).
\]
\[
\tag{25}
  \sin(\alpha\gamma\delta)\sin(\beta\gamma\delta)
  \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{AB}}{k}}
       {\sin\measuredangle\alpha\beta} =
  \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta).
\]
\[
\tag{26}
  \sin\measuredangle\alpha\delta
  \sin\measuredangle\beta\delta
  \sin\measuredangle\gamma\delta
  \sin(\alpha\delta,\beta\delta,\gamma\delta) =
  \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta).
\]
\[
\tag{27}
  \frac{\sin(BCD )}{\sin(\beta \gamma\delta)} =
  \frac{\sin(CDA )}{\sin(\gamma\delta\alpha)} =
  \frac{\sin(DBA )}{\sin(\delta\beta \alpha)} =
  \frac{\sin(ABC )}{\sin(\alpha\beta \gamma)} =
  \frac{\sin(ABCD)}{\sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta)}.
\]

Our two tetrahedral functions are connected by the relations
\begin{align*}
\sin(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta) &=
  \frac{\sin^3(ABCD)}
       {\sin(BCD)\sin(CDA)\sin(DBA)\sin(ABC)},
\\
\sin(ABCD) &=
  \frac{\sin^3(\alpha\beta\gamma\delta)}
       {\sin(\beta\gamma\delta) \sin(\gamma\delta\alpha)
        \sin(\delta\beta\alpha) \sin(\alpha\beta\gamma)}.
\end{align*}

%[File: 182.png]
\mysect{Simple form for the differential of volume of a tetrahedron} % ??
The analogy between the sine amplitude\index{Amplitude of tetrahedron} and the sextuple
of the euclidean expression for the volume appears even more
distinctly in the infinitesimal domain.
\begin{align*}
   \Limdot\sin (ABC)
&= \frac{1}{k^2}\overline{AB}\,.\, \overline{AC}\,.\, \sin \measuredangle BAC
\\
&= \frac{2}{k^2}\Area \triangle ABC.
\\
   \Limdot\sin (ABCD)
&= \limdot\, (ABCD)
\\
&= \frac{6}{k^3} \Voltet ABCD.
\tag{28}
\end{align*}

Following our previous analogy, suppose that we have six
planes, no three coaxal, passing by fours through four actual
or ideal, but not collinear points. Let the remaining intersections
be at a finite distance from the three chosen points,
but infinitesimally near one another. An infinitesimal
region will thus be formed, on the analogy of a euclidean
parallelepiped, which may be divided into six tetrahedra\index{Tetrahedron} of
such sort that the limit of the ratio of the sine amplitudes, or
of the euclidean volumes\index{Volume01@Volume}, of any two is unity. Six times the
euclidean volume of any one of these tetrahedra may be defined
as the euclidean volume of the region.

So far the analogy between two and three dimensions has
been sufficiently good. Each time we have had a function
called \emph{sine amplitude} corresponding in many particulars to
a simple multiple of the euclidean area or volume, and approaching
a multiple of the area or volume as a limit,
when the figure becomes infinitesimal. In the plane there
appeared, besides half the sine amplitude and the euclidean
area, a third expression, namely, the discrepancy or excess.
In three dimensions this function is, sad to relate, entirely
lacking; that is to say, there is no simple function of the
measures of a tetrahedron which possesses the property that
when one tetrahedron is the logical sum of two others, the
function of the sum is the sum of the functions. It is the
lack of this function that renders the problem of non-euclidean
volumes difficult.%
\footnote{It is highly interesting that in four dimensions a function playing the
role of the discrepancy appears once more. See Dehn\index{Dehn}, `Die eulersche Formel
in Zusammenhang mit dem Inhalt in der nicht-euklidischen Geometrie,'
\emph{Mathematische Annalen}, vol.~lxi, 1906.}
%endfootnote

Suppose, in general, that we have a three dimensional
region connex up to the boundary, and that we divide it
%[File: 183.png]
into a number of extremely tiny tetrahedra\index{Tetrahedron}. The limit of
the sum of the euclidean volume\index{Volume05@--- of tetrahedron} of each, multiplied by the
value for a point therein of a continuous function of the
coordinates of that point, as all the volumes approach zero
uniformly, shall be called the \emph{volume integral}\index{Volume02@--- integral} for that region
of that function. The proofs for the existence of that volume
integral, and its independence of the method of subdivision,
are analogous to those already referred to for the surface
integral. In particular, the volume integral of the function
unity shall be called the \emph{volume}\index{Volume01@Volume} of the region. Two regions
will have the same volume if they be congruent, made up
of the same number of parts, mutually congruent in pairs,
or if by the adjunction of such pairs they may be completed
to be congruent.

If the limiting surface of a region be made up of a series of
plane surfaces, and if no line, not lying in a plane of the
surface, can contain more than two points of the surface, then
it is easy to show that the region may be divided up into a
number of tetrahedra, and the problem of finding the volume of
any such region reduces to the problem of finding the volume
of a tetrahedron. This problem may, in turn, be reduced
to that of finding the volume of a tetrahedron of particularly
simple structure. To begin with, we may assume that there
is one face which makes with the three others dihedral angles
whose measures are less than $\dfrac{\pi}{2}$, for the bisectors of the
dihedral angles of the original tetrahedron will always divide
it into smaller tetrahedra possessing this property. The perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}
on the plane of this face, from the opposite vertex,
will, then, pass through a point within the face, and, with the
help of this perpendicular, we may subdivide into three
smaller tetrahedra, for each of which the line of one edge is
perpendicular to the plane of one face.

Consider, next, a tetrahedron where the line of one edge
is indeed perpendicular to the plane of a face. There are
two possibilities. First, in the plane of this face neither of
the face angles whose vertex is not at the foot of the
perpendicular is obtuse; secondly, one of these angles is
obtuse. (The case where both were obtuse could not occur
in a small region.) In the first case we might draw a line
from the foot of the perpendicular to a point of the opposite
edge in this particular face, perpendicular to the line of
that edge, and thus, by a familiar theorem in elementary
geometry, which holds equally in the non-euclidean case,
divide the tetrahedron into two others, each of which possesses
%[File: 184.png]
the property that the lines of two opposite edges are perpendicular
to two of the faces. These we shall for the moment
call \emph{simplest type}. In the second case, from the vertex of the
obtuse angle mentioned, draw a line perpendicular to the line
of the opposite edge in this particular face (and passing
through a point within this edge), and connect the intersection
with the vertex opposite this face. The tetrahedron\index{Tetrahedron} will be
divided up into a tetrahedron of the simplest type, and one
of the sort considered in case 1. We have, then, merely to
consider the volume of a tetrahedron\index{Volume05@--- of tetrahedron} of the simplest type.

Let the vertices of the tetrahedron be $A$, $B$, $C$, $D$, where $AB$
is perpendicular to $BCD$ and $DC$ perpendicular to $ABC$. Let a
plane perpendicular to $AB$ contain a point $B_1$ of $(AB)$ whose
distance from $A$ shall have the measure $x$; while this plane
meets $(AC)$ and $(AD)$ in $C_1$ and $D_1$ respectively. The volume
of the region bounded by this plane, and an adjacent one of
the same type and the three faces through $A$, will be $dx$, multiplied
by the surface integral over the $\triangle B_1 C_1 D_1$ of the cosine
of the $k^{\text{th}}$ part of the distance of a point from $B_1$ (Cf.\ Ch.
IV\@. (2).) This integral takes a striking form.%
\footnote{The integration which follows is a very special case of a much more
general one for \emph{n} dimensions given by Schl\"afli\index{Schl\"afli}, \emph{Theorie der vielfachen Kontinuit\"at},
Zurich, 1901, p.~646. This paper of Schl\"afli's is posthumous; it was originally
written in 1855, when the science of non-euclidean geometry had not reached
its present recognition. It is very general, extremely difficult reading, and
hampered by a fearful and wonderful terminology, e.g.\ our tetrahedron of the
simplest type is a special case of an \emph{Artiothoscheme}. It is, however, a striking
piece of geometrical work. Schl\"afli gives a shorter account of his work in
his `R\'eduction d'une int\'egrale multiple qui comprend l'arc d'un cercle et
l'aire d'un triangle sph\'erique comme cas partieuliers', \emph{Lionville's Journal}, vol.\
xxii, 1855.}
%endfootnote

Let the distance from $B_1$ to a variable point $P$ of the
triangle be $r$, while $\phi$ is the measure of $\measuredangle C_1 B_1 P$. We wish
to find
$$
k\iint\sin\frac{r}{k}\cos\frac{r}{k}dr\,d\phi.
$$
Let $B_1P$ meet $(C_1 D_1)$ in $E_1$. The limits of integration for $r$
are 0 and $\overline{B_1E_1}$; hence we have merely to find
$$
\frac{k^2}{2}\int_0^{\measuredangle C_1B_1D_1}\sin^2\frac{\overline{B_1E_1}}{k} d\phi.
$$
Now $C_1D_1$ is perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to $B_1C_1$, hence
$$
\tan\phi\sin\frac{\overline{B_1C_1}}{k} = \tan\frac{\overline{E_1C_1}}{k},
$$

%[File: 185.png]
\[
  \cos\frac{\overline{B_1 E_1}}{k}
= \cos\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k}
  \cos\frac{\overline{E_1 C_1}}{k}
  \tan\frac{\overline{B_1 E_1}}{k}
= \tan\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k} \sec\phi. \tag{Ch.~IV.~(5), (6).}
\]
\[
  \sin^2\frac{\overline{B_1 E_1}}{k} d\phi
= \frac{1}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k} d\overline{E_1 C_1}.
\]
Our required integral is then
\[
  \frac{k}{2} \int \sin\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k}
  d\overline{E_1 C_1}
= \frac{k}{2} \sin\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k}
  \cdot \overline{C_1 D_1}
\]

Let the reader note the astonishing feature of this result,
namely, that it involves one side of a triangle directly, and
another trigonometrically.

Let the measure of the dihedral angle whose edge is $(C_1 D_1)$
be $\theta$, this will also be the measure of
$\measuredangle A C_1 B_1$ which is the
plane angle of the dihedral one.
\begin{align*}
  \cos\theta
&= \cos\frac{\overline{A B_1}}{k} \sin\measuredangle BAC,
\\
  \sin\theta d\theta
&= \frac{1}{k} \sin\frac{\overline{A B_1}}{k}
   \sin\measuredangle BAC dx
\\
&= \frac{1}{k}  \sin\frac{\overline{A   B_1}}{k}
   \cdot \frac{\sin\dfrac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k} }
              {\sin\dfrac{\overline{A   C_1}}{k} } dx
\\
&= \frac{1}{k} \sin\theta \sin\frac{\overline{B_1 C_1}}{k} dx.
\end{align*}

\mysect{Reduction to a single quadrature of the problem of finding the volume of a tetrahedron}
We thus get for our volume\index{Volume05@--- of tetrahedron} the strange formula%
\footnote{See Schl\"afli\index{Schl\"afli}, \emph{R\'eduction}, p.~381,
where it is stated that this integral cannot
be evaluated by integration by parts. This same integral was discovered,
apparently independently, by Richmond\index{Richmond}, `The Volume of a Tetrahedron in
Elliptic Space,' \emph{Quarterly Journal of Mathematics}, vol.~xxxiv, 1902, p.~175.}
%endfootnote
\[
  \text{Vol.} = \frac{k^2}{2} \int \overline{C_1 D_1} d\theta. \tag{29}
\]

We can easily express this integral in terms of $\theta$,
\begin{align*}
   \tan\frac{\overline{C_1 D_1}}{k}
&= \sin\frac{\overline{A   C_1}}{k}
   \tan\measuredangle DAC
=a\sin\frac{\overline{A   C_1}}{k},
\\
   \cos\frac{\overline{A   C_1}}{k}
&= \ctn\measuredangle BAC \ctn\theta
= b\ctn\theta.
\\
  \text{Vol.}
&= \frac{k^3}{2} \int \tan^{-1}
  [ a\sqrt{1-b^2\ctn^2\theta} ] d\theta.  \tag{30}
\end{align*}

%[File: 186.png]
This formula apparently represents about as close an
approach as can be made towards \emph{finding the volume} of this
tetrahedron\index{Volume05@--- of tetrahedron}, for, in the general case,%
\footnote{Schl\"afli\index{Schl\"afli}, \emph{Vielfache Kontinuit\"at}, p.~95,
gives a formula for the special case
where the sum of the squares of the cosines of the dihedral angles is equal
to unity. The proof is highly intricate, and not suitable to reproduce here.}
%endfootnote
it does not seem possible
to effect the quadrature in terms of elementary functions.

\mysect{Volume of a cone of revolution}
If a right triangle be rotated completely about one of the
sides adjacent to the right angle, the figure so generated shall
be called a \emph{cone of revolution}\index{Cone of revolution}. The volume\index{Volume03@--- of cone}
within the surface
may be found as follows. Let the vertex of the cone be $A$
and the centre of the base $O$, while $P$ is a point within the
cone. Let $Q$ be the intersection of $(AO)$ with a perpendicular
from $P$, while the base circle meets the plane $A OP$ in $B$.
$(AB)$ shall meet $PQ$ in $R$. Let us also write
\begin{align*}
  \overline{AB} =& s,\quad \overline{AR} = r,\quad
  \overline{AO} = h,\quad \measuredangle OAB = \theta.
\\
  \text{Vol.}
&= k\int_0^{\overline{QR}} \int_0^h \int_0^{2\pi}
  \sin\frac{\overline{QP}}{k} \cos\frac{\overline{QP}}{k}
  d\overline{AQ}\, d\overline{QP}\, d\theta
\\
&= 2\pi k\int_0^h \int_0^{\overline{QR}}
  \sin\frac{\overline{QP}}{k} \cos\frac{\overline{QP}}{k}
  d\overline{AQ}.\, d\overline{QP}
\\
&= \pi k^2 \int_0^h \sin^2 \frac{\overline{QR}}{k} d\overline{AQ}.
\end{align*}
\[
  \tan \frac{\overline{AQ}}{k}
= \tan\frac{r}{k} \cos \theta. \tag{Ch.~V.~(6).}
\]
\[
  d\overline{AQ}
= \frac{\cos\theta \sec^2\dfrac{r}{k} dr}
        {1 + \cos^2\theta \tan^2\dfrac{r}{k}}.
\]
\[
  \sin\frac{\overline{QR}}{k}
= \sin\frac{r}{k} \sin\theta.
\]
\[
  \text{Vol.}
= \pi k^2\sin^2\theta \cos\theta
  \int_0^h \frac{\tan^2\dfrac{r}{k}                 }
                {1 + \cos^2\theta \tan^2\dfrac{r}{k}} dr.
\]
\[
  \text{Put}\quad\tan\frac{r}{k} = x.
\]
%[File: 187.png]
\begin{align*}
\text{Vol. } &= \pi k^3\cos\theta\sin^2\theta
  \int_0^{\tan\frac{h}{k}}
  \frac{x^2dx}{(1+x^2)(1+x^2\cos^2\theta)} \\
&= \pi k^3\cos\theta \Bigl[
  \int_0^{\tan\frac{h}{k}}
  \frac{dx}{1+x^2\cos\theta} -
  \int_0^{\tan\frac{h}{k}}
  \frac{dx}{1+x^2} \Bigr] \\
&= \pi k^3\cos\theta \Bigl[
  \frac{1}{\cos\theta}\tan^{-1}(x\cos\theta)-\tan^{-1}x
  \Bigr]_0^{\tan\frac{h}{k}} \\
\tag{31}
&= \pi k^2[h-s\cos\theta].\footnotemark
\end{align*}
\footnotetext{This formula is given without sufficiently detailed proof by Frischauf\index{Frischauf},
loc.\ cit., p.~99. A tedious demonstration was subsequently worked out by
Von Frank\index{Frank@Von Frank}, `Der K\"orperinhalt des senkrechten Cylinders und Kegels in der
absoluten Geometrie,' \emph{Grunerts Archiven}, vol.~lix, 1876.}
%endfootnote

\mysect{Volume of a sphere}
To find the volume within a proper sphere\index{Volume04@--- of sphere}, where the
distance from the centre to every point of the surface has the
constant value $R$,
\begin{align*}
\text{Vol.} &= k^2 \int_0^R \int_0^\pi \int_0^{2\pi}
  \sin^2\frac{2}{k}\sin\theta\, dr\, d\theta\, d\phi \\
&= 4\pi k^2\int_0^R \sin^2\frac{r}{k}dr \\
\tag{32}
&= \pi k^3\left(\frac{2R}{k}-\sin\frac{2R}{k}\right).
\end{align*}

\mysect{Volume of the whole of elliptic or of spherical space}
Let the reader show that the total volumes of elliptic and
of spherical space, where $k = 1$ will be, respectively,
\[
\pi^2,\quad 2\pi^2.
\]

%[File: 188.png]



%CHAPTER XV
\mychap{XV}{INTRODUCTION TO DIFFERENTIAL GEOMETRY}
\mysect{Curvature of a space or plane curve}

The task which we shall undertake in the present chapter
is to develop the differential geometry of curves and surfaces
in non-euclidean space.%
\footnote{The developments of this chapter follow the general scheme worked out
for the euclidean case in Bianchi-Lukat\index{Bianchi}, \emph{Vorlesungen \"uber Differentialgeometrie},
Leipzig, 1899, Chapters I, III, IV, and VI\@. In Chapters XXI and XXII of the
same work will be found a different development of the non-euclidean case.
It is, however, so general, yet so concise, as to be scarcely suitable to serve
as an introduction to the subject.}
%endfootnote
We shall introduce a notable simplification in our work by abandoning
homogeneous coordinates\index{Coordinates02@Co-ordinates of a point}, and assuming that
\[
(xx) = k^2.   \tag{1}
\]
In the elliptic case we shall take $x_0 \geqq 0$; in the hyperbolic,
$\dot{x}_0 = \dfrac{1}{k}x_0 \geqq 0$ for all real points.

Of course in exceptional cases, where we wish to include
points of the Absolute\index{Absolute} or beyond, this proceeding is not
legitimate; we shall therefore assume, unless we specifically
state the contrary, that we are limiting ourselves to a real
region, where no absolute or ultra infinite\index{Ultra-infinite elements} points are included
in the hyperbolic case. We shall, further, have for the
distance\index{Distance04@Distance element} of two points $(x)$, $(x')$.
\[
  \cos\frac{d}{k} = \frac{(xx')}{k^2},\quad
  \sin^2\frac{d}{k}
= \frac{\begin{Vmatrix}
           x_0 & x_1 & x_2 & x_3 \\
           x_0{}'& x_1{}'& x_2{}'& x_3{}'
         \end{Vmatrix}^2           }
       {k^4                        }.  \tag{2}
\]
When $x_i{}' = x_i + dx_i$ we have for the square of the differential
of distance
\[
  k^2 \frac{ds^2}{k^2} = ds^2 = \frac{(xx)(dx\,dx) - (x\,dx)^2}{k^2}.
\]
\[
  (x+dx,\;x+dx) = k^2,\quad (x\,dx) = -\tfrac{1}{2}(dx\,dx),
\]
\[
  ds^2 = (dx\,dx).   \tag{3}
\]

We shall mean by an analytic curve, such a curve that the
coordinates of its points are analytic functions of a single
variable. The formulae developed in this chapter will hold
%[File: 189.png]
equally well under the supposition that the functions and
their first three partial derivatives exist and are finite in our
region, but the gain in generality is of little interest to the
geometer, and we shall assume from here on that when we
speak of curve we mean analytic curve.

Let us imagine that at a chosen point of a curve, say $P$, a
tangent is drawn. We shall take two near points $P'$ and $P''$
on the curve and tangent respectively, so situated near $P$ and
on the same side of the normal plane that
$\overline{PP'} = \overline{PP''}$. Then
we shall define%
\footnote{This definition is taken from Bianchi\index{Bianchi}, loc.\ cit., p.~603. It is there
ascribed to Voss\index{Voss}.}
%endfootnote
\[
\limdot \frac{2\overline{P'P''}}{\overline{PP'^2}},
\]
as the curvature of the given curve at that point. If we
compare with Chapter XI.~(2), and define as the osculating
circle\index{Circle01@Circle} to a curve at a point, the limit of the circle through
that and two adjacent points, we shall have

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~1. The curvature of a curve\index{Curvature01@Curvature of a curve} at any point is equal
to that of its osculating circle, and is equal to the absolute
value of the product of the square root of the curvature of
space and the cotangent of the $k^{\text{th}}$ part of the distance of each
point of the circle from its centre.

Let us now suppose that the equations of our curve are
written in the form
\begin{align*}
x_i &= x_i(t_0)+(t-t_0)x_i{}'(t_0)
+ \frac{(t-t_0)^2}{2}x_i{}''(t_0) + \ldots
\\
x_i{}^{(n)} &= \frac{d^n}{dt^n}x_i(t).
\end{align*}
Then for a point on the tangent we shall have coordinates\index{Coordinates02@Co-ordinates of a point}
\[
X_i=\lambda[x_i(t_0)+(t-t_0)x_i{}'(t_0)].
\]
To get the value of $\lambda$.
\[
(XX) = xx = k^2, \quad (xx') = 0,
\]
\[
X_i = \frac{x_i(t_0) + (t-t_0)x_i{}'(t_0)}
           {\sqrt{1 + \dfrac{(t-t_0)^2}{k^2}(x_i{}' x_i{}')}}.
\]

Developing by the binomial theorem, and rejecting powers
of $(t-t_0)$ above the second
\[
X_i = x_i(t_0) + (t-t_0)x_i{}'(t_0)
    - \frac{(t-t_0)^2}{2k^2}(x'x')x_i(t_0).
\]

%[File: 190.png]
Subtracting from the series development of $x_i$ we get for
our curvature $\dfrac{1}{\rho}$.
\[
\frac{1}{\rho^2} =
  \frac{\left[ (x''x'')+\dfrac{2}{k^2}(xx'')(x'x')
             + \dfrac{1}{k^4}(x'x')^2(xx) \right]}
       {(x'x')^2},
\]
\[
\tag{4}
\frac{1}{\rho^2} = \frac{(x''x'')}{(x'x')^2} - \frac{1}{k^2}.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~2. The square of the curvature of a curve\index{Curvature01@Curvature of a curve} is the
square of its curvature treated as a curve in a four-dimensional
euclidean space, minus the measure of
curvature\index{Measure02@--- of curvature of space}\index{K@$\dfrac{1}{k^2}$ measure of curvature of space}\index{Curvature06@--- of space}
of the non-euclidean
space.

\mysect{Analoga of direction cosines of tangent, principal normal, and binormal}
It will be convenient to consider, besides our point $(x)$,
three other points allied to it. $(t)$ shall be orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points} to $(x)$
and on the tangent, $(z)$ orthogonal to $(x)$ on the principal
normal, and $(\xi)$ orthogonal to $(x)$ on the binormal. These
three will replace the direction cosines of tangent, principal
normal, and binormal, which figure so prominently in the
euclidean theory. In hyperbolic space these points lie without
the actual domain to which we suppose $(x)$ confined.
\[
(xt) = (xz) = (x\xi) = (tz) = (t\xi) = (z\xi) = 0.
\]

If a point trace an infinitesimal arc $ds$, the angle of the
corresponding absolute polar planes is
$\left|\sqrt{\dfrac{ds^2}{k^2}}\right|$.

We shall, hereafter, take as our parameter on the given
curve $s$, the length of arc, so that
\[
x_i{}'=\frac{dx_i}{ds}, \quad (x'x')=1.
\]

As $(t)$ lies on the tangent, its coordinates will be of the form
\[
t_i = lx_i+mx_i{}',
\]
\[
(tt) = (xx) = k^2, \quad (tx) = 0, \quad (xx') = 0,
\]
\[
\tag{5}
t_i = kx_i{}'.
\]

For the point $(z)$ we shall have
\[
z_i = \lambda x_i + \mu x_i{}' + \nu x_i{}'',
\]
\[
(zx) = (zx') = (xx') = (x'x') + (xx'') = 0,
\]
\[
(zz) = (xx) =k^2, \quad (x'x') = 1.
\]
%[File: 191.png]
\begin{align*}
  z_i &= \frac{x_i + k^2x_i{}''}{\sqrt{k^2(x''x'')-1}},  \\
  z_i &= \frac{\rho}{k}(x_i + k^2x_i{}'').  \tag{6}
\end{align*}
To determine $\xi$ we shall have the conditions
\[
  (\xi x) = (\xi z) =  (\xi t) = 0,\quad  (\xi\xi) = k^2,
\]
\[
  \xi_i = \rho\frac{\partial}{\partial y_i} \lvert y x x' x''\rvert. \tag{7}
\]

We shall define the \emph{torsion}\index{Torsion} of our curve as the limit of the
ratio of the angle of two successive osculating planes to the
differential of arc. We thus get
\[
  \frac{1}{T} = \frac{1}{k} \frac{\sqrt{(d\xi\,d\xi)}}{ds}.  \tag{8}
\]

\mysect{Frenet's formulae for the non-euclidean case}
Reverting to our formulae $(5)$ and $(6)$
\[
  \frac{dt_i}{ds} = \frac{z_i}{\rho} - \frac{x_i}{k}.   \tag{9}
\]
\[
  (x\xi) = (x'\xi) = (x''\xi) = (x\xi') = (x'\xi') = (\xi\xi') = 0.
\]
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{Hence}
&\frac{d\xi_i}{ds} &= lz_i,
\\
&\text{or, more specifically}
&  \frac{d\xi_i}{ds} &= \frac{z_i}{T}.   \tag{10}
\\
&\text{We have also}
&  (xz) = (xz') = &(x'z) = (zz') = 0, & \phantom{\text{We have also}}\\
&&  z_i{}' = \lambda &t_i + \mu\xi_i,   \\
&&  \frac{dz_i}{ds} = -&\frac{t_i}{\rho} - \frac{\xi_i}{T}.   \tag{11}
\end{flalign*}

The reader will see at once that $(9)$, $(10)$, $(11)$ are the
analoga of Frenet's\index{Frenet} formulae for euclidean curves.

We have, so far, overlooked the question of the sign of the
torsion, but that is well determined from the above formulae,
and it is important now to find the geometric difference
between the case where the torsion is negative, and that
where it is positive. We shall carry through the work for
the elliptic case only, the hyperbolic may be treated in the
same way, but it is wiser there to replace the coordinates
$(x)$ by $(\dot{x})$.

As before we shall choose $s$ as the independent variable,
so that
\[
  (xx) = k^2,\quad (xx') = (x'x'') = 0,\quad (x'x') = -(xx'')= 1.
\]

%[File: 192.png]
The sign of $t_i$ (which may be ideal) will be found from (5),
that of $z_i$ from (6), and that of $\xi_i$ from (7), while the sign
of T\index{Torsion} will be given by (10).

The equation of the plane of the tangent and binormal
will be
\begin{equation*}
|Xxt\xi| = (Xx) + k^2(Xx'') = 0.
\end{equation*}
Putting in the coordinates of a near-by point of the curve,
\begin{equation*}
x_i + x_i{}'\Delta s + x_i{}''\frac{(\Delta s)^2}{2},
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
k^2 - \frac{(\Delta s)^2}{2} - k^2 + k^2(x''x'')\frac{\Delta s^2}{2} = \frac{k^2}{\rho^2}\frac{(\Delta s)^2}{2},
\end{equation*}\label{no2}
and this is essentially positive, so that, in general, the curve
will not cross this plane here. Again, we see by (6) that
we may give to a point on the principal normal close to $(x)$
the coordinates
\begin{equation*}
x_i + \epsilon x_i{}''.
\end{equation*}
Substituting in the equation of the plane we get
\begin{equation*}
\frac{k^2}{\rho^2}\epsilon,
\end{equation*}
so that this will lie on the same side as the curve if $\epsilon > 0$.

Let us call \emph{positive} that part of the curve near our point for
which $\Delta s > 0$. The positive part of the tangent shall be that
which lies on the same side of the normal plane as the
positive part of the curve, while that part of the principal
normal shall be called positive which lies on the same side of
the plane of tangent and binormal as does the curve. Let us
find the Plueckerian coordinates of a ray from $x_i + x_i{}'\Delta s$ on
the positive part of the tangent to $x_i + \epsilon x_i{}''$ on the positive
part of the principal normal. We get
\begin{equation*}
p_{ij} = \epsilon\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_i&x_j\\x_i{}''&x''_j\end{array}\right|
+ \Delta s\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_i{}'&x'_j\\x_i&x_j\end{array}\right|
+ \epsilon\Delta s\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_i{}'&x'_j\\x_i{}''&x''_j\end{array}\right|.
\end{equation*}

In like manner for a ray\index{Ray} from $(x)$ to a point on the positive
part of the curve
\begin{equation*}
x_i + x_i{}'\Delta_1 s + x_i{}''\frac{(\Delta_1 s)^2}{2} + x_i{}'''\frac{(\Delta_1 s)^3}{3!},
\end{equation*}
we get
\begin{equation*}
q_{kl} = \Delta_1 s\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_k&x_l\\x_k{}'&x_l{}'\end{array}\right|
+ \frac{(\Delta_1 s)^2}{2}\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_k&x_l\\x_k{}''&x_l{}''\end{array}\right|
+ \frac{(\Delta_1 s)^3}{3!}\left|\begin{array}{cc}x_k&x_l\\x_k{}'''&x_l{}'''\end{array}\right|.
\end{equation*}
The relative moment of these two rays, as defined at the
close of Chapter IX, will be
\begin{equation*}
\smallsum p_{ij}q_{kl} \equiv \epsilon\frac{\Delta s (\Delta_1 s)^3}{6}|xx'x''x'''|.
\end{equation*}

%[File: 193.png]
The factors outside of the determinant are all, by hypothesis,
positive, so that the sign depends merely upon that of the
determinant, and this by (7) is equal to $\dfrac{\rho(\xi x''')}{k}$.

Now
\[
(\xi x'') = 0, \quad (\xi x''') = -(\xi' x'').
\]
Hence the relative moment will have the sign of
\[
  \frac{-\rho}{kT}(zx'')
= \frac{-\rho^2}{k^2T} [(xx'')+k^2(x''x'')]
= -\frac{1}{T}.
\]

\mysect{Sign of the torsion}
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~3. The torsion\index{Torsion} at a general point of a curve is
positive when the relative moment\index{Moment02@--- relative of two rays}
of a ray\index{Ray} thence to a point
on the positive part of the curve, and a ray from a point on
the positive part of the tangent to one on the positive part of
the principal normal\index{Normals01@Normals to curve} is negative; when the latter product is
positive, the torsion is negative.

Intuitively stated this means that the torsion is positive
when the curve resembles a left-hand screw, otherwise
negative.

\mysect{Evolutes of a space curve}
We shall next take up the evolutes\index{Evolutes} of a curve. Let $(\overline{x})$
be a point of an evolute. Then
\begin{align*}
x_i &= \cos\frac{\overline{s}}{k}\overline{x_i}
     - \sin\frac{\overline{s}}{k}\overline{t_i}, \\
\frac{dx_i}{d\overline{s}}
&= -\sin\frac{\overline{s}}{k} \frac{\overline{z_i}}{\overline{\rho}}.
\end{align*}
Remembering that $\dfrac{dx_i}{d\overline{s}} = kt_i$, while $\overline{z_i}$ is on the principal
normal of the evolute.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~4. A tangent to an analytic curve at a general
point will be in the osculating plane at the corresponding
point of any evolute.

Since $(\overline{x})$ lies in the normal plane at $(x)$, we may write
\[
w\overline{x_i} = x_i + u\xi_i + vz_i,
\]
\[
w\frac{d\overline{x_i}}{ds} =
  -\frac{1}{w} (x_i+u\xi_i+vz_i)\frac{dw}{ds} +
  \frac{t_i}{k} + u\frac{z_i}{T} -
  v\left( \frac{t_i}{\rho} + \frac{\xi_i}{T} \right)
  +\xi_i\frac{du}{ds} + z_i\frac{dx}{ds}.
\]

Now $\dfrac{d\overline{x_i}}{ds}$ is linearly dependent on $(x)$ and
$(\overline{x})$,
\[
\left( t\frac{d\overline{x}}{ds} \right) = 0, \quad
v = \frac{\rho}{k},
\]
%[File: 194.png]
and, for the same reason, the assemblage of all terms in $(\xi)$
and $(z)$ must be a linear combination of $(x)$ and $(\overline{x})$, and so
proportional to $w\overline{x_i} - x_i = u\xi_i + vz_i$
\[
  \Bigl[ \frac{du}{ds} - \frac{\rho}{kT} \Bigr]\xi_i +
  \Bigl[ \frac{u}{T} + \frac{d\rho}{kds} \Bigr]z_i =
  \lambda\left[ u\xi_i + \frac{\rho}{k}z_i \right],
\]
\[
  \frac{\dfrac{du}{ds}-\dfrac{\rho}{kT} }
       {\dfrac{u}{T} + \dfrac{1}{k}\dfrac{d\rho}{ds}} =
  \frac{u}{\dfrac{\rho}{k} },
\]
\[
  \tan^{-1}\left( \frac{u}{\dfrac{\rho}{k}} \right) =
  \int\frac{ds}{T} + C =
  (\sigma + C).
\]

To get $(w)$ we have
\[
(\overline{x}\,\overline{x}) = k^2,
\]
\[
w=\sqrt{1+u^2+v^2} =
  \sqrt{1+\frac{\rho^2}{k^2}\sec^2(\sigma+C)}.
\]
\[
\tag{12}
\overline{x_i} =
  \frac{\left( x_i+z_i\dfrac{\rho}{k} \right)\cos(\sigma+C)
       + \dfrac{\rho}{k}\xi_i\sin(\sigma+C) }
       {\sqrt{\dfrac{\rho^2}{k^2} + \cos^2(\sigma+C)} }.
\]

The coordinates of the point of the line $(x)(\overline{x})$
orthogonal\index{Evolutes}\index{Normals01@Normals to curve}\index{Perpendicularity}
to $(x)$ will be
\[
\lambda x_i + \mu \overline{x_i},
\]
\begin{align*}
\lambda k^2 + \frac{\mu k^2\cos(\sigma+C)}
  {\sqrt{\dfrac{\rho^2}{k^2} + \cos^2(\sigma+C)}} &= 0,
\\
(\lambda^2+\mu^2)k^2 +
  \frac{2\lambda\mu k^2\cos(\sigma+C)}
  {\sqrt{\dfrac{\rho^2}{k^2} + \cos(\sigma+C)}} &= k^2,
\\
\mu = \frac{-\sqrt{\dfrac{\rho^2}{k^2} + \cos(\sigma+C)}}
           {\dfrac{\rho}{k} },   \quad
  \lambda &= \frac{\cos(\sigma+C)}
                 {\dfrac{\rho}{k} }.
\end{align*}

The point in question will therefore have the coordinates\index{Coordinates02@Co-ordinates of a point}
\[
\xi_i\sin(\sigma+C) + z_i\cos(\sigma+C).
\]

%[File: 195.png]
This gives us the significance of $\sigma$, namely $(\sigma + C)$ is the $k^{\text{th}}$
part of the distance from this point to $(z)$, i.e.\ $(\sigma + C)$ represents
the angle which this normal\index{Normals01@Normals to curve} makes with the principal
normal. If, then, we take two evolutes\index{Evolutes} of our curve the
angle between their corresponding tangents, i.e.\ those which
meet on the involute, is
\[
  (\sigma + C_1) - (\sigma + C_2) = C_1 - C_2.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~5. Corresponding tangents to two evolutes of
a curve meet at a constant angle.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~6. If the generators of a developable surface be
turned through a constant angle about the tangents to one
of their orthogonal trajectories, the resulting surface is
developable.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~7. The tangents to an evolute of a plane curve
make a constant angle with the plane of the curve.

\mysect{Two fundamental quadratic differential forms for a surface}
The foregoing theorems and formulae exhibit sufficiently
the close analogy between the differential theory of curves
in euclidean and in non-euclidean space. It is our next task
to take up the theory of surfaces, and we shall find a no
less striking analogy there. We shall mean by an \emph{analytic
surface} the locus of a point whose coordinates are analytic
functions of two independent parameters. We shall exclude
from consideration all singular points of such surfaces. If
the parameters be $(u)$ and $(v)$, we shall have for the squared
distance element\index{Distance04@Distance element}
\[
  ds^2 = E\,du^2 + 2 F\,du\,dv + G\,dv^2,
\]
\[
  E = \left( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
             \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right),\quad
  F = \left( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
             \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right),\quad
  G = \left( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
             \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right),
\]
\[
  EG - F^2
= \begin{Vmatrix}
    \dfrac{\partial x_0}{\partial u}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_1}{\partial u}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_2}{\partial u}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_3}{\partial u}
  \smallskip\\
    \dfrac{\partial x_0}{\partial v}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_1}{\partial v}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_2}{\partial v}
  & \dfrac{\partial x_3}{\partial v}
  \end{Vmatrix}^2.                \tag{13}
\]

This is a positive definite form in the elliptic case, and in
the actual domain of hyperbolic space, to which we shall
restrict ourselves. The discriminant, under this same restriction,
will always be greater than zero, for it will vanish only
when the tangent plane to the surface\index{Tangent plane to surface} is also tangent to the
Absolute.

The equation of the tangent plane at $(x)$ will be
\[
  \left |  Xx
  \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
  \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
   \right|
= 0.
\]

%[File: 196.png]
The Absolute pole of this plane\index{Tangent plane to surface} will be
\[
  y_i = \frac{\dfrac{\partial}{r_i}
               \left|
                 rx
               \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
               \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}
               \right|  }
             {\sqrt{EG-F^2}  }.   \tag{14}
\]

We shall consistently use the letter $(y)$ throughout the
present chapter to indicate this point. The equation of the
plane through the normal, and the point $(x + dx)$, will be
\[
  \begin{vmatrix}
    (Xx) & (xx)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right)
\\
    \left( X\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right) & E
\\
    \left( X\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right) & F
  \end{vmatrix} du
+
  \begin{vmatrix}
    (Xx) & (xx)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right)
\\
    \left( X\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \right) & F
\\
    \left( X\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right)
  & \left( x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right) & G
  \end{vmatrix} dv
= 0.
\]
\[
  \sum_i^{0.. 3} \left[
  \left( F\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}
       - E\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} \right) du
+
  \left( G\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}
       - F\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} \right) dv
 \right] X_i = 0.
\]

The cosine of the angle which this plane makes with that
through the normal and the point $(x + \delta x)$, or the cosine of
the angle of the two arcs from $(x)$ to $(x + dx)$ and
$(x + \delta x)$,
will be
\[
  \frac{E\,du\,\delta u
       + F (du\,\delta v + \delta u\,dv)
       + G\,dv\,\delta v }
       {ds\,\delta s }.             \tag{15}
\]

\mysect{Conditions for mutually conjugate or perpendicular tangents}
The two will be mutually perpendicular\index{Conjugate02@--- directions on a surface} if
\[
  E\,du\,\delta u
+ F (du\,\delta v + \delta u\,dv)
+ G\,dv\,\delta v = 0.
\]

The condition for perpendicularity between the parameter
curves will be
\[
  F = 0.      \tag{16}
\]

The equation of the tangent plane at $(x + dx)$ is
\begin{multline*}
  \left| X
  \Bigl( x
       + \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}du
       + \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}dv \Bigr)
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
       + \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}du
       + \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}dv \Bigr)
  \right.
\\
  \left.
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
       + \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}du
       + \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}dv           \Bigr)
  \right|
= 0.
\end{multline*}
Neglecting differentials of higher order than the first, we
have
\begin{multline*}
  \left| Xx \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right|
+ \left[
  \left| Xx \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}
            \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right|
+
  \left| Xx \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v} \right|
  \right] du
\\
+ \left[
  \left| Xx \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}
            \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right|
+
  \left| Xx \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \right|
  \right] dv
= 0.
\end{multline*}
%[File: 197.png]
The line of intersection with the tangent plane at $(x)$ will be
found by equating to zero separately the first and the last
four terms. This line will contain the point $(x + \delta x)$ if
\[
  D\,du\,\delta u + D'(du\,\delta v + dv\,\delta u)
+ D''dv\,\delta v = 0.
\]
\[
  D  = \frac{\left| x \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                      \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}
                      \dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \right|}
            {\sqrt{EG-F^2}},
\quad
  D' = \frac{\left| x \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                      \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}
                      \dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}
             \right|}
            {\sqrt{EG-F^2}},
\quad
  D''= \frac{\left| x \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                      \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}
                      \dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \right|}
            {\sqrt{EG-F^2}}.
\tag{17}
\]

The signs of $D$, $D'$, $D''$ to be determined presently.

These are the equations for tangents to conjugate systems
of curves, or, briefly put, the equations determining differentials
in conjugate directions\index{Conjugate02@--- directions on a surface}. The parameter curves will
be mutually conjugate if
\[
  D' = 0.     \tag{18}
\]

The differential equation for self-conjugate, or asymptotic\index{Asymptotic lines}
lines, will be
\[
  D\,du^2 + 2D'\,du\,dv + D''\,dv^2 = 0.    \tag{19}
\]

\mysect{Lines of curvature}
Returning to the point $(y)$, the pole of the tangent plane,
we have
\[
  \Bigl( y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \Bigr) = D,\quad
  \Bigl( y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v} \Bigr) =D',\quad
  \Bigl( y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr) = D'';
\]
\[
  (xy) = (y\,dx) = (x\,dy) = 0,
\]
\[
\begin{split}
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
         \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
=-\Bigl(y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \Bigr)
\quad
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
         \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)
= \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
         \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
=-\Bigl(y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v} \Bigr)
\hfill \\ \hfill
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
         \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)
=-\Bigl( y\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr),
\end{split}
\]
\[
  -(dy\,dx) = D\,du^2 + 2D'\,du\,dv + D''\,dv^2.    \tag{20}
\]

These equations will determine the signs of $D$, $D'$, $D''$.

Under what circumstances will the normals at two adjacent
points intersect, i.e.\ when will their minimum distance be
an infinitesimal of higher order than the element of arc?
Geometrically we see that the characteristic of the two
adjacent tangent planes must be perpendicular to its conjugate.
Conversely, when we do progress along such an infinitesimal
arc, the tangent plane may be said to rotate about a line
%[File: 198.png]
perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity} to the element of progression, and adjacent
normals\index{Normals02@--- to surface} are coplanar. At any general point of the surface,
except at an umbilical point where the involution of conjugate
tangents is made up of mutually perpendicular tangents,
there will be just two tangents which are mutually conjugate
and mutually perpendicular, and these give the elements
desired.

This fairly plausible geometrical reasoning may easily be
put on a sound analytical basis. The necessary and sufficient
condition that the four points $(x)$, $(y)$, $(x + dx)$, $(y + dy)$ should
be coplanar is

\[
 | yx\,dx\,dy | = 0,
\]
\[
 \left|\begin{array}{ccc}
(xx) & (x\,dx) & (x\,dy) \\
\Bigl(x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}\Bigr) & \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}dx\Bigr) & \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}dy \Bigr) \smallskip\\
 \Bigl(x\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}\Bigr) & \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial  v}dx\Bigr) &
 \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}dy \Bigr)
 \end{array}\right|  = 0. \tag*{by (14)}
\]
\[
\begin{array}{|cc|}
Edu + Fdv & Ddu + D'dv\\
Fdu + Gdv & D'du + D''dv
\end{array} = 0. \tag{21}
\]

This is the Jacobian of the binary homogeneous forms (13)
and (20), and gives the two tangents which are both mutually
perpendicular and mutually conjugate; the indetermination
mentioned above occurs in the case where
\[
E:F:G = D:D':D''.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~8. The normals to a surface may be assembled
into two families of developable surfaces. Each normal, with
the exception of those at umbilical points, lies in one surface
of each family.

The integral curves of the differential equation (20) are
called \emph{lines of curvature}. We see at once that

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~9. If two surfaces intersect along a line which is
a line of curvature for each, they intersect at a constant
angle, and if two surfaces intersect at a constant angle along
a curve which is a line of curvature for one it is a line of
curvature for the other.

\mysect{Dupin's theorem for triply orthogonal systems}
This is the theorem of Joachimsthal\index{Joachimsthal}, well known in the
euclidean case. No less celebrated is the beautiful theorem
of Dupin\index{Dupin}.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~10. In any triply orthogonal system of surfaces\index{Orthogonal02@--- system of surfaces},
the curves of intersection are lines of curvature.

%[File: 199.png]
Let the three families of surfaces be given by the equations
\[
x_i=f_i(uv),\quad x_i=\phi_i(vw),\quad x_i=\psi_i(wu),
\]
\[
(xx)=k^2,\quad
  \Bigl(x\frac{\partial x}{\partial u}\Bigr) =
  \Bigl(x\frac{\partial x}{\partial v}\Bigr) =
  \Bigl(x\frac{\partial x}{\partial w}\Bigr) = 0.
\]

As the parameter lines are, in every case, mutually perpendicular
\[
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \Bigr) =
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr) =
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr) =0,
\]
\begin{multline*}
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v\,\partial w}
  \Bigr) +
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}
  \Bigr) =
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v\,\partial w}
  \Bigr) +
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial w\,\partial u}
  \Bigr)
\\ = \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial w\,\partial u}
  \Bigr) +
\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}
  \Bigr) = 0,
\end{multline*}
\[
\Bigl(x\frac{\partial x}{\partial w}\Bigr) =
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \Bigr) =
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \frac{\partial x}{\partial w} \Bigr) =
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}
    \frac{\partial x}{\partial w}\Bigr) = 0,
\]
\[
\left| x \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
  \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
  \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v} \right| =
  D^{\prime}\sqrt{EG-F^2} = 0,
\]
\[
D^{\prime} = 0.
\]

The vanishing of $D^{\prime}$ and $F$ proves our theorem. Our statement
in Chapter XIII that confocal quadrics intersect in lines
of curvature\index{Curvature07@--- lines of} is hereby justified.

A surface all of whose curves are lines of curvature must
be a sphere. The normal at any point $P$ will determine, with
any other point $Q$ of the surface, a plane. The normals to
the surface along this curve, will, by hypothesis, generate an
evolute, and hence, by (7) make a fixed angle with the plane;
and this angle must be null, since, by hypothesis, one normal
lies in the plane. Hence the normals at $P$ and $Q$ intersect, or
all normals must pass through one point. Evidently the
orthogonal surface to a bundle of concurrent lines is a sphere.

Let us suppose that we have a conformal transformation\index{Conformal transformations} of
space. It will carry a triply orthogonal system of surfaces\index{Orthogonal02@--- system of surfaces}
into another such system, hence a line of curvature into a line
of curvature. It will, therefore, carry any surface all of
whose curves are lines of curvature into another such surface,
hence

\smallskip\emph{Theorem} 11.~Every conformal transformation of space
carries a sphere into a sphere.

Of course a plane is here regarded as a special case of a
sphere.

%[File: 200.png]
Let us now examine the normals along a line of curvature\index{Curvature07@--- lines of}.
Let $r$ be the distance from the point $(x)$ to the intersection
of the normal there with the adjacent normal, a point whose
coordinates shall be called $(\bar{x})$.

\[ \bar{x}_i = x_i \cos\frac{r}{k} - y_i \sin\frac{r}{k},
\]

\[ \frac{d\bar{x}_i}{ds} = \frac{dx_i}{ds}\cos\frac{r}{k} - \frac{dy_i}{ds}\sin\frac{r}{k} - \left [x_i\sin\frac{r}{k} - y_i\cos\frac{r}{k}\right ]\frac{dr}{ds}.
\]

Now, by hypothesis, $\left (\dfrac{d\bar{x}}{ds}\right )$ is linearly dependent on $(x)$
and $(y)$.
\[ dx_i\cos\frac{r}{k} - dy_i\sin\frac{r}{k} = \lambda(x_i + \mu y_i).
\]
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent But} &(x\,dx) &= (x\,dy) = (y\,dx) = (y\,dy) = (xy) = 0, & \phantom{\text{\indent But}}
\end{flalign*}
\[ \lambda = \mu = 0,
\]
\[ dx_i \equiv dy_i\tan\frac{r}{k},
\]
\[ \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}du + \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} dv = \tan\frac{r}{k}\Bigl[ \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial u}du + \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial v}dv \Bigr].
\]
\label{partial}

\mysect{Curvature of a curve on a surface}
In particular, let us take as parameter lines the lines of
curvature

\[ \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} = \tan\frac{r_1}{k} \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial u}, \quad \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} = \tan\frac{r_2}{k} \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial v},
\]

\[ (dx\,dy) = \frac{E}{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}}du^2 + \frac{G}{\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}dv^2,
\]

\[ (dy\,dy) = \frac{E}{\tan^2\dfrac{r_1}{k}}du^2 + \frac{G}{\tan^2\dfrac{r_2}{k}}dv^2. \tag{22}
\]
