In the general case,

\[ Edu + Fdv = -\tan\frac{r}{k} [Ddu + D'dv],
\]

\[ Fdu + Gdv = -\tan\frac{r}{k} [D'du + D''dv].
\]

Eliminating $\tan\dfrac{r}{k}$ we get our previous differential equation
for the lines of curvature. On the other hand, if we eliminate
$du$, $dv$ we get
%[File: 201.png]
\[
  (DD'' - {D'}^2)\tan^2 \frac{r}{k}
+ [ED'' + GD - 2FD']\tan\frac{r}{k} + (EG - F^2) = 0.   \tag{23}
\]
\[
  \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}} + \frac{1}{k\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}
= -\frac{ED'' + GD - 2FD'}{k[EG - F^2]};
\]
\[
  \frac{1}{k^2\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k} \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}
= \frac{DD'' - {D'}^2}{k^2(EG - F^2)}.                 \tag{24}
\]

These last two expressions shall be called the \emph{mean relative
curvature}\index{Curvature04@--- mean relative}
and the \emph{total relative curvature}\index{Curvature05@--- total relative}, respectively.
They are, by XI.~(2), the sum and the product of the curvatures of normal sections through the tangents to the lines of
curvature. Notice that they are absolute simultaneous
invariants of the two binary forms (13), (20).

Let us now look at the more general question of the
curvature of a curve\index{Curvature01@Curvature of a curve} on our surface. As, by (4), this does not
involve derivatives of higher order than the second, the
curvature at any point of a curve of the surface is identical
with that of the curve of intersection of the osculating plane
with the surface. Along our curve $u$ and $v$ will be functions
of $s$ the parameter of length of arc, so that, using our previous
notation,
\[
  t_i = k\Bigl[ \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}\frac{du}{ds}
              + \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}\frac{dv}{ds} \Bigr].
\]

The cosine of the angle which the principal normal to this
curve makes with the normal to the surface may be written
\[
  \cos \sigma = \pm \frac{(yz)}{k^2},
\]
\[
  \frac{z_i}{\rho} = \frac{dt_i}{ds} + \frac{x_i}{k},\quad
  \frac{\cos\sigma}{\rho}
= \pm\left( \frac{y\dfrac{dt}{ds}}{k^2} \right),
\]
\label{spotthediff}\begin{multline*}
  \frac{dt_i}{ds}
= k\left[
    \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u^2}\Bigl(\frac{du}{ds}\Bigr)^2
 + 2\frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u\,\partial v}
    \frac{du}{ds}\frac{dv}{ds}
 + \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial v^2}\Bigl(\frac{dv}{ds}\Bigr)^2
   \right]
\\
+ k\Bigl[ \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}\frac{d^2u}{ds^2}
        + \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}\frac{d^2v}{ds^2} \Bigr],
\end{multline*}
\[
  \frac{\cos\sigma}{\rho}
= \pm\frac{Ddu^2 + 2D'du\,dv + D''dv^2}
          {k[Edu^2 + 2Fdu\,dv + Gdv^2]}.
\]

The indetermination of sign may be used to make the
curvature essentially positive.

%[File: 202.png]
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~12. Meunier's\index{Meunier}. The curvature of a
curve\index{Curvature01@Curvature of a curve} on a
surface at any point is equal to the curvature of the normal
section with the same tangent divided by the cosine of the
angle which the principal normal makes with the normal to
the surface.

Reverting to our previous expressions $r_1$, $r_2$ and taking the
lines of curvature as parameter lines, the curvature of the
normal sections through the tangents to the lines of curvature
are
\[
\frac {1} {k \tan \dfrac {r_1} {k} },\quad
\frac {1} {k \tan \dfrac {r_2} {k} },
\]
\[
dx_i = \tan \frac {r_1} {k} dy_i,\quad
\delta x_i = \tan \frac {r_2} {k} \delta y_i,
\]
\[
E = \tan \frac {r_1} {k} D,\quad
G = \tan \frac {r_2} {k} D'',
\]
\[
\frac {1} {\rho} = \pm \Biggl[\frac {E} {k \tan \dfrac {r_1} {k}}
\Bigl( \frac {du} {ds} \Bigr)^2 +
\frac {G} {k \tan \dfrac {r_2} {k}} \Bigl(\frac {dv} {ds} \Bigr)^2\Biggr];
\]
or, if $\theta$ be the angle which the chosen tangent makes with
that to $v = $ cons.

\[
\frac {1} {\rho} =
\frac {\cos^2 \theta} {k \tan \dfrac {r_1} {k} } +
\frac {\sin^2 \theta} {k \tan \dfrac {r_2} {k} }.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~13. The normal sections of a surface at any point
having the greatest and the least curvature are those determined
by the tangents to the lines of curvature.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~14. If on each tangent to a surface at a point
a distance be laid off equal to the square root of the reciprocal
of the measure of curvature of the normal section with that
tangent, the locus of the points so formed will be a central
conic.

\mysect{Dupin's indicatrix}
We leave to the reader the task of filling in the details of
the proof of the last theorem, they will come very easily from
considering the equation of a central conic as given in
Chapter XII\@. Of course the theorem is untrue at a point
where the tangents to the two lines of curvature coincide.
This central conic is called \emph{Dupin's Indicatrix}\index{Indicatrix of Dupin}\index{Dupin} in the
euclidean case, and we may well use the same name in
the non-euclidean case also.

%[File: 203.png]
The curvature of a surface bears a close relation to the
element of arc of the point $(y)$.
\[
-(dx\,dy)=Ddu^2+2D'du\,dv+D''dv^2,
\]
\[
(dy\,dy)=e\,du^2+2f\,du\,dv+g\,dv^2,
\]
\[
\Bigl(y\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=\Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=
D'\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)-
D\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=0,
\]
\[
\lambda \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial u} =
D' \frac{\partial}{\partial s_i}\left\vert sxy\frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
\right\vert -D\frac{\partial}{\partial s_i}\left\vert sxy\frac{\partial x}{\partial v}\right\vert,
\]
\[
\lambda\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=-\lambda D=-D\sqrt{EG-F^2},
\]
\[
\Bigl(\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=\frac{D'^2E+D^2G-2DD'F}{EG-F^2},
\]
\[
\Bigl(\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\frac{\partial y}{\partial v}\Bigr)=\frac{D'D''E-(DD''+D'^2)F+DD'G}{EG-F^2},
\]
\[
\Bigl(\frac{\partial y}{\partial v}\frac{\partial y}{\partial v}\Bigr)=\frac{D''^2E-2D'D''F+D'^2G}{EG-F^2},
\]
\begin{flalign*}
-(e\,du^2 + 2f\,du\,dv &+ g\,dv^2)\\
&=\frac{1}{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} (Edu^2 + 2Fdu\,dv + Gdv^2)+\\
&+\left(\frac{1}{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}}+\frac{1}{\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}\right)(Ddu^2 + 2D'du\,dv + D''dv^2).
\tag{25}
\end{flalign*}

\mysect{Torsion of asymptotic lines}
An asymptotic curve\index{Asymptotic lines} has the property that as a point moves
along it, the tangent plane to the surface tends to rotate
about the tangent to this curve, i.e.\ the tangent plane to the
surface is the osculating plane to the curve, and the normal
to the surface is the binormal to the curve. In dealing with
such a curve the point $(y)$ on the normal will replace the
point we previously called $(\xi)$. The torsion of any asymptotic
line will be, by (8),

\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{T}=\frac{\sqrt{(dy\,dy)}}{kds}.
\end{equation*}

But, in the case of an asymptotic curve, the second part
of the right-hand side of (25) will be zero, while the parenthesis
%[File: 204.png]
in the first part is equal to $ds^2$, hence, for an asymptotic
line
\[ \frac{(dy\,dy)}{k^2ds^2} = \frac{1}{T^2} = \frac{-1}
{k^2\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}. \]

It is not difficult to see that the two asymptotic lines\index{Asymptotic lines} at a
point, when real, have torsion\index{Torsion} with opposite signs, we have
but to look at the special case of a ruled quadric, hence:

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~15. The two asymptotic lines at a point, when
real, have torsions equal to the two square roots of the
negative of the total relative curvature of the surface.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~16. In any surface of constant total relative
curvature, the torsion of every asymptotic line is constant
and equal to a square root of the total relative curvature, and
the necessary and sufficient condition that a surface should
have constant total relative curvature is that the asymptotic
lines of one set should have constant torsion. Under these
circumstances the asymptotic lines of the other set will have a
constant torsion equal to the negative of that already given,
and the square of either torsion will be the total relative
curvature.

\mysect{Total relative curvature, its relation to Gaussian curvature}
In speaking of the total curvature of a surface we have
used the word \emph{relative}\index{Curvature05@--- total relative}. It is now time to explain why that
adjective is chosen. Let us try to express our total relative
curvature in terms of $E$, $F$, $G$ and their derivatives. We have
\[ \frac{1}{k^2\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} =
\frac{DD''-D'^2}{k^2(EG - F^2)}. \tag{24}\]

For the sake of simplicity we shall take as parameter lines
$u$, $v$ the isotropic curves\index{Isotropic01@Isotropic curves} of the surface, i.e.\ those whose
tangents also touch the Absolute. We assume that our
surface is not a developable circumscribed to the Absolute,
and that in the region considered no tangent plane to the
surface touches the Absolute. The isotropic curves at every
point will therefore be distinct. We shall have

\[ E= G = 0,\quad (xx) = k^2,
\]

\[
\Bigl ( x \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr ) =
\Bigl ( x \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr ) =
\Bigl ( x \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \Bigr ) =
\Bigl ( x \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr ) =
0,
\]

\[ 2Fdu\,dv = ds^2,
\]

\[ \Bigl ( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr ) = \frac{\partial F}{\partial u},
\]

%[File: 205.png]
\[ \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr) = \frac{\partial F}{\partial v},
\]

\[ \Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}\Bigr)
- \Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}\Bigr) = \frac{\partial^2 F}{\partial u\,\partial v}; \]

\[
D'^2 = \frac{-1}{F^2}
\begin{vmatrix}
k^2 & 0 & 0 & -F\\
0 & 0 & F & 0\\
0 & F & 0 & 0\\
-F & 0 & 0 & \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}\dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u\,\partial v}\Bigr)
\end{vmatrix},
\]

\[
DD''=\frac{-1}{F^2}
\begin{vmatrix}
k^2 & 0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & F & \dfrac{\partial F}{\partial v}\smallskip\\
0 & F & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \dfrac{\partial F}{\partial u} & \Bigl(\dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}\dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}\Bigr)
\end{vmatrix},
\]

\[
\frac{DD''-D'^2}{k^2(EG-F^2)}
=\frac{1}{F^2}\Bigl[\frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial u}\frac{\partial F}{\partial v}
- \frac{\partial^2 F}{\partial u\,\partial v}\Bigr] - \frac{1}{k^2}. \tag{26}
\]

The first expression on the right is the Gaussian curvature\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian}
of a two-dimens\-ional manifold whose squared distance element
is $2Fdu\,dv$%
\footnote{Cf.\ Bianchi\index{Bianchi}, loc.\ cit., p.~68.}.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~17%
\footnote{Cf.\ Bianchi, loc.\ cit., p.~609.}.
The total relative curvature\index{Curvature05@--- total relative} of a surface is
equal to the difference between its total Gaussian curvature
and the measure of curvature of
space\index{K@$\dfrac{1}{k^2}$ measure of curvature of space}\index{Measure02@--- of curvature of space}\index{Curvature06@--- of space}.

The Gaussian curvature may also be called the \emph{total
absolute curvature}. Notice that this theorem remains true
in euclidean space where the measure of curvature is $0$.


\mysect{Surfaces of zero relative curvature}
The problem of finding all surfaces of total relative curvature
zero\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero} is quickly solved. Let us assume that

\[
\tan\frac {r_2} k= \infty.
\]

Then, by an equation just preceding (22), as

\[
\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} \neq 0, \quad \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial v}=0, \]
and there will be the same tangent plane all along $u = \text{const}$.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~18. A surface of total relative curvature zero is
a developable.

%[File: 206.png]
Clearly every developable has total relative curvature zero.

Much more interest attaches to the surfaces of total Gaussian
curvature\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian} zero, i.e.\ those which are developable upon the
euclidean plane. The total relative curvature will be $-\dfrac{1}{k^2}$.
There is an advantage in considering the hyperbolic and
elliptic cases separately.

In the hyperbolic case let $(y)$ be the centre of a sphere, the
constant distance thence to points of the surface being $r$

\[ \cos\frac r k = \frac {(xy)}{k^2}, \quad k^2\tan^2\frac r k = k^2\left[\frac{(xx)(yy)-(xy)^2}{(xy)^2}\right].
\]

If the surface is to be actual $(xx) = k^2$. If the sphere be
a proper one $(yy) = k^2$, the total relative curvature will be
$> \dfrac{-1}{k^2}$. In the case of a horocyclic surface\index{Horocyclic surface} we may not
assume $(yy) = k^2$, but must treat $(y)$ as homogeneous coordinates
where $(yy)=0$. We get then

\[ \frac{1}{k^2 \tan^2\dfrac{r}{k}} = - \frac{1}{k^2}.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~19.%
\footnote{Cf.\ Manning\index{Manning}, loc.\ cit., p.~52; Killing, \emph{Die Grundlagen der Geometrie},
Paderborn, 1898, p.~33.}
%endfootnote
The horocyclic surface of hyperbolic space is developable on the euclidean plane.

\mysect{Surfaces of zero Gaussian curvature}
In elliptic space there is a peculiarly notable class of
surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero, ruled surfaces. We have
already seen one example, the Clifford\index{Clifford} Surface of Chapter X.
This quadric, be it remembered, cuts the Absolute\index{Absolute} in two
generators of each set, and its own generators form an orthogonal
system. Now Dupin's\index{Dupin} indicatrix\index{Indicatrix of Dupin} shows that the
normal sections of greatest and of least curvature will be
determined by tangents bisecting the angles of the two
generators, and the planes of these normal sections will cut
the surface in two circles whose axes are the axes of revolution
of the surface, and whose centres lie on these axes. The
centres are thus mutually orthogonal points\index{Orthogonal points}, hence the total
relative curvature is $- \dfrac {1} {k^2},$ and the Gaussian curvature is zero.
This statement was given without proof in Chapter X. We
notice also that the generators of either set are paratactic,
and the question arises, will not this fact alone constitute
a sufficient condition that a surface should have Gaussian
curvature zero?

%[File: 207.png]
Let us imagine that we have a surface generated by $\infty^1$
paratactic\index{Parataxy} lines.%
\footnote{For an interesting treatment of these surfaces see Bianchi\index{Bianchi}, `Le superficie
a curvatura nulla nella geometria ellitica,' \emph{Annali di Matematica}, Serie 2,
Tomo 24, 1896.}
%endfootnote
The parameter $v$ shall give the actual distance measured on each line from an orthogonal trajectory
$v =$ const. We have for our distance element
\[
  ds^2 = Edu^2 + dv^2.
\]

We know, moreover, by Chapter IX that if two lines be
paratactic they have an infinite number of common perpendiculars
on which they determine congruent distances.
Hence $E$ is a function of $u$ alone, and we may choose $u$ so
that it shall be equal to unity
\[
  ds^2 = du^2 + dv^2,     \tag{27}
\]
and the Gaussian curvature\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian}\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero} is zero.

Conversely, suppose that we have a ruled surface of
Gaussian curvature zero. The square of the element of arc
may be written
\[
  ds^2 = Edu^2 + dv^2.
\]

Since the Gaussian curvature is zero
\[
  \frac{\partial^2 \sqrt{E}}{\partial v^2} = 0,\quad
  \sqrt{E} = \theta(u)v + \psi(u).
\]
On the other hand we may write our surface parametrically
in the form
\[
  x_i = f_i(u)\cos \frac{v_i}{k} + \phi_i(u)\sin \frac{v}{k},
\]
with the additional conditions
\[
  (f\!f) = (\phi\phi) = k^2,\quad
  (f\!f') = (\phi\phi') = (f\phi) = (f\phi') + (\phi f') = 0;
\]
\[
  E = (f'f')\cos^2 \frac{v}{k} + (\phi'\phi')\sin^2 \frac{v}{k}
    + 2(f'\phi')\sin \frac{v}{k} \cos \frac{v}{k},
\]
\[
  kF
= (\phi f')\cos^2 \frac{v}{k} - (f\phi')\sin^2 \frac{v}{k} = 0,\quad
  (f\phi') = (\phi f') = 0.
\]

These are identical with previous
\[
  E = [\theta(u)]^2 v^2 + 2\theta(u)\psi(u)v + [\psi(u)]^2,
\]
only when
\[
  \theta(u) \equiv 0.
\]

We may, then, take
\[
  E = 1,\quad ds^2 = du^2 + dv^2;
\]
%[File: 208.png]
and this shows that two adjacent generators determine equal
distances on all their orthogonal trajectories, and so are
paratactic\index{Parataxy}.

\mysect{Ruled surfaces of zero Gaussian curvature in elliptic or spherical space}
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~20. The necessary and sufficient condition that
a ruled surface in elliptic space should have Gaussian curvature
zero\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian}\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero}
is that its generators should be paratactic.

Another highly interesting criterion for a surface of constant
Gaussian curvature zero is obtained as follows:
\[
E=G=1, \quad F=0;
\]
\[
\Bigl( \frac{\partial   x}{\partial u  }
       \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u \,\partial v} \Bigr) =
\Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}
       \frac{\partial   x}{\partial v  } \Bigr) =
\Bigl( \frac{\partial   x}{\partial u  }
       \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr) = 0, \quad
\Bigl(x\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}\Bigr) = -1.
\]

The coordinates of the absolute pole of the tangent plane
are
\[
y_i = \frac{\partial}{\partial s_i}
  \left\lvert sx \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                 \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right\rvert.
\]

The coordinates of the absolute pole of the osculating plane
to the orthogonal trajectory of the generators, i.e.\ to a curve
$v = \text{const}$, are
\[
\lambda\xi_i =   \frac{\partial    }{\partial r_i}
  \left\lvert rx \frac{\partial   x}{\partial u  }
                 \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \right\rvert,
\]
\[
(y\xi) = 0.
\]

This shows that the generators are binormals to their
orthogonal trajectories. Our given surface may be written
in the form
\[
x_i = x_i(u) \cos\frac{v}{k} + \xi_i(u) \sin\frac{v}{k},
\]
\[
ds^2 = dv^2 +
  \Bigl[                 \cos^2\frac{v}{k}
       + \frac{k^2}{T^2} \sin^2\frac{v}{k} \Bigr] du^2.
\]

This reduces to
\[
du^2+dv^2,
\]
when, and only when
\[
\frac{1}{T^2} = \frac{1}{k^2}.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~21. The necessary and sufficient condition that a
ruled surface should have Gaussian curvature zero is that it
should be generated by the binormals to a curve whose
squared torsion\index{Torsion} is equal to the measure of curvature of
space\index{Measure02@--- of curvature of space}.

The proof given holds equally in hyperbolic space; the
surface is, however, in that case imaginary. If we compare
theorems 16 and 21, we get

%[File: 209.png]
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~22. The necessary and sufficient condition that
it should be possible to assemble the
normals\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}\index{Congruence10@--- of normals, to surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero}
to a surface\index{Normals02@--- to surface} into
one parameter families of left (right)
paratactics\index{Left and right parataxy}\index{Parataxy}\index{Right and left parataxy}, is that the
given surface should have
Gaussian curvature\index{Curvature02@--- Gaussian}
zero\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero}. It will,
then, be possible to assemble the normals into families of
right (left) paratactics also. The intersections of the given
surface with the various families of paratactics will be the
asymptotic lines of the former.

\mysect{Geodesic curvature and geodesic lines}
We shall, as in euclidean space, define as the geodesic
curvature at any point of a curve on our surface, the curvature
of its orthogonal projection on the tangent plane at that point.
Let us denote this by $\dfrac{1}{\rho_g}$, while $\sigma$ is the angle which the
osculating plane makes with the tangent plane to the surface.
Then, applying Meunier's\index{Meunier} theorem to the projecting cone
\[
  \frac{1}{\rho_g} = \frac{\cos\sigma}{\rho}.     \tag{28}
\]

As a first exercise, assuming $F = 0$, let us find the geodesic
curvature\index{Curvature03@--- geodesic}\index{Geodesic01@Geodesic curvature} of one of our parameter lines
\begin{align*}
  ds_v &= \sqrt{G}\,dv,
\\
  t_i  &= \frac{k}{\sqrt{G}} \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v},
\end{align*}
\[
  \frac{z_i}{\rho} = \frac{dt_i}{ds} + \frac{x_i}{k}
= \frac{k}{\sqrt{G}}
  \left[ \frac{\partial}{\partial v}
         \Bigl(\frac{1}{\sqrt{G}}
         \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}\Bigr)
  \right] + \frac{x_i}{k}.
\]

To find $\cos\sigma$ must determine the distance of $(z)$ from
the point orthogonal to $(x)$ on the curve $v =$ const., i.e.\ to the
point
$\dfrac{k}{\sqrt{E}}\Bigl( \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr)$.
\[
  \cos\frac{\sigma}{\rho}
= \frac{1}{\sqrt{EG}}
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
         \frac{\partial}{\partial v}
         \bigl( \frac{1}{\sqrt{G}}
                \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \bigr)
  \Bigr),
\]
\[
  \frac{1}{\rho_g}
= \frac{-1}{\sqrt{EG}} \frac{\partial \sqrt{G}}{\partial u}.  \tag{29}
\]

For the other parameter line
\[
  \frac{1}{\rho_g}
= \frac{-1}{\sqrt{EG}} \frac{\partial \sqrt{E}}{\partial v}.
\]

Let us now, more generally, find the geodesic curvature of
the curve
\[
  v \cdot {} = v \cdot(u).
\]

%[File: 210.png]
Once more we shall make use of the isotropic\index{Isotropic01@Isotropic curves} parameters,
so that

\[ E = G = 0,
\]

\[ ds = \sqrt{2Fv'}du, \quad v'= \frac{dv}{du},
\]
\ungap

\begin{multline*}
\quad t_i = \frac{k}{\sqrt{2Fv'}}\Bigl[\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} + v'\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}\Bigr],
\\
\shoveleft{\frac{z_i}{\rho} = \frac{k}{2Fv'}\Bigl[\frac{\partial^2 x_i}
{\partial u^2} + 2\frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u\,\partial v}v' +
\frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial v^2}v'^2 + \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}v''\Bigr] +}
\\
+ \frac{k}{\sqrt{2Fv'}}\Bigl[\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} +
\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}v'\Bigr]\frac{d}{du}\frac{1}{\sqrt{2Fv'}} + \frac{x_i}{k}.
\end{multline*}
For an orthogonal trajectory to this curve
\begin{align*}
\frac{\delta v}{\delta u} &= - \frac{dv}{du} = - v', \\
\delta s &= \delta u \sqrt{-2Fv'}, \\
\bar{t}_i &= \frac{-k}{\sqrt{-2Fv'}}\left[ \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} -
v'\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v}\right], \\
\cos\frac{\sigma}{\rho} &= \frac{1}{\rho k^2}(z\bar{t}), \\
\frac{1}{\rho_g} &= \frac{-1}{\sqrt{-2Fv'}}\left[\frac{\dfrac
{\partial F}{\partial v}v' - \dfrac{\partial F}{\partial u}}{2F} +
\frac{v''}{2v'}\right] \\
&\qquad = \frac1{\sqrt{-F^2}}\Bigl[\frac d{du}\frac{\sqrt{F}}{\sqrt{2v'}} -
\frac{\partial}{\partial v}\sqrt{2Fv'}\Bigr].\tag{30}
\end{align*}

What will be the nature of those curves whose geodesic
curvature\index{Curvature03@--- geodesic}\index{Geodesic01@Geodesic curvature}
vanishes, i.e.\ those curves whose osculating planes
pass through the normal? These shall be called geodesic
lines\index{Geodesic02@--- lines}, and, evidently, we shall have

\[ \frac{dv}{du}\frac{\sqrt{F}}{\sqrt{2v'}} =
\frac{\partial}{\partial v}\sqrt{2Fv'}. \]

This merely tells us that our given curve is an extremal,
i.e.\ the first variation of the length between two fixed points
is zero. If we assume that two sufficiently near points can
always be connected by a curve of minimum length%
\footnote{For a proof of the existence of this curve, see Bolza\index{Bolza},
\emph{Lectures on the Calculus of Variations}, Chicago, 1904, Ch. VIII.}
%endfootnote
we shall get

%[File: 211.png]
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~23.  The curve of shortest length between two
points of a surface is a geodesic line\index{Geodesic02@--- lines}.

Remembering 21, we have further


\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~24. The orthogonal trajectories of a family of
paratactic lines are geodesics of the surface generated by
these lines.

If we consider the two planes through the normal\index{Normals02@--- to surface} to a
surface and the two tangents to the lines of curvature, we
see that they are mutually perpendicular, and that each
touches the focal surface of the congruence of normals at the
point of intersection of the two adjacent normals in the other
plane.%
\footnote{For a simple proof of this general theorem see Picard\index{Picard}, loc.\ cit., vol.~i,
pp 307, 308.}
%endfootnote

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~25. In any congruence of normals\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}, the edges of
regression of the developable surfaces are geodesics of the
focal surfaces\index{Focal05@--- surfaces} of the congruence.

The osculating plane to any straight line is indeterminate;
the line is, therefore, a geodesic for all space; a result also
evident from Chapter II.~30. It is also clear that as the
expressions for the geodesic curvature of a parameter line in
terms of $E$, $F$, $G$ and their derivatives are the same in euclidean
and in non-euclidean space, and the formula for the distance
element is written in the same shape, so will the formula for
the geodesic curvature of any curve be the same. We might,
for instance, have given this formula in terms of the Beltrami\index{Beltrami}
invariants. We have, however, purposely avoided the introduction
of these into the present work, and will therefore
merely refer the reader to the current textbooks in differential
geometry,%
\footnote{e.g.\ Bianchi\index{Bianchi}, \emph{Differentialgeometrie}, cit.\ p.~258.}
%endfootnote

\mysect{Necessary conditions for a minimal surface}
\index{Minimal surfaces|(}

As a last problem in the differential geometry of surfaces
let us take up that of minimal surfaces. To begin with, what
will be the element of area? It is perfectly clear that the
expression for this will be the same as that in the euclidean
case. The sine of the angle formed by the parameter lines
will be, by (15)
\[
  \frac{\sqrt{EG-F^2}}{\sqrt{EG}},
\]
and the area of the elementary quadrilateral
\[
  \sqrt{EG-F^2}du\,dv.
\]

%[File: 212.png]
Let us, in particular, take the lines of curvature as parameter
lines. The formula for the area enclosed by a given
curve will be
\[
\iint\sqrt{EG}du \,dv.
\]

Let us compare this with the area\index{Area01@Area} enclosed by this curve
upon a surface reached by laying off on each normal an
extremely small distance $w(uv)$.
\begin{align*}
\overline{x_i} &= x_i\cos\frac{w}{k} + y_i\sin\frac{w}{k},
\\
d\overline{x_i}&=dx_i\cos\frac{w}{k} +dy_i\sin\frac{w}{k} -
\frac{1}{k}\left[ x_i\cos\frac{w}{k} - y_i\sin\frac{w}{k} \right]dw.
\end{align*}

The squared element of arc for this surface will be by (22)
\[
d\overline{s}^2 =
  E\left[ \cos\frac{w}{k} +
    \frac{\sin\dfrac{w}{k}}{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}} \right]^2 du^2 +
  G\left[ \cos\frac{w}{k} +
    \frac{\sin\dfrac{w}{k}}{\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} \right]   dv^2 +
  \frac{dw^2}{k^2}.
\]

This becomes, when we neglect powers of $w$ above the first,
\[
d\overline{s}^2 =
  E\left[1 + \frac{2\dfrac{w}{k}}{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}}\right] du^2 +
  G\left[1 + \frac{2\dfrac{w}{k}}{\tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}\right] dv^2.
\]

For the surface element we have
\[
\sqrt{EG}\left[1+
  2\frac{w}{k}
  \left( \frac{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k} + \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}
              {\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}   \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} \right) +
  4\frac{\dfrac{w^2}{k^2}}
              {\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}   \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}
  \right]^\frac{1}{2} du \,dv.
\]

Developing by the binomial theorem, and neglecting higher
powers of $w$ we have
\[
\iint\sqrt{EG}
  \left[ 1
       + \frac{w}{k}
         \left( \frac{\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k} + \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}}
                     {\tan\dfrac{r_1}{k}   \tan\dfrac{r_2}{k}} \right)
  \right] du \,dv.
\]

%[File: 213.png]
If we define as a minimal surface one where the first
variation of the area is zero, certainly a necessary condition,
we have

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~26. The necessary and sufficient condition that a
surface should be minimal is that the mean relative curvature
should be zero.

We see from (23) that the numerator of the expression for
the relative mean curvature\index{Curvature04@--- mean relative} is the simultaneous invariant
of (13) and (20), and vanishes when, and only when, the
tangents to the asymptotic lines\index{Asymptotic lines} are harmonically separated
by those to the isotropic ones, hence

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~27. The necessary and sufficient condition that a
surface should be minimal is that the asymptotic lines should
form an orthogonal system.

This theorem justifies our statement in Chapter X that a
Clifford\index{Clifford} surface is a minimal surface. It is very interesting
that in non-euclidean space we should have an algebraic
minimal surface (other than the plane) whose order is as low
as two.

We may go one long step further towards the solution of
the problem of minimal surfaces, namely, exhibit the differential
equations on which they depend.%
\footnote{Cf.\ Darboux\index{Darboux}, \emph{Leçons
sur la th\'eorie g\'en\'erale des surfaces}, vol.~iii, ch.~xiv, Paris,
1894. The reader is strongly urged to read this interesting chapter in connection with the present work.}
%endfootnote

\mysect{Integration of the resulting differential equations}
We shall once more take as parameter lines the isotropic
ones. These will form a conjugate system, since they are
harmonically separated by the asymptotic lines, hence
\[
  E = G = D' = 0,
\]
\[
  \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u\,\partial v}
= Ax_i + B\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}
       + C\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v},
\]
\[
  BF = \frac{1}{2}\frac{\partial G}{\partial u} = 0, \quad
  CF = \frac{1}{2}\frac{\partial E}{\partial u} = 0, \quad
  F = -Ak^2,
\]
\[
  \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u\,\partial v} + \frac{1}{k^2}Fx_i = 0.
\tag{31}
\]

It is merely necessary to find $F$ and take for $(x)$ four
solutions of (3) subject to the restriction $(xx) = k^2$.
Let us put
\[
  \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u^2}
= P\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u}
+ Q\frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} + Rx_i + Sy_i,
\]
%[File: 214.png]
which is certainly possible, since
\begin{equation*}
   \left| x y \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right| \not\equiv 0.
\end{equation*}

We easily find
\begin{equation*}
   R = Q = 0, \quad FP = \frac{\partial F}{\partial u} ,
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
   \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u^2} = \frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial w} \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} + S y_i.
\end{equation*}

\label{d3x}Now
\begin{equation*}
   \frac{\partial}{\partial v} \Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \Bigr) = 2 \Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial^3 x}{\partial u^2 \, \partial v} \Bigr) = -2 \Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial}{\partial u} (Fx) \Bigr) = 0,
\text{\ by (31)}.
\end{equation*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent Hence}
   &&\Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \Bigr) = \phi (u).
&\phantom{\text{\indent Hence}}
\end{flalign*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent If}
   &&\phi (u) \equiv 0, \quad D = 0.
&\phantom{\text{\indent If}}
\end{flalign*}

The total relative curvature is zero, and the surface is
developable. In a developable surface the asymptotic lines\index{Asymptotic lines}
fall together, by (24); hence a minimal developable must be
circumscribed to the Absolute, and cannot be real in the
actual domain. Conversely it is clear that every developable
circumscribed to the Absolute is a minimal surface in that its
asymptotic lines are mutually perpendicular, even though it
lie in a region of our space where the concept area has not
been defined.

In the second case let us suppose $\phi (u) \not\equiv 0$.

Let us replace $u$ by $\bar{u} (u)$ so that $\Bigl( \dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial \bar{u}}
\dfrac{\partial^2 x}{\partial \bar{u}} \Bigr)
= \dfrac{1}{k^2}$. Then
replace the letter $\bar{u}$ by the letter $u$ once more.

\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent Then}
&& S = \frac{1}{k^2}, \quad \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u^2} =
   \frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial u} \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} + \frac{y_i}{k^2}.
&\phantom{\text{\indent Then}}
\end{flalign*}
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent In like manner}
&& \frac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial v^2} =
   \frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial v} \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} + \frac{y_i}{k^2}.
&\phantom{\text{\indent In like manner}}
\end{flalign*}

Multiplying through by $\dfrac{\partial^2 x_i}{\partial u^2}$ and adding
\begin{equation*}
   \Bigl( \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2} \Bigr) =
   \frac{1}{k^2} + \frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial u} \frac{\partial F}{\partial v}.
\end{equation*}

%[File: 215.png]
On the other hand

\begin{align*}
\frac{\partial^2 F}{\partial u \,\partial v}=&
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}
  \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}\Bigr) +
  \Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u}\frac{\partial^3 x}{\partial u \,\partial v^2}\Bigr),\\
=&\Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2}
  \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}\Bigr)-\frac{1}{k^2}\Bigl(\frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
  \frac{\partial}{\partial v} (xF)\Bigr),\\
=&\Bigl(\frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial u^2} \frac{\partial^2 x}{\partial v^2}\Bigr)-\frac{1}{k^2}F^2,\\
\frac{\partial^2 F}{\partial u \,\partial v} =&
  \frac{1-F^2}{k^2}+\frac{1}{F} \frac{\partial F}{\partial u} \frac{\partial F}{\partial v},
\end{align*}
\[
k^2 \frac{\partial^2\log F}{\partial u \,\partial v}=\frac{1}{F}-F.\tag{32}
\]
\begin{flalign*}
&\text{\indent Lastly, let us put}
&&F=e^{2iw},
&\phantom{\text{\indent Lastly, let us put}}
\end{flalign*}
\begin{equation*}
k^2 \frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial u \,\partial v}+\sin2w=0.\tag{33}
\end{equation*}

When $F$ has been found we may, as already noted, find $(x)$
from (31).
\index{Minimal surfaces|)}

%[File: 216.png]
%CHAPTER XVI
\mychap{XVI}{DIFFERENTIAL LINE-GEOMETRY}
\mysect{Analoga of Kummer's coefficients}
\index{Congruence07@--- of lines, analytic|(}

In Chapter IX we gave the foundations of the Pl\"uckerian
line-geometry, and the fundamental invariants of a metrical
character; in Chapter X we saw what advantages arose from
taking the cross instead of the line as element, and introducing
suitable coordinates. Chapter XV was given to the
differential geometry of curves and surfaces. It is the object
of the present chapter to draw all of these threads together
into a theory of differential line-geometry, and, in particular,
a theory of two-parameter line systems or congruences.%
\footnote{The first part of the present chapter follows, with slight modifications,
a rather inaccessible memoir by Fibbi\index{Fibbi}, `I sistemi doppiamente infiniti di
raggi negli spazii di curvatura costante,' \emph{Annali della R. Scuola Normale Superiore},
Pisa, 1891.}
%endfootnote

We shall define as an analytic line-congruence a system
whose Pl\"uckerian coordinates are analytic functions of two
independent parameters, say $u$ and $v$. This is equivalent to
supposing that our lines are determined by two points, which
we may assume mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}, whose coordinates are
analytic functions of the two independent parameters in
question.
\begin{equation*}
\tag{1}
x_i = x_i(uv), \quad y_i = y_i(uv), \quad
(xx) = (yy) = k^2, \quad (xy) = 0.
\end{equation*}

Following Kummer's\index{Kummer} classical method, we shall write the
following fundamental quadratic expression:
\begin{multline*}
k^2(dx\,dx)-(y\,dx)^2 =
  \begin{Vmatrix}
     y_0 &  y_1 &  y_2 &  y_3 \\
    dx_0 & dx_1 & dx_2 & dx_3
  \end{Vmatrix}^2
\\= Edu^2 + 2Fdu\,dv + Gdv^2, \phantom{(2)'''}
\end{multline*}\ungap
\begin{multline*}
\tag{2}
k^2(dy\,dy)-(x\,dy)^2 =
  \begin{Vmatrix}
     x_0 &  x_1 &  x_2 &  x_3 \\
    dy_0 & dy_1 & dy_2 & dy_3
  \end{Vmatrix}^2
\\= E'du^2 + 2F'du\,dv + G'dv^2.
\end{multline*}\ungap\label{dudv}
\begin{align*}
k^2(dx\,&dy) = e\,du^2 + (f + f')du\,dv + g\,dv^2,
\\
&k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
           \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr) -
    \Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr)^2 = E,
\\
&k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
           \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr) -
    \Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr)
    \Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr) = F,
\\
%[File: 217.png]
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
            \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)
-    \Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)^2 = G.   \tag{3}
\\
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
-    \Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)^2 = E',
\\
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)
-    \Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
     \Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr) = F',
\\
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial y}{\partial v}
            \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)
-    \Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)^2 = G'.   \tag{4}
\\
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
     \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr) = e,\phantom{f'}
\quad
  k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
     \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr) = f,
\\
& k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
     \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr) = f',\phantom{e}
\quad
  k^2\Bigl( \frac{\partial y}{\partial v}
     \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr) = g.   \tag{5}
\\
  EG - F^2
&= \left| yx \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right|^2 \equiv \Delta^2,
\quad
  E'G' - F'^2
= \left| xy \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}
            \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \right|^2 \equiv \Delta'^2.
\tag{6}
\end{align*}

\mysect{Their fundamental relations}
The following relations will subsist between these various
expressions:
\[
  \Delta x_i = k^2\frac{\partial \Delta}{\partial x_i},\quad
  \Delta' y_i = k^2\frac{\partial \Delta'}{\partial y_i},
\]
\begin{align*}
&\text{since}
&(xy)
& = \Bigl( x \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr)
= \Bigl( x \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)
= \Bigl( y \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
= \Bigl( y \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr) = 0,
&\phantom{\text{since}}
\end{align*}
\begin{align*}
& E' = k^2 \Bigl( \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}
                  \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
     - \frac{k^4}{\Delta^2}
       \left| y \frac{\partial y}{\partial u}
                \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \right|^2,
\\
& E' = \frac{1}{\Delta^2} [Ge^2 - 2Fef + Ef^2],
\\
& F' = \frac{1}{\Delta^2} [Gef' - F(eg + f\!f') + Efg].   \tag{7}
\\
& G' = \frac{1}{\Delta^2} [Gf'^2 - 2F(f'g) + Eg^2],
\\
& E = \frac{1}{\Delta'^2} [G'e^2 - 2F'ef' + E'f'^2].    \tag{8}
\\
& F = \frac{1}{\Delta'^2} [G'ef - F'(eg + f\!f') + E'f'g],
\\
& G = \frac{1}{\Delta'^2} [G'f^2 - 2F'(fg) + E'g^2],
\end{align*}\label{gprime}
\[
\Delta\Delta' = (eg - f\!f').                           \tag{9}
\]

%[File: 218.png]
Notice that $\Delta$ and $\Delta'$ being square roots of positive definite
forms cannot vanish in the real domain.

We remember from Chapter IX, that two lines which are
not paratactic have two common perpendiculars\index{Perpendicularity} meeting them
in pairs of mutually orthogonal points\index{Orthogonal points}. Let us, as a first
problem, find where the common perpendicular to a line of
our congruence and an adjacent line meets the given line.
The coordinates of an arbitrary point of our line may be
written $\left( x\cos\dfrac{r}{k} + y\sin\dfrac{r}{k} \right)$ while an arbitrary point of an
adjacent line will be $\lambda(x + dx) + \mu(y + dy)$.

Let us begin by writing that the second of these points is
orthogonal to $\left( x\sin\dfrac{r}{k} - y\cos\dfrac{r}{k} \right)$ the point of the first line
orthogonal to the first point, while, on the other hand, the first
point lies in the absolute polar plane of
$\mu(x + dx) - \lambda(y + dy)$.
There will result two linear homogeneous equations in $\lambda$ and $\mu$
whose determinant must be equated to zero. When this is
simplified in view of the identities
\[
  (x\,dx) = -\tfrac{1}{2}(dx\,dx), \quad
  (y\,dy) = -\tfrac{1}{2}(dy\,dy),
\]
\[
  (x\,dy) + (y\,dx) = -(dx\,dy),
\]
we shall have
\begin{multline*}
  \left|
  \begin{aligned}
   &[k^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}(dx\,dx)]\sin\frac{r}{k}
  - (y\,dx)\cos\frac{r}{k}   \\
  -&[k^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}(dy\,dy)]\sin\frac{r}{k}
  - (x\,dy)\cos\frac{r}{k}
  \end{aligned}
  \right.
\\
  \left.
  \begin{aligned}
    (x\,dy)\sin\frac{r}{k}
  -&[k^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}(dy\,dy)]\cos\frac{r}{k}\\
    (y\,dx)\sin\frac{r}{k}
  +&[k^2 - \tfrac{1}{2}(dx\,dx)]\cos\frac{r}{k}
  \end{aligned}
  \right| = 0.   \tag{10}
\end{multline*}
Casting aside infinitesimals above the second order
\begin{multline*}
  k^2(dx\,dy) \left( \cos^2\frac{r}{k} - \sin^2\frac{r}{k} \right)
\\
- \left[k^2(dx\,dx) - (y\,dx)^2 - k^2(dy\,dy) + (x\,dy)^2\right]
   \sin\frac{r}{k}\cos\frac{r}{k} = 0, \phantom{(11)}
\end{multline*}\ungap
\begin{multline*}
  (e\,du^2 + (f+f')du\,dv + g\,dv^2)
   \left( \cos^2\frac{r}{k} - \sin^2\frac{r}{k} \right)
\\
+ \left[(E-E')du^2 + 2(F-F')du\,dv + (G-G')dv^2\right]
   \sin\frac{r}{k}\cos\frac{r}{k} = 0.         \tag{11}
\end{multline*}

%[File: 219.png]
This will give $\infty^1$ determinations for $r$ in the general case
where
\begin{equation*}
e : \left( \frac{f+f'}{2} \right) : g \not\equiv (E-E') : (F-F') : (G-G'), \tag{12}
\end{equation*}
and, as we saw in Chapter X, Theorem 5, with the corresponding
elliptic case, these common perpendiculars will
generate a surface of the fourth order, analogous to the
euclidean cylindroid. We shall call a congruence where
inequality (12) holds a `general' congruence\index{Congruence08@--- of lines, general}.

Let us now ask what are the maximum and minimum
values for $r$ in (11). Equating to zero the partial derivatives
to $du$ and $dv$ we get
\[
\Bigl[ e\,du + \frac{f+f'}{2}dv \Bigr]
  \left(\tan^2\frac{r}{k}-1 \right)
  +[(E-E')du + (F-F')dv]\tan\frac{r}{k} = 0,
\]
\[
\Bigl[\frac{(f+f')}{2}du + g\,dv \Bigr]
  \left(\tan^2\frac{r}{k}-1 \right)
  +[(F-F')du + (G-G')dv]\tan\frac{r}{k} = 0.
\]
\label{noplus}

Eliminating $r$ we have
\begin{multline*}
\Bigl[e(F-F') - \frac{(f+f')}{2}(E-E')\Bigr]du^2 \\
  +[e(G-G')-g(E-E')]du\,dv \hspace{0.9in} \\
  +\Bigl[\frac{(f+f')}{2}(G-G')-g(E-E')\Bigr]dv^2 = 0. \tag{13}
\end{multline*}

Each root of this will give two values to $\tan\dfrac{r}{k}$ corresponding
to two mutually orthogonal points. On the other hand, if we
eliminate $du : dv$ we get
\begin{multline*}
\left(eg-\tfrac{1}{4}(f+f')^2\right)
  \left(\tan^2\frac{r}{k}-1\right)^2 +
  [e(G-G') \\
  \quad -(F-F')(f+f') + g(E-E')]
  \left(\tan^2\frac{r}{k}-1\right)\tan\frac{r}{k} \\
  + [(E-E')(G-G')-(F-F')^2]\tan^2\frac{r}{k} = 0.\tag{14}
\end{multline*}

\mysect{Limiting points and focal points}
The left-hand side of this equation is the discriminant
of (11) looked upon as an equation in $du: dv$. It gives,
therefore, those points of the given line where the two perpendiculars
coalesce. Such points shall be called `limiting
%[File: 220.png]
points'\index{Limiting points and planes}. They will determine two regions (when real) point
by point mutually orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}, which contain the intersections
of the line with the real common perpendiculars\index{Perpendicularity}. In the
same way we might find limiting planes through the line
determining two dihedral angles whose faces are, in pairs,
mutually perpendicular, and which when real, with their
verticals, determine all planes wherein lie all real common
perpendiculars to the given line and its immediate neighbours.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~1. A line of a
general analytic congruence
contains four limiting points,
mutually orthogonal in pairs,
and these, when real, determine
two real regions of the line
where it meets the real common perpendiculars
with adjacent lines of the congruence.
They are also the points where
the two perpendiculars coincide.}
{\emph{Theorem}~1$'$. Through a
line of a general analytic congruence will pass four limiting
planes, mutually perpendicular in pairs, and these, when
real, determine two real regions of the axial pencil
through the line which contain all planes wherein are
real common perpendiculars
to the line and adjacent lines
of the congruence. They are
also the planes in which the
two perpendiculars coincide.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

We shall now look more closely into the question of the
reality of limiting points and places. We may so choose our
coordinate system that the equations of the line in question
shall be $x_1 = x_2 = 0$. Reverting to equation (8) of Chapter
X the equation of the ruled quartic surface will be, in the
hyperbolic case\label{hyberbolic}
\[
  a(-\dot{x}_0{}^2 + \dot{x}_3{}^2 )\dot{x}_1\dot{x}_2
+ b( \dot{x}_1{}^2 + \dot{x}_2{}^2 )x_0 x_3 = 0.   \tag{$15$}
\]

Let the reader show%
\footnote{See the author's \emph{Dual Projective Geometry}, cit., p.~26.}
%endfootnote
that in the elliptic case we shall have
\[
  (a_1 - a_2)(x_0{}^2 + x_3{}^2)x_1x_2
+ (a_1 + a_2)(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2)x_0x_3 = 0.   \tag{15$'$}
\]

To find the limiting points on the line $x_1 = x_2 = 0$, equate
to zero the discriminant of this looked upon as an equation in
\[
  \dot{x}_1  : \dot{x}_2 \quad \text{\ or } \quad x_1  : x_2.
\]
\[
  a^2(-\dot{x}_0{}^2 + \dot{x}_3{}^2) - 4b^2\dot{x}_0{}^2\dot{x}_3{}^2 = 0.
\tag{$16$}
\]
\[
  (a_1 - a_2)^2(x_0{}^2 + x_3{}^2)^2 - 4(a_1 + a_2)^2 x_0{}^2 x_3{}^2 = 0.  \tag{16$'$}
\]

In like manner for the limiting planes we shall have
\[
  b^2(\dot{x}_1{}^2 + \dot{x}_2{}^2)^2 + 4a^2\dot{x}_1{}^2\dot{x}_2{}^2 = 0.      \tag{$17$}
\]
\[
  (a_1 + a_2)^2(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2) - 4(a_1 - a_2)^2x_1{}^2 x_2{}^2 = 0.
\tag{17$'$}
\]

%[File: 221.png]
Notice that the centres of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of the limiting points
are $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ $(0, 0,$ $0, 1)$; while the bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of the dihedral
angles of the limiting planes\index{Limiting points and planes} are $(0, 1,$ $0, 0)$ $(0, 0, 1, 0)$.

If we look more closely into the roots of the last four
equations we see that the roots of (16) are all real, those of
(17) all imaginary. As for the two equations (16$'$) and (17$'$)
the one will have real roots, the other imaginary ones, whence

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~2. In hyperbolic space the limiting points of an
actual line are real, and the limiting planes imaginary. In
elliptic space this may occur, or the planes may be all real
and the points all imaginary.

Giving to $x_0:x_3$ one of the values from (16$'$) we see that
\begin{equation*}
\frac{x_0{}^2 + x_3{}^2}{x_0x_3}= \pm\frac{2(a_1 +a_2)}{a_1 - a_2}.
\end{equation*}

Substituting in (15$'$) we have

\begin{equation*}
x_1+x_2=0 \quad \text{\ or } \quad x_1-x_2=0
\end{equation*}

The four limiting points will yield but these two planes,
hence

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~3. The perpendiculars\index{Perpendicularity}
at the limiting points
line in two planes called
`principal planes'\index{Principal points and planes} whose dihedral
angles have the same
bisectors as pairs of limiting
planes.}
{\emph{Theorem}~3$'$. The perpendiculars
in the limiting planes
meet the line in two points
called `principal points' whose
centres of gravity are those of
two pairs of limiting points.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Reverting to (16$'$) we see that we may also write

\begin{equation*}
x_0:x_3= \pm(\sqrt{a_1}\pm\sqrt{a_2}):(\sqrt{a_1}\mp\sqrt{a_2}).
\end{equation*}

Let us pick out a pair of limiting points which are not
mutually orthogonal, say\label{pointpair}
\begin{equation*}
(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2}, 0, 0, \sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2})\;
(-(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2}), 0, 0, \sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2}).
\end{equation*}

The perpendicular from the point $(x)$ to the line $x_1=x_2=0$
meets it in the point $(x_0, 0, 0, x_3)$. Calling $d_1$, $d_2$ the distances
thence to the limiting points just chosen we have
\begin{align*}
\tan{\frac{d_1}{k}}&=\frac{(\sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2})x_0-
(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2})x_3}{(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2})x_0+(\sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2})x_3},\\
\tan{\frac{d_2}{k}}&=\frac{(\sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2})x_0+
(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2})x_3}{-(\sqrt{a_1}+\sqrt{a_2})x_0+(\sqrt{a_1}-\sqrt{a_2})x_3}.
\end{align*}

%[File: 222.png]
Further, let $(\omega)$ be the angle which the plane through
$x_1 = x_2 = 0$ and $(x)$ makes with the principal plane
\[
  x_1 + x_2 = 0.
\]
\[
  \cos^2\omega = \frac{(x_1 - x_2)^2}{2(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2)},\quad
  \sin^2\omega = \frac{(x_1 + x_2)^2}{2(x_1{}^2 + x_2{}^2)},
\]
\[
  \tan\frac{d_1}{k}\cos^2\omega + \tan\frac{d_2}{k}\sin^2\omega = 0.
\tag{18}
\]

This is, of course, the direct analog of Hamilton's\index{Hamilton} well-known formula for the cylindroid.%
\footnote{For the Hamiltonian equation see Bianchi, \emph{Differentialgeometrie}, cit., p.~261.
For the non-euclidean form here given, cf.\ Fibbi\index{Fibbi}, loc.\ cit., p.~57. Fibbi's work
is burdened with many long formulae; one cannot help admiring his skill
in handling such cumbersome expressions at all.}
%endfootnote

Returning to the notations wherewith we opened the
present chapter, let us find the focal points\index{Focal04@--- points and planes} of our line, i.e.\ the
points where it intersects adjacent lines of the congruence,
or rather, the points where the distance becomes infinitesimal
to a higher order. Here, if the focal point be
\[
  \Bigl( x\cos\frac{r}{k} + y\sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr),
\]
we shall have
\begin{multline*}
  x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} + y_i\sin\frac{r}{k}
= (x_i + dx_i)\cos\frac{r+dr}{k} + (y_i + dy_i)\sin\frac{r+dr}{k}
\\
  dx_i\cos\frac{r}{k} + dy_i\sin\frac{r}{k}
- \frac{1}{k} \Bigl( x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} - y_i\sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr)dr = 0.
\end{multline*}
\[
  k\,dr = (x\,dy),
\]
\begin{multline*}
  \Bigl[
    k^2 \Bigl( \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial u} \cos\frac{r}{k}
             + \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial u} \sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr)
    - \Bigl( x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} - y_i\sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr)
    \Bigl( x \frac{\partial y}{\partial u} \Bigr)
  \Bigr] du +
\\
+ \Bigl[
    k^2 \Bigl( \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial v} \cos\frac{r}{k}
             + \frac{\partial y_i}{\partial v} \sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr)
    - \Bigl( x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} - y_i\sin\frac{r}{k} \Bigr)
    \Bigl( x \frac{\partial y}{\partial v} \Bigr)
  \Bigr] dv = 0.
\end{multline*}

Multiplying through by $\dfrac{\partial y_i}{\partial u}$ and adding, then multiplying
through by $\dfrac{\partial y_i}{\partial v}$ and adding again
\begin{align*}
  [e\,du + f\,dv]\cos\frac{r}{k} + [E'du + F'dv]\sin\frac{r}{k} = 0,\\
  [f'du + g\,dv]\cos\frac{r}{k} + [F'du + G'dv]\sin\frac{r}{k} = 0.
\end{align*}

%[File: 223.png]
Replacing $(y\,dx)$ by $(-x\,dy)$ we have, similarly

\[\tag{19}
\begin{split}
\left[e\,du+f'dv\right]\sin{\frac{r}{k}}+[Edu+Fdv]\cos{\frac{r}{k}}=0,
\\
\left[f\,du+g\,dv\right]\sin{\frac{r}{k}}+[Fdu+Gdv]\cos{\frac{r}{k}}=0.
\end{split}
\]

Eliminating $r$
\[\tag{20}
\begin{split}
(E'f'-F'e)du^2+[E'g-F'(f-f')-G'e]du\,dv+(F'g-G'f)dv^2=0&,
\\
(Ef-Fe)du^2+[Eg-F(f'-f)-Ge]du\,dv+(Fg-Gf)dv^2=0&.
\end{split}
\]

Eliminating $du:dv$
\[\tag{21}
\begin{split}
(E'G'-F')^2\tan^2{\frac{r}{k}}+[E'g-F'(f+f')+G'e]\tan{\frac{r}{k}}+
(eg-f\!f')=0&,
\\
(eg-f\!f')\tan^2{\frac{r}{k}}+[Eg-F(f+f')+Ge]
\tan{\frac{r}{k}}+(EG-F^2)=0&.
\end{split}
\]

Subtracting one of these equations from the other
\begin{multline*}
[(eg-f\!f')-(E'G'-F'^2)]\tan^2{\frac{r}{k}}+[(E-E')g-(F-F')(f+f') \\
+(G-G')e]\tan{\frac{r}{k}}+[(EG-F^2)-(eg-f\!f')]=0.\tag{22}
\end{multline*}

We see at once that the middle coefficients are identical in
(14) and (22), and these will vanish when, and only when, we
are measuring from a centre of gravity\index{Centre03@--- of gravity of points} of the roots.

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~4. The centres of gravity of the focal points
are identical with those of two pairs of limiting points\index{Limiting points and planes}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~4$'$. The bisectors\index{Bisector of an angle} of the dihedral angles of
two focal planes\index{Focal04@--- points and planes} are identical with those of
two pairs of limiting planes.}

\mysect{Necessary and sufficient conditions for a normal congruence}
The focal properties of a congruence of
normals\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}\index{Normals02@--- to surface}
are especially
interesting. Here we may suppose that $(y)$ is the
Absolute pole of the tangent plane to the surface described
by $(x)$. We have then
\begin{equation*}
\Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial u}\Bigr)=\Bigl(y\frac{\partial x}{\partial v}\Bigr)=\Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial u}\Bigr)=\Bigl(x\frac{\partial y}{\partial v}\Bigr)=0,
\end{equation*}

\begin{equation*}
-\Bigl(x\frac{\partial^2 y}{\partial u \,\partial v}\Bigr)=f=f'.
\end{equation*}

%[File: 224.png]
Suppose, conversely, that
\[
  f = f'.
\]

Let us put $\bar{x}_i = x_i\cos\dfrac{r}{k} + y_i\sin\dfrac{r}{k}$ and show that we may
find $r$ so that our line is normal\index{Normals02@--- to surface} to the surface traced by $(\bar{x})$.
For this it is necessary and sufficient that the point of the
line orthogonal to $(\bar{x})$ should be orthogonal to every displacement of $(\bar{x})$. This point being
$\left( x\sin\dfrac{r}{k} - y\cos\dfrac{r}{k} \right)$, we must
have
\[
  \sin\frac{r}{k}(x\,d\bar{x}) - \cos\frac{r}{k}(y\,d\bar{x}) = 0,
\]
\[
  (y\,dx) = -k\,dr,
\]
and $(y\,dx)$ must be an exact differential, i.e.
\[
  \frac{\partial}{\partial u}
  \Bigl( y\frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)
= \frac{\partial}{\partial v}
  \Bigl( y\frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr), \quad
  f = f'.   \tag{23}
\]

This condition can be put into a more geometrical form.
Let us, in fact, find the necessary and sufficient condition that
the focal planes\index{Focal04@--- points and planes} should be mutually perpendicular. Writing
their equations in the form
\[
  \lvert Xxy\,dx \rvert = 0, \quad \lvert Xxy\,\delta x \rvert = 0,
\]
the numerator of the expression for the cosine of their angle
will be
\[
  \begin{vmatrix}
    k^2     & 0      & (y\,\delta x)   \smallskip \\
    0       & k^2    & -\tfrac{1}{2}(\delta x\,\delta x)   \smallskip \\
    (y\,dx) & -\tfrac{1}{2}(dx\,dx) & (dx\,\delta x)
  \end{vmatrix}
= k^2 [k^2(dx\,\delta x) - (y\,dx)(y\,\delta x)].
\]

For perpendicularity,
\[
  Edu\,\delta u + F(du\,\delta v + \delta u\,dv) + Gdv\,\delta v = 0.
\]

Now, by (20),
\[
  \frac{du\,\delta u}{dv\,\delta v} = \frac{Fg-Gf'}{Ef-Fe},
\quad
  \Bigl[\frac{du}{dv} + \frac{\delta u}{\delta v}\Bigr]
= \frac{Ge + F(f'-f)-Eg}{Ef-Fe}.
\]

Hence
\[
  (EG - F^2)(f - f') = 0.
\]

Let us give the name \emph{pseudo-normal} to the absolute polar
of a normal congruence\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}. We thus get

%[File: 225.png]
\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~5. The necessary
and sufficient condition that
a congruence should be normal
is that the focal planes\index{Focal04@--- points and planes} through
each line should be mutually
perpendicular\index{Perpendicularity}.}
{\emph{Theorem}~5$'$. The necessary
and sufficient condition that a
congruence should be pseudo-normal\index{Pseudo-normal congruence}
is that the focal points
on each line should be mutually
orthogonal\index{Orthogonal points}.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

If we subtract one of the equivalent equations (20) from the
other, we get an equation which reduces to (13) when, and
only when
\[
f=f'.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~6. The necessary and sufficient condition that
a general congruence should be composed of
normals\index{Normals02@--- to surface}\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}
is that
the focal points should coincide with a pair of limiting points.

In a normal congruence let us suppose that $(x)$ traces a
surface to which the given lines are normal so that
\[
(y\,dx) =-(x\,dy) = 0.
\]

Let us then put
\[
  \overline{x_i} = x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} + y_i\sin\frac{r}{k},\quad
  \overline{y_i} = x_i\cos\frac{r}{k} - y_i\sin\frac{r}{k},
\]
where $y$ is constant. We see at once that
\[
(\overline{y}\,d\overline{x}) = -(\overline{x}\,d\overline{y}) = 0.
\]

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~7. If a constant distance be laid off on each normal
to a surface from the foot, in such a way that the points
on adjacent normals are on the same side of the tangent
plane\index{Plane} corresponding to either, the locus of the points so found
is a surface with the same normals as the original one.

Let us suppose that we have a normal congruence determined by mutually
orthogonal points $(x)$ and $(y)$, where
$x_i = x_i(uv)$ traces a surface, not one of the orthogonal trajectories
of the congruence. We shall choose as parameter
lines in this surface the isotropic curves, so that
\[
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial u}
         \frac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr) =
  \Bigl( \frac{\partial x}{\partial v}
         \frac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr) = 0.
\]

The sine of the angle which our given line makes with the
normal to this surface is
\[
\sin\theta = \sqrt{
  \frac{2\Bigl(y\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u} \Bigr)
         \Bigl(y\dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)   }
       {k^2\Bigl( \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
                  \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v} \Bigr)}}
\]

%[File: 226.png]
Let all the lines of our congruence\index{Congruence09@--- of normals} be reflected or refracted
in this surface in such a way that
\[
\sin \overline{\theta} = n \sin \theta.
\]

We must replace $y$ by $\overline{y}$ where
\[
\overline{y_i} = ny_i +
  \lambda\frac{\partial}{\partial t_i}
  \left|
    tx
   \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial u}
   \dfrac{\partial x}{\partial v}
  \right|.
\]

It is easily seen that for the new congruence also
\[
f=f'.
\]

\mysect{Malus-Dupin theorem}
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~8. If a normal congruence be subjected to any
finite number of reflections or refractions, the resulting congruence
is normal.

We shall now abandon the general congruence and assume
that, contrary to (12)
\begin{equation*}
\tag{24}
  e  : \frac{f+f'}{2}  : g \equiv
  (E-E')  : (F-F')  : (G-G').
\end{equation*}

There are two sharply distinct sub-cases which must not
be confused:
\[
\text{(a) }f\equiv f', \quad \text{(b) } f \not\equiv f'.
\]

In either case, as we readily see, (11) is illusory, and there
is no ruled quartic determined by the common perpendiculars
to a line and its neighbours; these perpendiculars will either
all meet the given line at one of two mutually orthogonal
points, or two adjacent lines will be paratactic\index{Parataxy}, and have $\infty^1$
common perpendiculars.

\mysect{Isotropic congruences, and congruences of normals to surfaces of zero curvature}
Our condition for focal points expressed in (23) was independent
of (12), and this shows that our two sub-cases just
mentioned differ in this, that the first is a normal\index{Normals02@--- to surface} congruence,
while the second is not. Let $(x)$ be a point where our line
meets a set of perpendiculars, $(y)$ being thus the other such
point. Then under our first hypothesis, we shall have
\[
e=f = f'=g = 0.
\]

We see that the focal points will fall into $(x)$ and $(y)$ likewise.
These are mutually orthogonal, and so by equation (26) of the
last Chapter, that the total relative curvature of the surface
will be $-\dfrac{1}{k^2}$ or the Gaussian curvature zero. We see also by
theorem (22) of that chapter that it is possible to assemble the
lines of our congruence into families of left or right
paratactics\index{Left and right parataxy}\index{Right and left parataxy}
according as we assemble them by means of the one or the
other set of asymptotic lines of the given surface. Conversely,
%[File: 227.png]
if we have given a congruence of
normals\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}\index{Congruence10@--- of normals,
to surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero} to a surface of
Gaussian curvature zero\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero}, two
normals\index{Normals02@--- to surface} adjacent to a given one
are paratactic thereunto. There must be, then, two values of
$du: dv$ for which (11), looked upon as an equation in $r$, becomes
entirely illusory. Hence (24) must hold, and as we have
normal congruence (23) is also true.

We now make the second assumption
\[
f \not\equiv f'.
\]

We shall still take $(x)$ as a point where the line meets the
various common perpendiculars, so that we may put
\[
e = \frac{f+f'}{2} = g = 0.
\]

We may take as coordinates of a focal plane\index{Focal05@--- surfaces}
\[
u_i = \frac{\partial}{\partial t_i} |t\,xy\,dx|,
\]
\[
(uu) = k^2 [Edu^2 +2Fdu\,dv +Gdv^2].
\]

But by (20) this expression vanishes. Hence the focal
planes all touch the Absolute\index{Absolute}, and the focal surface must be
a developable circumscribed thereunto. It is clear that the
lines of such a congruence cannot be assembled into paratactic
families.

This type of congruence shall be called
`isotropic'\index{Congruence11@--- isotropic}\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence}.%
\footnote{The earliest discussion of these interesting congruences in non-euclidean
space will be found in the author's\index{Author} article `Les congruences isotropes qui
servent \`a repr\'esenter les fonctions d'une variable complexe', \emph{Atti della R.
Accademia delle Scienze di Torino}, xxxix, 1903, and xl, 1904. In the same
number of the same journal as the first of these will be found an article
by Bianchi\index{Bianchi}, `Sulla rappresentazione di Clifford delle congruenze rettilinee
nello spazio ellitico.' Professor Bianchi uses the word `isotropic' to cover
both what we have here defined as isotropic congruences, and also congruences
of normals to surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero, distinguishing the latter
by the name of `normal'. The author, on the other hand, included in his
definition of isotropic congruences those which, later, we shall define as
`pseudo-isotropic'. A discussion of these definitions will be found in a note
at the beginning of the second of the author's articles.}
%endfootnote

Let us take an isotropic congruence, or congruence of
normals to a surface of Gaussian curvature zero, and choose
$(x)$ and $(y)$ so that
\[
e = \tfrac{1}{2}(f+f')=g=0,
\]
\[
\overline{x_i} = x\cos\frac{r}{k} + y\sin\frac{r}{k},
\]
\[
(d\overline{x}\,d\overline{x}) =
  \cos^2\frac{r}{k}(dx\,dx) + \sin^2\frac{r}{k}(dy\,dy).
\]

%[File: 228.png]
This expression will be unaltered if we change $r$ into $-r$.
Conversely, when such is the case, we must have $(dx\,dy) = 0$,
and the congruence will be either
isotropic\index{Congruence11@--- isotropic}\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence}, or composed of
normals\index{Normals02@--- to surface}\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}\index{Normals02@--- to surface}
to a surface of Gaussian curvature
zero\index{Congruence10@--- of normals, to surfaces of Gaussian curvature zero}\index{Curvature08@--- surfaces of zero}.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~9. The necessary and sufficient condition that a
congruence should be either isotropic, or composed of normals
to a surface of Gaussian curvature zero, is that it should consist
of lines connecting corresponding points of two mutually
applicable surfaces, which pairs of points determine always
the same distance. The centres of gravity of these pairs of
points will be the points where the various lines meet the
common perpendiculars to themselves and the adjacent lines.

In elliptic (or spherical) space, there is advantage in studying our last
two types of congruence from a different point
of view, suggested by the developments of Chapter X.

Let us rewrite the equations (11) there given.
\begin{align*}
  (x_0 y_i - x_i y_0) + (x_j y_k - x_k y_j) &= {}_lX_i,   \\
  (x_0 y_i - x_i y_0) - (x_j y_k - x_k y_j) &= {}_rX_i.   \tag{25}
\end{align*}

These equations were originally written under the supposition that $(x)$ and $(y)$
were homogeneous. At present if we so
choose the unit of measure that $k = 1$ we have
\[
  ({}_lX\,{}_lX) = ({}_rX\,{}_rX) = 1.      \tag{26}
\]

\mysect{Spherical representation of rays in elliptic space}
These coordinates $({}_lX)$, $({}_rX)$ were formerly looked upon
as giving the lines through the origin $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ respectively
left and right paratactic to the given line. They may now be
looked upon as coordinates of two points of two unit spheres\index{Sphere}
of euclidean space, called, respectively, the left and right
\emph{representing spheres}\index{Spheres, representing}%
\footnote{This representation was first published independently by Study, `Zur
nichteuklidischen etc.,' and Fubini\index{Fubini}, `Il parallelismo di Clifford negli spazii
ellitici,' \emph{Annali della R. Scuola Normale di Pisa}, Vol.~ix, 1900. The latter writer
does not, however, distinguish with sufficient clearness between rays\index{Ray} and
lines.}
%endfootnote
The representation is not, however,
unique. On the one hand the two lines of a cross will be
represented by the same points, on the other, we get the same
line if we replace either representing point by its diametrical
opposite. We shall avoid ambiguity by assuming that each
line is doubly overlaid with two opposite `rays', meaning
thereby a line with a sense or sequence attached to its points,
as indicated in the beginning of Chapter V or end of Chapter
IX\@. We shall assume that by reversing the signs in one triad
of coordinates we replace our ray by a ray on the absolute
%[File: 229.png]
polar of its line, while by reversing both sets of signs, we
replace the ray\index{Ray} by its opposite.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~10. There is a perfect one to one correspondence
between the assemblage of all real rays of elliptic or spherical
space, and that of pairs of real points of two euclidean spheres.
Opposite rays of the same line will be represented by diametrically
opposite pairs of points, rays on mutually absolute
polar lines by identical points on one sphere\index{Sphere} and opposite
points of the other. Rays on left (right) paratactic lines will
be represented by identical or opposite points of the left (right)
sphere\index{Spheres, representing}.

Two rays shall be said to be paratactic when their lines are.
Reverting to Theorem 12 of Chapter X.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~11. The perpendicular distances of the lines of two
rays or the angles of these rays are half the difference and
half the sum of the pairs of spherical distances of their representing points.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~12. The necessary and sufficient condition that the
lines of two rays should intersect is that the spherical distances
of the pairs of representing points should be equal; each will
intersect the absolute polar of the other if these spherical
distances be supplementary.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~13. Each ray of a common perpendicular to the
lines of two rays will be represented by a pair of poles of two
great circles which connect the pairs of representing points.

It is clear that an analytic congruence may be represented
in the form
\begin{equation*}
_lX_i={}_lX_i(uv),\quad
_rX_i={}_rX_i(uv),
\end{equation*}

or else, in general,
\begin{equation*}
_lX_i={}_lX_i(_rX_1\,_rX_2\,_rX_3).
\end{equation*}

Two adjacent rays will intersect, or intersect one another's
polars if
\begin{equation*}
(d_lX\,d_lX)=(d_rX\,d_rX).
\end{equation*}

The common perpendicular to two adjacent rays will have
coordinates
\begin{equation*}
\lambda_lY_i=\frac{\partial}{\partial_lZ_i}\left|_lZ\,_lX\,d_lX\right|,\quad
\mu_rX_i=\frac{\partial}{\partial_rZ_i}\left|_rZ\,_rX\,d_rX\right|.
\end{equation*}

\mysect{Representation of normal congruence}
The condition that a congruence should be either normal or
pseudo-normal is
\begin{equation*}
(d_lXd_lX)=(d_rXd_rX),
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
(\delta_lX\delta_lX)=(\delta_rX\delta_rX),
\end{equation*}
%[File: 230.png]
\[
  \begin{vmatrix}
  ( _lX \,_lX)( _lX\, \delta \,_lX) \smallskip \\
  ( _lX \,d \,_lX)(d \,_lX \,\delta \,_lX) \end{vmatrix} = \pm
  \begin{vmatrix}
  ( _rX \,_rX)( _rX\, \delta \,_rX) \smallskip \\
  ( _rX \,d \,_rX)(d \,_rX \,\delta \,_rX) \end{vmatrix} ,
\]
from these
\begin{equation*}
\tag{27}
(d \,_l X\, \delta \,_lX) = \pm (d\,_rX\,\delta\,_rX).
\end{equation*}

Let us determine the significance of the double sign. If, in
particular, we take the congruence of
normals\index{Congruence09@--- of normals}\index{Normals02@--- to surface} to a sphere
whose centre is $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ we shall get the equations
\[
  _lX_i = {}_rX_i,
\]
and this transformation keeps areas invariant in value and
sign. On the other hand, the congruence of rays in the
absolute polar of this plane will be
\[
  {}_lX_i = - {}_rX_i,
\]
a transformation which changes the signs of all areas. Lastly,
we may pass from one normal congruence to another by a
continuous change, wherein the sign in equation (27) will not
be changed, hence%
\footnote{Cf.\ Study\index{Study}, loc.\ cit., p.~321; Fubini\index{Fubini}, p.~46.}
%endfootnote

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~14. A normal congruence
will be represented
by a relation between the two
spheres which keeps areas invariant
in actual value and
sign, and every such relation
will give a normal congruence.}
{\emph{Theorem}~14$'$. A pseudo-normal\index{Pseudo-normal congruence}
congruence will be
represented by a relation between
the two spheres where
the sum of corresponding areas
on the two is zero, and every
such relation will give a
pseudo-normal congruence.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

\mysect{Isotropic congruence represented by an arbitrary function of the complex variable}
Let us next take an isotropic congruence. Here two
common perpendiculars to two adjacent lines necessarily
intersect, or each intersects the absolute polar of the other.
The same will hold for the absolute polar of an isotropic
congruence, a `pseudo-isotropic' congruence\index{Pseudo-isotropic congruence}, let us say. Such
a congruence will not have a focal surface at all, but a focal
curve, which lies on the Absolute. On the representing
spheres, in the case of either of these congruences, two intersecting
arcs of one will make the same angle, in absolute
value, as the corresponding arcs on the other. In the particular
case of the isotropic congruence of all lines through
the point $(1, 0, 0, 0)$ the relation between the two representing
spheres is a directly conformal one, while in the case of the
pseudo-isotropic congruence of all lines in the plane $(1, 0, 0, 0)$
we have an inversely conformal relation. We may now repeat
%[File: 231.png]
the reasoning by continuity used in the case of the normal
congruence, and get%
\footnote{First given in the Author's\index{Author} first article on isotropic congruences, recently cited.}
%endfootnote

\medskip\twincol{\emph{Theorem}~15. The necessary and sufficient condition that a congruence
should be isotropic\index{Congruence11@--- isotropic}\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence}
is that the corresponding relation between the representing
spheres should be directly conformal.}
{\emph{Theorem}~15$'$. The necessary and sufficient condition that a congruence should be
pseudo-isotropic\index{Pseudo-isotropic congruence}
is that the corresponding relation between the representing spheres
should be inversely conformal.}\par\medskip
%end twincol

Let us take up the isotropic case more fully. Any directly
conformal relation between the real domains of two euclidean
spheres of radius unity may be represented by an analytic
function of the complex variable. Let us give the coordinates
of points of our representing spheres in the following parametric form:
\begin{align*}
_lX_1&=\frac{u_1u_2-1}{u_1u_2+1},\quad &
_rX_1&=\frac{z_1z_2-1}{z_1z_2+1},\\
_lX_2&=\frac{i(u_1-u_2)}{u_1u_2+1},\quad &
_rX_2&=\frac{i(z_1-z_2)}{z_1z_2+1}, & \tag{28} \\
_lX_3&=\frac{u_1+u_2}{u_1u_2+1},\quad &
_rX_3&=\frac{z_1+z_2}{z_1z_2+1}.
\end{align*}

We shall get a real ray when
\begin{equation*}
u_2=\bar u_1,\quad z_2=\bar z_1.
\end{equation*}

In order to have a real directly conformal relation between
the two spheres, our transformation must be such as to carry
a rectilinear generator into another generator, i.e.
\begin{equation*}
u_1=u_1(z_1),\quad u_2=\bar u_1(z_2).\tag{29}
\end{equation*}

For an inversely conformal transformation
\begin{equation*}
u_1=u_1(z_2),\quad u_2=\bar u_1(z_1).\tag{30}
\end{equation*}

All will thus depend on the single analytic function $u_1(z)$.

The opposite of the ray $(u)$ $(z)$ will be
\begin{align*}
u_1{}'&=-\frac{1}{u_2}, \qquad z_1{}' =-\frac{1}{z_2}, \\
u_2{}'&=-\frac{1}{u_1}, \qquad z_2{}' =-\frac{1}{z_1}.
\end{align*}

%[File: 232.png]
Let us now inquire under what circumstances the following equation will hold:
\[
  \bar{u}_1\Bigl(-\frac{1}{z_1}\Bigr) = \frac{-1}{u_1(z_1)}. \tag{31}
\]

If this hold identically, the opposite of every ray of the
congruence will belong thereto. If not, there will still be
certain rays of the congruence for which it is true. To begin
with it will be satisfied by all rays of the congruence for
which
\[
  u_1 u_2 + 1 = 0, \quad z_1 z_2 + 1 = 0.
\]

This amounts to putting
\[
  (_lX\,_lX) = (_rX\,_rX) = 0.
\]

We saw in Chapter X that, interpreted in cross\index{Cross} coordinates,
these are the equations which characterize an improper cross\index{Improper01@Improper cross}
of the second sort, which is made up of a pencil of tangents
to the Absolute\index{Absolute}. Such a pencil we may also call an improper
ray\index{Improper02@--- ray} of the second sort. Let us see under what circumstances
such a ray $(uz)$ will intersect a proper ray $(u'z')$ orthogonally.
Geometrically, we see that either the proper ray must pass
through the vertex of the pencil, or lie in the plane thereof,
and analytically we shall have
\[
  (u_1 - u_1{}')(u_2 - u_2{}') = (z_1 - z_1{}')(z_2 - z_2{}') = 0,
\]
\[
  u_1 u_2 + 1 = z_1 z_2 + 1 = 0.
\]

There are four solutions to these equations. By considering
a special case we are able to pick out those two where the ray
lies in the plane of the pencil\label{z1prime}
\[
\begin{aligned}
  u_1 &= u_1{}', \qquad &  z_1 &= z_1{}',   \\
  u_2 &= -\frac{1}{u_1{}'}, \qquad &  z_2 &= -\frac{1}{z_1{}'},
\end{aligned}
\]
or else
\[
\begin{aligned}
  u_1 &= -\frac{1}{u_2{}'}, \qquad &  z_1 &= -\frac{1}{z_2{}'}, \\
  u_2 &= u_2{}', \qquad &  z_2 &= z_2{}',
\end{aligned}
\]

The proper ray $(u') (z')$ was supposed to belong to our
congruence. The condition that the improper one $(u) (z)$ shall
also belong thereto will be
\[
  \bar{u}_1\Bigl(-\frac{1}{z_1{}'}\Bigr) = -\frac{1}{u_1(z_1{}')}.
\]

%[File: 233.png]
\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~16.%
\footnote{See the Author's\index{Author} second note on isotropic congruences, p.~13.}
%endfootnote
The necessary and sufficient condition that
the opposite of a real ray of an isotropic congruence should
also belong thereunto is that the ray should be coplanar with
an improper ray\index{Improper02@--- ray} of the second sort belonging to the congruence.
When the latter are present in infinite number in an
irreducible congruence, the congruence contains the opposite
of each of its rays.

The two cases here given may be still more sharply distinguished
by geometrical considerations. The focal surface\index{Focal05@--- surfaces}
of an isotropic congruence\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence}\index{Congruence11@--- isotropic}
is a developable circumscribed to
the Absolute\index{Absolute}, and will have a real equation when the congruence
is real. There are two distinct possibilities; first, the
equation of this surface is reducible in the rational domain;
second, it is not. In the first case the surface is made up of
two conjugate imaginary portions; in the second there is one
portion which is its own conjugate imaginary. In the first
case there will be a finite number of planes which touch the
Absolute and also each of the two portions of the focal surface
at the same point, namely, those which touch the Absolute
at the points of intersection of the two curves of contact with
the two portions of the focal surface. In these planes only
shall we have improper rays of the second sort belonging to
the congruence. If, on the other hand, the focal surface be
irreducible, every point of the curve of contact may be looked
upon as being in the intersection of two adjacent planes
tangent to the Absolute, and the focal surface which is its
own conjugate imaginary. The tangents at each of these
points will be improper rays of the second sort of the congruence.
Theorem 17 may now be given in a better form.

\smallskip\emph{Theorem}~17. The necessary and sufficient condition that
an isotropic congruence should contain the opposite of each
of its rays is that the focal surface should be irreducible.

\mysect{Special examples of this representation}
It is very easy to observe the distinction between the two
cases in the case of the linear function
\begin{equation*}
u_1=\frac{\alpha z_1+\beta}{\gamma z_1+\delta}.
\end{equation*}

If $\beta=-\bar\gamma$, $\delta=\bar\alpha$, (29) is identically satisfied. But here
it will be seen that if we write
\begin{equation*}
\alpha=a+bi,\quad \gamma=-c+di,
\end{equation*}
our congruence is nought else than the assemblage of all rays
through the point $(a, b, c, d)$. The focal surface is the cone of
%[File: 234.png]
tangents thence to the Absolute\index{Isotropic02@--- congruence}, clearly its own conjugate
imaginary. On the other hand, when $\alpha$, $\beta$, $\gamma$, $\delta$ are not connected
by these relations, we shall have a line congruence of
the fourth order, and second class, as is easily verified. It is
well known%
\footnote{Cf.\ Sturm\index{Sturm}, \emph{Gebilde erster und zweiter Ordnung der Liniengeometrie},
Leipzig, 1892-96, Vol.~ii, p.~320.}
%endfootnote
that a congruence of the second order and fourth
class has no focal surface, but a focal curve composed of two
conics, so our present congruence has as focal surface two
conjugate imaginary quadric cones which are circumscribed
to the Absolute. When their conjugate imaginary centres fall
together in a real point, we revert to the previous case.

When $(u)$ and $(z)$ are connected by the vanishing of a
polynomial of order $m$ in $u_1$ and order $n$ in $z_1$, in the general
case where (31) does not hold identically, we shall have
a line-congruence of order $(m+n)^2$. When, however, (31) does
hold, we must subtract from this the order of the curve of
contact of the focal surface and Absolute, and then divide by
2 to allow for the fact that there are two opposite rays on
each line.
